__label__di “Your bundle has some importance, however,” continued the clerk, when they had laughed their fill (it was observable that the subject of their mirth joined in the laughter when he saw them laughing); “for though I dare say it is not stuffed full of friedrichs d’or and louis d’or--judge from your costume and gaiters--still--if you can add to your possessions such a valuable property as a relation like Mrs. General Epanchin, then your bundle becomes a significant object at once.
__label__di That is, of course, if you really are a relative of Mrs. Epanchin’s, and have not made a little error through--well, absence of mind, which is very common to human beings; or, say--through a too luxuriant fancy?”  “Oh, you are right again,” said the fair-haired traveller, “for I really am _almost_ wrong when I say she and I are related.
__label__di She is hardly a relation at all; so little, in fact, that I was not in the least surprised to have no answer to my letter.
__label__di I expected as much.”  “H’m! you spent your postage for nothing, then.
__label__di H’m! you are candid, however--and that is commendable.
__label__di H’m! Mrs. Epanchin--oh yes! a most eminent person.
__label__di I know her.
__label__di As for Mr. Pavlicheff, who supported you in Switzerland, I know him too--at least, if it was Nicolai Andreevitch of that name? A fine fellow he was--and had a property of four thousand souls in his day.”  “Yes, Nicolai Andreevitch--that was his name,” and the young fellow looked earnestly and with curiosity at the all-knowing gentleman with the red nose.
__label__di This sort of character is met with pretty frequently in a certain class.
__label__di They are people who know everyone--that is, they know where a man is employed, what his salary is, whom he knows, whom he married, what money his wife had, who are his cousins, and second cousins, etc., etc.
__label__di These men generally have about a hundred pounds a year to live on, and they spend their whole time and talents in the amassing of this style of knowledge, which they reduce--or raise--to the standard of a science.
__label__di During the latter part of the conversation the black-haired young man had become very impatient.
__label__di He stared out of the window, and fidgeted, and evidently longed for the end of the journey.
__label__di He was very absent; he would appear to listen--and heard nothing; and he would laugh of a sudden, evidently with no idea of what he was laughing about.
__label__di “Excuse me,” said the red-nosed man to the young fellow with the bundle, rather suddenly; “whom have I the honour to be talking to?”  “Prince Lef Nicolaievitch Muishkin,” replied the latter, with perfect readiness.
__label__di “Prince Muishkin? Lef Nicolaievitch? H’m! I don’t know, I’m sure! I may say I have never heard of such a person,” said the clerk, thoughtfully.
__label__di “At least, the name, I admit, is historical.
__label__di Karamsin must mention the family name, of course, in his history--but as an individual--one never hears of any Prince Muishkin nowadays.”  “Of course not,” replied the prince; “there are none, except myself.
__label__di I believe I am the last and only one.
__label__di As to my forefathers, they have always been a poor lot; my own father was a sublieutenant in the army.
__label__di I don’t know how Mrs. Epanchin comes into the Muishkin family, but she is descended from the Princess Muishkin, and she, too, is the last of her line.”  “And did you learn science and all that, with your professor over there?” asked the black-haired passenger.
__label__di “Oh yes--I did learn a little, but--”  “I’ve never learned anything whatever,” said the other.
__label__di “Oh, but I learned very little, you know!” added the prince, as though excusing himself.
__label__di “They could not teach me very much on account of my illness.”  “Do you know the Rogojins?” asked his questioner, abruptly.
__label__di “No, I don’t--not at all! I hardly know anyone in Russia.
__label__di Why, is that your name?”  “Yes, I am Rogojin, Parfen Rogojin.”  “Parfen Rogojin? dear me--then don’t you belong to those very Rogojins, perhaps--” began the clerk, with a very perceptible increase of civility in his tone.
__label__di “Yes--those very ones,” interrupted Rogojin, impatiently, and with scant courtesy.
__label__di I may remark that he had not once taken any notice of the blotchy-faced passenger, and had hitherto addressed all his remarks direct to the prince.
__label__di “Dear me--is it possible?” observed the clerk, while his face assumed an expression of great deference and servility--if not of absolute alarm: “what, a son of that very Semen Rogojin--hereditary honourable citizen--who died a month or so ago and left two million and a half of roubles?”  “And how do _you_ know that he left two million and a half of roubles?”  asked Rogojin, disdainfully, and not deigning so much as to look at the other.
__label__di “However, it’s true enough that my father died a month ago, and that here am I returning from Pskoff, a month after, with hardly a boot to my foot.
__label__di They’ve treated me like a dog! I’ve been ill of fever at Pskoff the whole time, and not a line, nor farthing of money, have I received from my mother or my confounded brother!”  “And now you’ll have a million roubles, at least--goodness gracious me!”  exclaimed the clerk, rubbing his hands.
__label__di “Five weeks since, I was just like yourself,” continued Rogojin, addressing the prince, “with nothing but a bundle and the clothes I wore.
__label__di I ran away from my father and came to Pskoff to my aunt’s house, where I caved in at once with fever, and he went and died while I was away.
__label__di All honour to my respected father’s memory--but he uncommonly nearly killed me, all the same.
__label__di Give you my word, prince, if I hadn’t cut and run then, when I did, he’d have murdered me like a dog.”  “I suppose you angered him somehow?” asked the prince, looking at the millionaire with considerable curiosity.
__label__di But though there may have been something remarkable in the fact that this man was heir to millions of roubles there was something about him which surprised and interested the prince more than that.
__label__di Rogojin, too, seemed to have taken up the conversation with unusual alacrity it appeared that he was still in a considerable state of excitement, if not absolutely feverish, and was in real need of someone to talk to for the mere sake of talking, as safety-valve to his agitation.
__label__di As for his red-nosed neighbour, the latter--since the information as to the identity of Rogojin--hung over him, seemed to be living on the honey of his words and in the breath of his nostrils, catching at every syllable as though it were a pearl of great price.
__label__di “Oh, yes; I angered him--I certainly did anger him,” replied Rogojin.
__label__di “But what puts me out so is my brother.
__label__di Of course my mother couldn’t do anything--she’s too old--and whatever brother Senka says is law for her! But why couldn’t he let me know? He sent a telegram, they say.
__label__di What’s the good of a telegram? It frightened my aunt so that she sent it back to the office unopened, and there it’s been ever since! It’s only thanks to Konief that I heard at all; he wrote me all about it.
__label__di He says my brother cut off the gold tassels from my father’s coffin, at night ‘because they’re worth a lot of money!’ says he.
__label__di Why, I can get him sent off to Siberia for that alone, if I like; it’s sacrilege.
__label__di Here, you--scarecrow!” he added, addressing the clerk at his side, “is it sacrilege or not, by law?”  “Sacrilege, certainly--certainly sacrilege,” said the latter.
__label__di “And it’s Siberia for sacrilege, isn’t it?”  “Undoubtedly so; Siberia, of course!”  “They will think that I’m still ill,” continued Rogojin to the prince, “but I sloped off quietly, seedy as I was, took the train and came away.
__label__di Aha, brother Senka, you’ll have to open your gates and let me in, my boy! I know he told tales about me to my father--I know that well enough but I certainly did rile my father about Nastasia Philipovna that’s very sure, and that was my own doing.”  “Nastasia Philipovna?” said the clerk, as though trying to think out something.
__label__di “Come, you know nothing about _her_,” said Rogojin, impatiently.
__label__di “And supposing I do know something?” observed the other, triumphantly.
__label__di “Bosh! there are plenty of Nastasia Philipovnas.
__label__di And what an impertinent beast you are!” he added angrily.
__label__di “I thought some creature like you would hang on to me as soon as I got hold of my money.”  “Oh, but I do know, as it happens,” said the clerk in an aggravating manner.
__label__di “Lebedeff knows all about her.
__label__di You are pleased to reproach me, your excellency, but what if I prove that I am right after all? Nastasia Phillpovna’s family name is Barashkoff--I know, you see--and she is a very well known lady, indeed, and comes of a good family, too.
__label__di She is connected with one Totski, Afanasy Ivanovitch, a man of considerable property, a director of companies, and so on, and a great friend of General Epanchin, who is interested in the same matters as he is.”  “My eyes!” said Rogojin, really surprised at last.
__label__di “The devil take the fellow, how does he know that?”  “Why, he knows everything--Lebedeff knows everything! I was a month or two with Lihachof after his father died, your excellency, and while he was knocking about--he’s in the debtor’s prison now--I was with him, and he couldn’t do a thing without Lebedeff; and I got to know Nastasia Philipovna and several people at that time.”  “Nastasia Philipovna? Why, you don’t mean to say that she and Lihachof--” cried Rogojin, turning quite pale.
__label__di “No, no, no, no, no! Nothing of the sort, I assure you!” said Lebedeff, hastily.
__label__di “Oh dear no, not for the world! Totski’s the only man with any chance there.
__label__di Oh, no! He takes her to his box at the opera at the French theatre of an evening, and the officers and people all look at her and say, ‘By Jove, there’s the famous Nastasia Philipovna!’ but no one ever gets any further than that, for there is nothing more to say.”  “Yes, it’s quite true,” said Rogojin, frowning gloomily; “so Zaleshoff told me.
__label__di I was walking about the Nefsky one fine day, prince, in my father’s old coat, when she suddenly came out of a shop and stepped into her carriage.
__label__di I swear I was all of a blaze at once.
__label__di Then I met Zaleshoff--looking like a hair-dresser’s assistant, got up as fine as I don’t know who, while I looked like a tinker.
__label__di ‘Don’t flatter yourself, my boy,’ said he; ‘she’s not for such as you; she’s a princess, she is, and her name is Nastasia Philipovna Barashkoff, and she lives with Totski, who wishes to get rid of her because he’s growing rather old--fifty-five or so--and wants to marry a certain beauty, the loveliest woman in all Petersburg.’ And then he told me that I could see Nastasia Philipovna at the opera-house that evening, if I liked, and described which was her box.
__label__di Well, I’d like to see my father allowing any of us to go to the theatre; he’d sooner have killed us, any day.
__label__di However, I went for an hour or so and saw Nastasia Philipovna, and I never slept a wink all night after.
__label__di Next morning my father happened to give me two government loan bonds to sell, worth nearly five thousand roubles each.
__label__di ‘Sell them,’ said he, ‘and then take seven thousand five hundred roubles to the office, give them to the cashier, and bring me back the rest of the ten thousand, without looking in anywhere on the way; look sharp, I shall be waiting for you.’ Well, I sold the bonds, but I didn’t take the seven thousand roubles to the office; I went straight to the English shop and chose a pair of earrings, with a diamond the size of a nut in each.
__label__di They cost four hundred roubles more than I had, so I gave my name, and they trusted me.
__label__di With the earrings I went at once to Zaleshoff’s.
__label__di ‘Come on!’ I said, ‘come on to Nastasia Philipovna’s,’ and off we went without more ado.
__label__di I tell you I hadn’t a notion of what was about me or before me or below my feet all the way; I saw nothing whatever.
__label__di We went straight into her drawing-room, and then she came out to us.
__label__di “I didn’t say right out who I was, but Zaleshoff said: ‘From Parfen Rogojin, in memory of his first meeting with you yesterday; be so kind as to accept these!’  “She opened the parcel, looked at the earrings, and laughed.
__label__di “‘Thank your friend Mr. Rogojin for his kind attention,’ says she, and bowed and went off.
__label__di Why didn’t I die there on the spot? The worst of it all was, though, that the beast Zaleshoff got all the credit of it! I was short and abominably dressed, and stood and stared in her face and never said a word, because I was shy, like an ass! And there was he all in the fashion, pomaded and dressed out, with a smart tie on, bowing and scraping; and I bet anything she took him for me all the while!  “‘Look here now,’ I said, when we came out, ‘none of your interference here after this--do you understand?’ He laughed: ‘And how are you going to settle up with your father?’ says he.
__label__di I thought I might as well jump into the Neva at once without going home first; but it struck me that I wouldn’t, after all, and I went home feeling like one of the damned.”  “My goodness!” shivered the clerk.
__label__di “And his father,” he added, for the prince’s instruction, “and his father would have given a man a ticket to the other world for ten roubles any day--not to speak of ten thousand!”  The prince observed Rogojin with great curiosity; he seemed paler than ever at this moment.
__label__di “What do you know about it?” cried the latter.
__label__di “Well, my father learned the whole story at once, and Zaleshoff blabbed it all over the town besides.
__label__di So he took me upstairs and locked me up, and swore at me for an hour.
__label__di ‘This is only a foretaste,’ says he; ‘wait a bit till night comes, and I’ll come back and talk to you again.’  “Well, what do you think? The old fellow went straight off to Nastasia Philipovna, touched the floor with his forehead, and began blubbering and beseeching her on his knees to give him back the diamonds.
__label__di So after awhile she brought the box and flew out at him.
__label__di ‘There,’ she says, ‘take your earrings, you wretched old miser; although they are ten times dearer than their value to me now that I know what it must have cost Parfen to get them! Give Parfen my compliments,’ she says, ‘and thank him very much!’ Well, I meanwhile had borrowed twenty-five roubles from a friend, and off I went to Pskoff to my aunt’s.
__label__di The old woman there lectured me so that I left the house and went on a drinking tour round the public-houses of the place.
__label__di I was in a high fever when I got to Pskoff, and by nightfall I was lying delirious in the streets somewhere or other!”  “Oho! we’ll make Nastasia Philipovna sing another song now!” giggled Lebedeff, rubbing his hands with glee.
__label__di “Hey, my boy, we’ll get her some proper earrings now! We’ll get her such earrings that--”  “Look here,” cried Rogojin, seizing him fiercely by the arm, “look here, if you so much as name Nastasia Philipovna again, I’ll tan your hide as sure as you sit there!”  “Aha! do--by all means! if you tan my hide you won’t turn me away from your society.
__label__di You’ll bind me to you, with your lash, for ever.
__label__di Ha, ha! here we are at the station, though.”  Sure enough, the train was just steaming in as he spoke.
__label__di Though Rogojin had declared that he left Pskoff secretly, a large collection of friends had assembled to greet him, and did so with profuse waving of hats and shouting.
__label__di “Why, there’s Zaleshoff here, too!” he muttered, gazing at the scene with a sort of triumphant but unpleasant smile.
__label__di Then he suddenly turned to the prince: “Prince, I don’t know why I have taken a fancy to you; perhaps because I met you just when I did.
__label__di But no, it can’t be that, for I met this fellow” (nodding at Lebedeff) “too, and I have not taken a fancy to him by any means.
__label__di Come to see me, prince; we’ll take off those gaiters of yours and dress you up in a smart fur coat, the best we can buy.
__label__di You shall have a dress coat, best quality, white waistcoat, anything you like, and your pocket shall be full of money.
__label__di Come, and you shall go with me to Nastasia Philipovna’s.
__label__di Now then will you come or no?”  “Accept, accept, Prince Lef Nicolaievitch” said Lebedef solemnly; “don’t let it slip! Accept, quick!”  Prince Muishkin rose and stretched out his hand courteously, while he replied with some cordiality:  “I will come with the greatest pleasure, and thank you very much for taking a fancy to me.
__label__di I dare say I may even come today if I have time, for I tell you frankly that I like you very much too.
__label__di I liked you especially when you told us about the diamond earrings; but I liked you before that as well, though you have such a dark-clouded sort of face.
__label__di Thanks very much for the offer of clothes and a fur coat; I certainly shall require both clothes and coat very soon.
__label__di As for money, I have hardly a copeck about me at this moment.”  “You shall have lots of money; by the evening I shall have plenty; so come along!”  “That’s true enough, he’ll have lots before evening!” put in Lebedeff.
__label__di “But, look here, are you a great hand with the ladies? Let’s know that first?” asked Rogojin.
__label__di “Oh no, oh no!” said the prince; “I couldn’t, you know--my illness--I hardly ever saw a soul.”  “H’m! well--here, you fellow--you can come along with me now if you like!” cried Rogojin to Lebedeff, and so they all left the carriage.
__label__di Lebedeff had his desire.
__label__di He went off with the noisy group of Rogojin’s friends towards the Voznesensky, while the prince’s route lay towards the Litaynaya.
__label__di It was damp and wet.
__label__di The prince asked his way of passers-by, and finding that he was a couple of miles or so from his destination, he determined to take a droshky.
__label__di II.
__label__di General Epanchin lived in his own house near the Litaynaya.
__label__di Besides this large residence--five-sixths of which was let in flats and lodgings--the general was owner of another enormous house in the Sadovaya bringing in even more rent than the first.
__label__di Besides these houses he had a delightful little estate just out of town, and some sort of factory in another part of the city.
__label__di General Epanchin, as everyone knew, had a good deal to do with certain government monopolies; he was also a voice, and an important one, in many rich public companies of various descriptions; in fact, he enjoyed the reputation of being a well-to-do man of busy habits, many ties, and affluent means.
__label__di He had made himself indispensable in several quarters, amongst others in his department of the government; and yet it was a known fact that Fedor Ivanovitch Epanchin was a man of no education whatever, and had absolutely risen from the ranks.
__label__di This last fact could, of course, reflect nothing but credit upon the general; and yet, though unquestionably a sagacious man, he had his own little weaknesses--very excusable ones,--one of which was a dislike to any allusion to the above circumstance.
__label__di He was undoubtedly clever.
__label__di For instance, he made a point of never asserting himself when he would gain more by keeping in the background; and in consequence many exalted personages valued him principally for his humility and simplicity, and because “he knew his place.” And yet if these good people could only have had a peep into the mind of this excellent fellow who “knew his place” so well! The fact is that, in spite of his knowledge of the world and his really remarkable abilities, he always liked to appear to be carrying out other people’s ideas rather than his own.
__label__di And also, his luck seldom failed him, even at cards, for which he had a passion that he did not attempt to conceal.
__label__di He played for high stakes, and moved, altogether, in very varied society.
__label__di As to age, General Epanchin was in the very prime of life; that is, about fifty-five years of age,--the flowering time of existence, when real enjoyment of life begins.
__label__di His healthy appearance, good colour, sound, though discoloured teeth, sturdy figure, preoccupied air during business hours, and jolly good humour during his game at cards in the evening, all bore witness to his success in life, and combined to make existence a bed of roses to his excellency.
__label__di The general was lord of a flourishing family, consisting of his wife and three grown-up daughters.
__label__di He had married young, while still a lieutenant, his wife being a girl of about his own age, who possessed neither beauty nor education, and who brought him no more than fifty souls of landed property, which little estate served, however, as a nest-egg for far more important accumulations.
__label__di The general never regretted his early marriage, or regarded it as a foolish youthful escapade; and he so respected and feared his wife that he was very near loving her.
__label__di Mrs. Epanchin came of the princely stock of Muishkin, which if not a brilliant, was, at all events, a decidedly ancient family; and she was extremely proud of her descent.
__label__di With a few exceptions, the worthy couple had lived through their long union very happily.
__label__di While still young the wife had been able to make important friends among the aristocracy, partly by virtue of her family descent, and partly by her own exertions; while, in after life, thanks to their wealth and to the position of her husband in the service, she took her place among the higher circles as by right.
__label__di During these last few years all three of the general’s daughters--Alexandra, Adelaida, and Aglaya--had grown up and matured.
__label__di Of course they were only Epanchins, but their mother’s family was noble; they might expect considerable fortunes; their father had hopes of attaining to very high rank indeed in his country’s service--all of which was satisfactory.
__label__di All three of the girls were decidedly pretty, even the eldest, Alexandra, who was just twenty-five years old.
__label__di The middle daughter was now twenty-three, while the youngest, Aglaya, was twenty.
__label__di This youngest girl was absolutely a beauty, and had begun of late to attract considerable attention in society.
__label__di But this was not all, for every one of the three was clever, well educated, and accomplished.
__label__di It was a matter of general knowledge that the three girls were very fond of one another, and supported each other in every way; it was even said that the two elder ones had made certain sacrifices for the sake of the idol of the household, Aglaya.
__label__di In society they not only disliked asserting themselves, but were actually retiring.
__label__di Certainly no one could blame them for being too arrogant or haughty, and yet everybody was well aware that they were proud and quite understood their own value.
__label__di The eldest was musical, while the second was a clever artist, which fact she had concealed until lately.
__label__di In a word, the world spoke well of the girls; but they were not without their enemies, and occasionally people talked with horror of the number of books they had read.
__label__di They were in no hurry to marry.
__label__di They liked good society, but were not too keen about it.
__label__di All this was the more remarkable, because everyone was well aware of the hopes and aims of their parents.
__label__di It was about eleven o’clock in the forenoon when the prince rang the bell at General Epanchin’s door.
__label__di The general lived on the first floor or flat of the house, as modest a lodging as his position permitted.
__label__di A liveried servant opened the door, and the prince was obliged to enter into long explanations with this gentleman, who, from the first glance, looked at him and his bundle with grave suspicion.
__label__di At last, however, on the repeated positive assurance that he really was Prince Muishkin, and must absolutely see the general on business, the bewildered domestic showed him into a little ante-chamber leading to a waiting-room that adjoined the general’s study, there handing him over to another servant, whose duty it was to be in this ante-chamber all the morning, and announce visitors to the general.
__label__di This second individual wore a dress coat, and was some forty years of age; he was the general’s special study servant, and well aware of his own importance.
__label__di “Wait in the next room, please; and leave your bundle here,” said the door-keeper, as he sat down comfortably in his own easy-chair in the ante-chamber.
__label__di He looked at the prince in severe surprise as the latter settled himself in another chair alongside, with his bundle on his knees.
__label__di “If you don’t mind, I would rather sit here with you,” said the prince; “I should prefer it to sitting in there.”  “Oh, but you can’t stay here.
__label__di You are a visitor--a guest, so to speak.
__label__di Is it the general himself you wish to see?”  The man evidently could not take in the idea of such a shabby-looking visitor, and had decided to ask once more.
__label__di “Yes--I have business--” began the prince.
__label__di “I do not ask you what your business may be, all I have to do is to announce you; and unless the secretary comes in here I cannot do that.”  The man’s suspicions seemed to increase more and more.
__label__di The prince was too unlike the usual run of daily visitors; and although the general certainly did receive, on business, all sorts and conditions of men, yet in spite of this fact the servant felt great doubts on the subject of this particular visitor.
__label__di The presence of the secretary as an intermediary was, he judged, essential in this case.
__label__di “Surely you--are from abroad?” he inquired at last, in a confused sort of way.
__label__di He had begun his sentence intending to say, “Surely you are not Prince Muishkin, are you?”  “Yes, straight from the train! Did not you intend to say, ‘Surely you are not Prince Muishkin?’ just now, but refrained out of politeness?”  “H’m!” grunted the astonished servant.
__label__di “I assure you I am not deceiving you; you shall not have to answer for me.
__label__di As to my being dressed like this, and carrying a bundle, there’s nothing surprising in that--the fact is, my circumstances are not particularly rosy at this moment.”  “H’m!--no, I’m not afraid of that, you see; I have to announce you, that’s all.
__label__di The secretary will be out directly--that is, unless you--yes, that’s the rub--unless you--come, you must allow me to ask you--you’ve not come to beg, have you?”  “Oh dear no, you can be perfectly easy on that score.
__label__di I have quite another matter on hand.”  “You must excuse my asking, you know.
__label__di Your appearance led me to think--but just wait for the secretary; the general is busy now, but the secretary is sure to come out.”  “Oh--well, look here, if I have some time to wait, would you mind telling me, is there any place about where I could have a smoke? I have my pipe and tobacco with me.”  “_Smoke?_” said the man, in shocked but disdainful surprise, blinking his eyes at the prince as though he could not believe his senses.
__label__di “No, sir, you cannot smoke here, and I wonder you are not ashamed of the very suggestion.
__label__di Ha, ha! a cool idea that, I declare!”  “Oh, I didn’t mean in this room! I know I can’t smoke here, of course.
__label__di I’d adjourn to some other room, wherever you like to show me to.
__label__di You see, I’m used to smoking a good deal, and now I haven’t had a puff for three hours; however, just as you like.”  “Now how on earth am I to announce a man like that?” muttered the servant.
__label__di “In the first place, you’ve no right in here at all; you ought to be in the waiting-room, because you’re a sort of visitor--a guest, in fact--and I shall catch it for this.
__label__di Look here, do you intend to take up you abode with us?” he added, glancing once more at the prince’s bundle, which evidently gave him no peace.
__label__di “No, I don’t think so.
__label__di I don’t think I should stay even if they were to invite me.
__label__di I’ve simply come to make their acquaintance, and nothing more.”  “Make their acquaintance?” asked the man, in amazement, and with redoubled suspicion.
__label__di “Then why did you say you had business with the general?”  “Oh well, very little business.
__label__di There is one little matter--some advice I am going to ask him for; but my principal object is simply to introduce myself, because I am Prince Muishkin, and Madame Epanchin is the last of her branch of the house, and besides herself and me there are no other Muishkins left.”  “What--you’re a relation then, are you?” asked the servant, so bewildered that he began to feel quite alarmed.
__label__di “Well, hardly so.
__label__di If you stretch a point, we are relations, of course, but so distant that one cannot really take cognizance of it.
__label__di I once wrote to your mistress from abroad, but she did not reply.
__label__di However, I have thought it right to make acquaintance with her on my arrival.
__label__di I am telling you all this in order to ease your mind, for I see you are still far from comfortable on my account.
__label__di All you have to do is to announce me as Prince Muishkin, and the object of my visit will be plain enough.
__label__di If I am received--very good; if not, well, very good again.
__label__di But they are sure to receive me, I should think; Madame Epanchin will naturally be curious to see the only remaining representative of her family.
__label__di She values her Muishkin descent very highly, if I am rightly informed.”  The prince’s conversation was artless and confiding to a degree, and the servant could not help feeling that as from visitor to common serving-man this state of things was highly improper.
__label__di His conclusion was that one of two things must be the explanation--either that this was a begging impostor, or that the prince, if prince he were, was simply a fool, without the slightest ambition; for a sensible prince with any ambition would certainly not wait about in ante-rooms with servants, and talk of his own private affairs like this.
__label__di In either case, how was he to announce this singular visitor?  “I really think I must request you to step into the next room!” he said, with all the insistence he could muster.
__label__di “Why? If I had been sitting there now, I should not have had the opportunity of making these personal explanations.
__label__di I see you are still uneasy about me and keep eyeing my cloak and bundle.
__label__di Don’t you think you might go in yourself now, without waiting for the secretary to come out?”  “No, no! I can’t announce a visitor like yourself without the secretary.
__label__di Besides the general said he was not to be disturbed--he is with the Colonel C--.
__label__di Gavrila Ardalionovitch goes in without announcing.”  “Who may that be? a clerk?”  “What? Gavrila Ardalionovitch? Oh no; he belongs to one of the companies.
__label__di Look here, at all events put your bundle down, here.”  “Yes, I will if I may; and--can I take off my cloak”  “Of course; you can’t go in _there_ with it on, anyhow.”  The prince rose and took off his mantle, revealing a neat enough morning costume--a little worn, but well made.
__label__di He wore a steel watch chain and from this chain there hung a silver Geneva watch.
__label__di Fool the prince might be, still, the general’s servant felt that it was not correct for him to continue to converse thus with a visitor, in spite of the fact that the prince pleased him somehow.
__label__di “And what time of day does the lady receive?” the latter asked, reseating himself in his old place.
__label__di “Oh, that’s not in _my_ province! I believe she receives at any time; it depends upon the visitors.
__label__di The dressmaker goes in at eleven.
__label__di Gavrila Ardalionovitch is allowed much earlier than other people, too; he is even admitted to early lunch now and then.”  “It is much warmer in the rooms here than it is abroad at this season,”  observed the prince; “but it is much warmer there out of doors.
__label__di As for the houses--a Russian can’t live in them in the winter until he gets accustomed to them.”  “Don’t they heat them at all?”  “Well, they do heat them a little; but the houses and stoves are so different to ours.”  “H’m! were you long away?”  “Four years! and I was in the same place nearly all the time,--in one village.”  “You must have forgotten Russia, hadn’t you?”  “Yes, indeed I had--a good deal; and, would you believe it, I often wonder at myself for not having forgotten how to speak Russian? Even now, as I talk to you, I keep saying to myself ‘how well I am speaking it.’ Perhaps that is partly why I am so talkative this morning.
__label__di I assure you, ever since yesterday evening I have had the strongest desire to go on and on talking Russian.”  “H’m! yes; did you live in Petersburg in former years?”  This good flunkey, in spite of his conscientious scruples, really could not resist continuing such a very genteel and agreeable conversation.
__label__di “In Petersburg? Oh no! hardly at all, and now they say so much is changed in the place that even those who did know it well are obliged to relearn what they knew.
__label__di They talk a good deal about the new law courts, and changes there, don’t they?”  “H’m! yes, that’s true enough.
__label__di Well now, how is the law over there, do they administer it more justly than here?”  “Oh, I don’t know about that! I’ve heard much that is good about our legal administration, too.
__label__di There is no capital punishment here for one thing.”  “Is there over there?”  “Yes--I saw an execution in France--at Lyons.
__label__di Schneider took me over with him to see it.”  “What, did they hang the fellow?”  “No, they cut off people’s heads in France.”  “What did the fellow do?--yell?”  “Oh no--it’s the work of an instant.
__label__di They put a man inside a frame and a sort of broad knife falls by machinery--they call the thing a guillotine--it falls with fearful force and weight--the head springs off so quickly that you can’t wink your eye in between.
__label__di But all the preparations are so dreadful.
__label__di When they announce the sentence, you know, and prepare the criminal and tie his hands, and cart him off to the scaffold--that’s the fearful part of the business.
__label__di The people all crowd round--even women--though they don’t at all approve of women looking on.”  “No, it’s not a thing for women.”  “Of course not--of course not!--bah! The criminal was a fine intelligent fearless man; Le Gros was his name; and I may tell you--believe it or not, as you like--that when that man stepped upon the scaffold he _cried_, he did indeed,--he was as white as a bit of paper.
__label__di Isn’t it a dreadful idea that he should have cried--cried! Whoever heard of a grown man crying from fear--not a child, but a man who never had cried before--a grown man of forty-five years.
__label__di Imagine what must have been going on in that man’s mind at such a moment; what dreadful convulsions his whole spirit must have endured; it is an outrage on the soul that’s what it is.
__label__di Because it is said ‘thou shalt not kill,’ is he to be killed because he murdered some one else? No, it is not right, it’s an impossible theory.
__label__di I assure you, I saw the sight a month ago and it’s dancing before my eyes to this moment.
__label__di I dream of it, often.”  The prince had grown animated as he spoke, and a tinge of colour suffused his pale face, though his way of talking was as quiet as ever.
__label__di The servant followed his words with sympathetic interest.
__label__di Clearly he was not at all anxious to bring the conversation to an end.
__label__di Who knows? Perhaps he too was a man of imagination and with some capacity for thought.
__label__di “Well, at all events it is a good thing that there’s no pain when the poor fellow’s head flies off,” he remarked.
__label__di “Do you know, though,” cried the prince warmly, “you made that remark now, and everyone says the same thing, and the machine is designed with the purpose of avoiding pain, this guillotine I mean; but a thought came into my head then: what if it be a bad plan after all? You may laugh at my idea, perhaps--but I could not help its occurring to me all the same.
__label__di Now with the rack and tortures and so on--you suffer terrible pain of course; but then your torture is bodily pain only (although no doubt you have plenty of that) until you die.
__label__di But _here_ I should imagine the most terrible part of the whole punishment is, not the bodily pain at all--but the certain knowledge that in an hour,--then in ten minutes, then in half a minute, then now--this very _instant_--your soul must quit your body and that you will no longer be a man--and that this is certain, _certain_! That’s the point--the certainty of it.
__label__di Just that instant when you place your head on the block and hear the iron grate over your head--then--that quarter of a second is the most awful of all.
__label__di “This is not my own fantastical opinion--many people have thought the same; but I feel it so deeply that I’ll tell you what I think.
__label__di I believe that to execute a man for murder is to punish him immeasurably more dreadfully than is equivalent to his crime.
__label__di A murder by sentence is far more dreadful than a murder committed by a criminal.
__label__di The man who is attacked by robbers at night, in a dark wood, or anywhere, undoubtedly hopes and hopes that he may yet escape until the very moment of his death.
__label__di There are plenty of instances of a man running away, or imploring for mercy--at all events hoping on in some degree--even after his throat was cut.
__label__di But in the case of an execution, that last hope--having which it is so immeasurably less dreadful to die,--is taken away from the wretch and _certainty_ substituted in its place! There is his sentence, and with it that terrible certainty that he cannot possibly escape death--which, I consider, must be the most dreadful anguish in the world.
__label__di You may place a soldier before a cannon’s mouth in battle, and fire upon him--and he will still hope.
__label__di But read to that same soldier his death-sentence, and he will either go mad or burst into tears.
__label__di Who dares to say that any man can suffer this without going mad? No, no! it is an abuse, a shame, it is unnecessary--why should such a thing exist? Doubtless there may be men who have been sentenced, who have suffered this mental anguish for a while and then have been reprieved; perhaps such men may have been able to relate their feelings afterwards.
__label__di Our Lord Christ spoke of this anguish and dread.
__label__di No! no! no! No man should be treated so, no man, no man!”  The servant, though of course he could not have expressed all this as the prince did, still clearly entered into it and was greatly conciliated, as was evident from the increased amiability of his expression.
__label__di “If you are really very anxious for a smoke,” he remarked, “I think it might possibly be managed, if you are very quick about it.
__label__di You see they might come out and inquire for you, and you wouldn’t be on the spot.
__label__di You see that door there? Go in there and you’ll find a little room on the right; you can smoke there, only open the window, because I ought not to allow it really, and--.” But there was no time, after all.
__label__di A young fellow entered the ante-room at this moment, with a bundle of papers in his hand.
__label__di The footman hastened to help him take off his overcoat.
__label__di The new arrival glanced at the prince out of the corners of his eyes.
__label__di “This gentleman declares, Gavrila Ardalionovitch,” began the man, confidentially and almost familiarly, “that he is Prince Muishkin and a relative of Madame Epanchin’s.
__label__di He has just arrived from abroad, with nothing but a bundle by way of luggage--.”  The prince did not hear the rest, because at this point the servant continued his communication in a whisper.
__label__di Gavrila Ardalionovitch listened attentively, and gazed at the prince with great curiosity.
__label__di At last he motioned the man aside and stepped hurriedly towards the prince.
__label__di “Are you Prince Muishkin?” he asked, with the greatest courtesy and amiability.
__label__di He was a remarkably handsome young fellow of some twenty-eight summers, fair and of middle height; he wore a small beard, and his face was most intelligent.
__label__di Yet his smile, in spite of its sweetness, was a little thin, if I may so call it, and showed his teeth too evenly; his gaze though decidedly good-humoured and ingenuous, was a trifle too inquisitive and intent to be altogether agreeable.
__label__di “Probably when he is alone he looks quite different, and hardly smiles at all!” thought the prince.
__label__di He explained about himself in a few words, very much the same as he had told the footman and Rogojin beforehand.
__label__di Gavrila Ardalionovitch meanwhile seemed to be trying to recall something.
__label__di “Was it not you, then, who sent a letter a year or less ago--from Switzerland, I think it was--to Elizabetha Prokofievna (Mrs. Epanchin)?”  “It was.”  “Oh, then, of course they will remember who you are.
__label__di You wish to see the general? I’ll tell him at once--he will be free in a minute; but you--you had better wait in the ante-chamber,--hadn’t you? Why is he here?” he added, severely, to the man.
__label__di “I tell you, sir, he wished it himself!”  At this moment the study door opened, and a military man, with a portfolio under his arm, came out talking loudly, and after bidding good-bye to someone inside, took his departure.
__label__di “You there, Gania?” cried a voice from the study, “come in here, will you?”  Gavrila Ardalionovitch nodded to the prince and entered the room hastily.
__label__di A couple of minutes later the door opened again and the affable voice of Gania cried:  “Come in please, prince!”  III.
__label__di General Ivan Fedorovitch Epanchin was standing in the middle of the room, and gazed with great curiosity at the prince as he entered.
__label__di He even advanced a couple of steps to meet him.
__label__di The prince came forward and introduced himself.
__label__di “Quite so,” replied the general, “and what can I do for you?”  “Oh, I have no special business; my principal object was to make your acquaintance.
__label__di I should not like to disturb you.
__label__di I do not know your times and arrangements here, you see, but I have only just arrived.
__label__di I came straight from the station.
__label__di I am come direct from Switzerland.”  The general very nearly smiled, but thought better of it and kept his smile back.
__label__di Then he reflected, blinked his eyes, stared at his guest once more from head to foot; then abruptly motioned him to a chair, sat down himself, and waited with some impatience for the prince to speak.
__label__di Gania stood at his table in the far corner of the room, turning over papers.
__label__di “I have not much time for making acquaintances, as a rule,” said the general, “but as, of course, you have your object in coming, I--”  “I felt sure you would think I had some object in view when I resolved to pay you this visit,” the prince interrupted; “but I give you my word, beyond the pleasure of making your acquaintance I had no personal object whatever.”  “The pleasure is, of course, mutual; but life is not all pleasure, as you are aware.
__label__di There is such a thing as business, and I really do not see what possible reason there can be, or what we have in common to--”  “Oh, there is no reason, of course, and I suppose there is nothing in common between us, or very little; for if I am Prince Muishkin, and your wife happens to be a member of my house, that can hardly be called a ‘reason.’ I quite understand that.
__label__di And yet that was my whole motive for coming.
__label__di You see I have not been in Russia for four years, and knew very little about anything when I left.
__label__di I had been very ill for a long time, and I feel now the need of a few good friends.
__label__di In fact, I have a certain question upon which I much need advice, and do not know whom to go to for it.
__label__di I thought of your family when I was passing through Berlin.
__label__di ‘They are almost relations,’ I said to myself, ‘so I’ll begin with them; perhaps we may get on with each other, I with them and they with me, if they are kind people;’ and I have heard that you are very kind people!”  “Oh, thank you, thank you, I’m sure,” replied the general, considerably taken aback.
__label__di “May I ask where you have taken up your quarters?”  “Nowhere, as yet.”  “What, straight from the station to my house? And how about your luggage?”  “I only had a small bundle, containing linen, with me, nothing more.
__label__di I can carry it in my hand, easily.
__label__di There will be plenty of time to take a room in some hotel by the evening.”  “Oh, then you _do_ intend to take a room?”  “Of course.”  “To judge from your words, you came straight to my house with the intention of staying there.”  “That could only have been on your invitation.
__label__di I confess, however, that I should not have stayed here even if you had invited me, not for any particular reason, but because it is--well, contrary to my practice and nature, somehow.”  “Oh, indeed! Then it is perhaps as well that I neither _did_ invite you, nor _do_ invite you now.
__label__di Excuse me, prince, but we had better make this matter clear, once for all.
__label__di We have just agreed that with regard to our relationship there is not much to be said, though, of course, it would have been very delightful to us to feel that such relationship did actually exist; therefore, perhaps--”  “Therefore, perhaps I had better get up and go away?” said the prince, laughing merrily as he rose from his place; just as merrily as though the circumstances were by no means strained or difficult.
__label__di “And I give you my word, general, that though I know nothing whatever of manners and customs of society, and how people live and all that, yet I felt quite sure that this visit of mine would end exactly as it has ended now.
__label__di Oh, well, I suppose it’s all right; especially as my letter was not answered.
__label__di Well, good-bye, and forgive me for having disturbed you!”  The prince’s expression was so good-natured at this moment, and so entirely free from even a suspicion of unpleasant feeling was the smile with which he looked at the general as he spoke, that the latter suddenly paused, and appeared to gaze at his guest from quite a new point of view, all in an instant.
__label__di “Do you know, prince,” he said, in quite a different tone, “I do not know you at all, yet, and after all, Elizabetha Prokofievna would very likely be pleased to have a peep at a man of her own name.
__label__di Wait a little, if you don’t mind, and if you have time to spare?”  “Oh, I assure you I’ve lots of time, my time is entirely my own!” And the prince immediately replaced his soft, round hat on the table.
__label__di “I confess, I thought Elizabetha Prokofievna would very likely remember that I had written her a letter.
__label__di Just now your servant--outside there--was dreadfully suspicious that I had come to beg of you.
__label__di I noticed that! Probably he has very strict instructions on that score; but I assure you I did not come to beg.
__label__di I came to make some friends.
__label__di But I am rather bothered at having disturbed you; that’s all I care about.--”  “Look here, prince,” said the general, with a cordial smile, “if you really are the sort of man you appear to be, it may be a source of great pleasure to us to make your better acquaintance; but, you see, I am a very busy man, and have to be perpetually sitting here and signing papers, or off to see his excellency, or to my department, or somewhere; so that though I should be glad to see more of people, nice people--you see, I--however, I am sure you are so well brought up that you will see at once, and--but how old are you, prince?”  “Twenty-six.”  “No? I thought you very much younger.”  “Yes, they say I have a ‘young’ face.
__label__di As to disturbing you I shall soon learn to avoid doing that, for I hate disturbing people.
__label__di Besides, you and I are so differently constituted, I should think, that there must be very little in common between us.
__label__di Not that I will ever believe there is _nothing_ in common between any two people, as some declare is the case.
__label__di I am sure people make a great mistake in sorting each other into groups, by appearances; but I am boring you, I see, you--”  “Just two words: have you any means at all? Or perhaps you may be intending to undertake some sort of employment? Excuse my questioning you, but--”  “Oh, my dear sir, I esteem and understand your kindness in putting the question.
__label__di No; at present I have no means whatever, and no employment either, but I hope to find some.
__label__di I was living on other people abroad.
__label__di Schneider, the professor who treated me and taught me, too, in Switzerland, gave me just enough money for my journey, so that now I have but a few copecks left.
__label__di There certainly is one question upon which I am anxious to have advice, but--”  “Tell me, how do you intend to live now, and what are your plans?”  interrupted the general.
__label__di “I wish to work, somehow or other.”  “Oh yes, but then, you see, you are a philosopher.
__label__di Have you any talents, or ability in any direction--that is, any that would bring in money and bread? Excuse me again--”  “Oh, don’t apologize.
__label__di No, I don’t think I have either talents or special abilities of any kind; on the contrary.
__label__di I have always been an invalid and unable to learn much.
__label__di As for bread, I should think--”  The general interrupted once more with questions; while the prince again replied with the narrative we have heard before.
__label__di It appeared that the general had known Pavlicheff; but why the latter had taken an interest in the prince, that young gentleman could not explain; probably by virtue of the old friendship with his father, he thought.
__label__di The prince had been left an orphan when quite a little child, and Pavlicheff had entrusted him to an old lady, a relative of his own, living in the country, the child needing the fresh air and exercise of country life.
__label__di He was educated, first by a governess, and afterwards by a tutor, but could not remember much about this time of his life.
__label__di His fits were so frequent then, that they made almost an idiot of him (the prince used the expression “idiot” himself).
__label__di Pavlicheff had met Professor Schneider in Berlin, and the latter had persuaded him to send the boy to Switzerland, to Schneider’s establishment there, for the cure of his epilepsy, and, five years before this time, the prince was sent off.
__label__di But Pavlicheff had died two or three years since, and Schneider had himself supported the young fellow, from that day to this, at his own expense.
__label__di Although he had not quite cured him, he had greatly improved his condition; and now, at last, at the prince’s own desire, and because of a certain matter which came to the ears of the latter, Schneider had despatched the young man to Russia.
__label__di The general was much astonished.
__label__di “Then you have no one, absolutely _no_ one in Russia?” he asked.
__label__di “No one, at present; but I hope to make friends; and then I have a letter from--”  “At all events,” put in the general, not listening to the news about the letter, “at all events, you must have learned _something_, and your malady would not prevent your undertaking some easy work, in one of the departments, for instance?”  “Oh dear no, oh no! As for a situation, I should much like to find one for I am anxious to discover what I really am fit for.
__label__di I have learned a good deal in the last four years, and, besides, I read a great many Russian books.”  “Russian books, indeed? Then, of course, you can read and write quite correctly?”  “Oh dear, yes!”  “Capital! And your handwriting?”  “Ah, there I am _really_ talented! I may say I am a real caligraphist.
__label__di Let me write you something, just to show you,” said the prince, with some excitement.
__label__di “With pleasure! In fact, it is very necessary.
__label__di I like your readiness, prince; in fact, I must say--I--I--like you very well, altogether,” said the general.
__label__di “What delightful writing materials you have here, such a lot of pencils and things, and what beautiful paper! It’s a charming room altogether.
__label__di I know that picture, it’s a Swiss view.
__label__di I’m sure the artist painted it from nature, and that I have seen the very place--”  “Quite likely, though I bought it here.
__label__di Gania, give the prince some paper.
__label__di Here are pens and paper; now then, take this table.
__label__di What’s this?” the general continued to Gania, who had that moment taken a large photograph out of his portfolio, and shown it to his senior.
__label__di “Halloa! Nastasia Philipovna! Did she send it you herself? Herself?” he inquired, with much curiosity and great animation.
__label__di “She gave it me just now, when I called in to congratulate her.
__label__di I asked her for it long ago.
__label__di I don’t know whether she meant it for a hint that I had come empty-handed, without a present for her birthday, or what,”  added Gania, with an unpleasant smile.
__label__di “Oh, nonsense, nonsense,” said the general, with decision.
__label__di “What extraordinary ideas you have, Gania! As if she would hint; that’s not her way at all.
__label__di Besides, what could _you_ give her, without having thousands at your disposal? You might have given her your portrait, however.
__label__di Has she ever asked you for it?”  “No, not yet.
__label__di Very likely she never will.
__label__di I suppose you haven’t forgotten about tonight, have you, Ivan Fedorovitch? You were one of those specially invited, you know.”  “Oh no, I remember all right, and I shall go, of course.
__label__di I should think so! She’s twenty-five years old today! And, you know, Gania, you must be ready for great things; she has promised both myself and Afanasy Ivanovitch that she will give a decided answer tonight, yes or no.
__label__di So be prepared!”  Gania suddenly became so ill at ease that his face grew paler than ever.
__label__di “Are you sure she said that?” he asked, and his voice seemed to quiver as he spoke.
__label__di “Yes, she promised.
__label__di We both worried her so that she gave in; but she wished us to tell you nothing about it until the day.”  The general watched Gania’s confusion intently, and clearly did not like it.
__label__di “Remember, Ivan Fedorovitch,” said Gania, in great agitation, “that I was to be free too, until her decision; and that even then I was to have my ‘yes or no’ free.”  “Why, don’t you, aren’t you--” began the general, in alarm.
__label__di “Oh, don’t misunderstand--”  “But, my dear fellow, what are you doing, what do you mean?”  “Oh, I’m not rejecting her.
__label__di I may have expressed myself badly, but I didn’t mean that.”  “Reject her! I should think not!” said the general with annoyance, and apparently not in the least anxious to conceal it.
__label__di “Why, my dear fellow, it’s not a question of your rejecting her, it is whether you are prepared to receive her consent joyfully, and with proper satisfaction.
__label__di How are things going on at home?”  “At home? Oh, I can do as I like there, of course; only my father will make a fool of himself, as usual.
__label__di He is rapidly becoming a general nuisance.
__label__di I don’t ever talk to him now, but I hold him in check, safe enough.
__label__di I swear if it had not been for my mother, I should have shown him the way out, long ago.
__label__di My mother is always crying, of course, and my sister sulks.
__label__di I had to tell them at last that I intended to be master of my own destiny, and that I expect to be obeyed at home.
__label__di At least, I gave my sister to understand as much, and my mother was present.”  “Well, I must say, I cannot understand it!” said the general, shrugging his shoulders and dropping his hands.
__label__di “You remember your mother, Nina Alexandrovna, that day she came and sat here and groaned--and when I asked her what was the matter, she says, ‘Oh, it’s such a _dishonour_ to us!’ dishonour! Stuff and nonsense! I should like to know who can reproach Nastasia Philipovna, or who can say a word of any kind against her.
__label__di Did she mean because Nastasia had been living with Totski? What nonsense it is! You would not let her come near your daughters, says Nina Alexandrovna.
__label__di What next, I wonder? I don’t see how she can fail to--to understand--”  “Her own position?” prompted Gania.
__label__di “She does understand.
__label__di Don’t be annoyed with her.
__label__di I have warned her not to meddle in other people’s affairs.
__label__di However, although there’s comparative peace at home at present, the storm will break if anything is finally settled tonight.”  The prince heard the whole of the foregoing conversation, as he sat at the table, writing.
__label__di He finished at last, and brought the result of his labour to the general’s desk.
__label__di “So this is Nastasia Philipovna,” he said, looking attentively and curiously at the portrait.
__label__di “How wonderfully beautiful!” he immediately added, with warmth.
__label__di The picture was certainly that of an unusually lovely woman.
__label__di She was photographed in a black silk dress of simple design, her hair was evidently dark and plainly arranged, her eyes were deep and thoughtful, the expression of her face passionate, but proud.
__label__di She was rather thin, perhaps, and a little pale.
__label__di Both Gania and the general gazed at the prince in amazement.
__label__di “How do you know it’s Nastasia Philipovna?” asked the general; “you surely don’t know her already, do you?”  “Yes, I do! I have only been one day in Russia, but I have heard of the great beauty!” And the prince proceeded to narrate his meeting with Rogojin in the train and the whole of the latter’s story.
__label__di “There’s news!” said the general in some excitement, after listening to the story with engrossed attention.
__label__di “Oh, of course it’s nothing but humbug!” cried Gania, a little disturbed, however.
__label__di “It’s all humbug; the young merchant was pleased to indulge in a little innocent recreation! I have heard something of Rogojin!”  “Yes, so have I!” replied the general.
__label__di “Nastasia Philipovna told us all about the earrings that very day.
__label__di But now it is quite a different matter.
__label__di You see the fellow really has a million of roubles, and he is passionately in love.
__label__di The whole story smells of passion, and we all know what this class of gentry is capable of when infatuated.
__label__di I am much afraid of some disagreeable scandal, I am indeed!”  “You are afraid of the million, I suppose,” said Gania, grinning and showing his teeth.
__label__di “And you are _not_, I presume, eh?”  “How did he strike you, prince?” asked Gania, suddenly.
__label__di “Did he seem to be a serious sort of a man, or just a common rowdy fellow? What was your own opinion about the matter?”  While Gania put this question, a new idea suddenly flashed into his brain, and blazed out, impatiently, in his eyes.
__label__di The general, who was really agitated and disturbed, looked at the prince too, but did not seem to expect much from his reply.
__label__di “I really don’t quite know how to tell you,” replied the prince, “but it certainly did seem to me that the man was full of passion, and not, perhaps, quite healthy passion.
__label__di He seemed to be still far from well.
__label__di Very likely he will be in bed again in a day or two, especially if he lives fast.”  “No! do you think so?” said the general, catching at the idea.
__label__di “Yes, I do think so!”  “Yes, but the sort of scandal I referred to may happen at any moment.
__label__di It may be this very evening,” remarked Gania to the general, with a smile.
__label__di “Of course; quite so.
__label__di In that case it all depends upon what is going on in her brain at this moment.”  “You know the kind of person she is at times.”  “How? What kind of person is she?” cried the general, arrived at the limits of his patience.
__label__di “Look here, Gania, don’t you go annoying her tonight.
__label__di What you are to do is to be as agreeable towards her as ever you can.
__label__di Well, what are you smiling at? You must understand, Gania, that I have no interest whatever in speaking like this.
__label__di Whichever way the question is settled, it will be to my advantage.
__label__di Nothing will move Totski from his resolution, so I run no risk.
__label__di If there is anything I desire, you must know that it is your benefit only.
__label__di Can’t you trust me? You are a sensible fellow, and I have been counting on you; for, in this matter, that, that--”  “Yes, that’s the chief thing,” said Gania, helping the general out of his difficulties again, and curling his lips in an envenomed smile, which he did not attempt to conceal.
__label__di He gazed with his fevered eyes straight into those of the general, as though he were anxious that the latter might read his thoughts.
__label__di The general grew purple with anger.
__label__di “Yes, of course it is the chief thing!” he cried, looking sharply at Gania.
__label__di “What a very curious man you are, Gania! You actually seem to be _glad_ to hear of this millionaire fellow’s arrival--just as though you wished for an excuse to get out of the whole thing.
__label__di This is an affair in which you ought to act honestly with both sides, and give due warning, to avoid compromising others.
__label__di But, even now, there is still time.
__label__di Do you understand me? I wish to know whether you desire this arrangement or whether you do not? If not, say so,--and--and welcome! No one is trying to force you into the snare, Gavrila Ardalionovitch, if you see a snare in the matter, at least.”  “I do desire it,” murmured Gania, softly but firmly, lowering his eyes; and he relapsed into gloomy silence.
__label__di The general was satisfied.
__label__di He had excited himself, and was evidently now regretting that he had gone so far.
__label__di He turned to the prince, and suddenly the disagreeable thought of the latter’s presence struck him, and the certainty that he must have heard every word of the conversation.
__label__di But he felt at ease in another moment; it only needed one glance at the prince to see that in that quarter there was nothing to fear.
__label__di “Oh!” cried the general, catching sight of the prince’s specimen of caligraphy, which the latter had now handed him for inspection.
__label__di “Why, this is simply beautiful; look at that, Gania, there’s real talent there!”  On a sheet of thick writing-paper the prince had written in medieval characters the legend:  “The gentle Abbot Pafnute signed this.”  “There,” explained the prince, with great delight and animation, “there, that’s the abbot’s real signature--from a manuscript of the fourteenth century.
__label__di All these old abbots and bishops used to write most beautifully, with such taste and so much care and diligence.
__label__di Have you no copy of Pogodin, general? If you had one I could show you another type.
__label__di Stop a bit--here you have the large round writing common in France during the eighteenth century.
__label__di Some of the letters are shaped quite differently from those now in use.
__label__di It was the writing current then, and employed by public writers generally.
__label__di I copied this from one of them, and you can see how good it is.
__label__di Look at the well-rounded a and d.
__label__di I have tried to translate the French character into the Russian letters--a difficult thing to do, but I think I have succeeded fairly.
__label__di Here is a fine sentence, written in a good, original hand--‘Zeal triumphs over all.’ That is the script of the Russian War Office.
__label__di That is how official documents addressed to important personages should be written.
__label__di The letters are round, the type black, and the style somewhat remarkable.
__label__di A stylist would not allow these ornaments, or attempts at flourishes--just look at these unfinished tails!--but it has distinction and really depicts the soul of the writer.
__label__di He would like to give play to his imagination, and follow the inspiration of his genius, but a soldier is only at ease in the guard-room, and the pen stops half-way, a slave to discipline.
__label__di How delightful! The first time I met an example of this handwriting, I was positively astonished, and where do you think I chanced to find it? In Switzerland, of all places! Now that is an ordinary English hand.
__label__di It can hardly be improved, it is so refined and exquisite--almost perfection.
__label__di This is an example of another kind, a mixture of styles.
__label__di The copy was given me by a French commercial traveller.
__label__di It is founded on the English, but the downstrokes are a little blacker, and more marked.
__label__di Notice that the oval has some slight modification--it is more rounded.
__label__di This writing allows for flourishes; now a flourish is a dangerous thing! Its use requires such taste, but, if successful, what a distinction it gives to the whole! It results in an incomparable type--one to fall in love with!”  “Dear me! How you have gone into all the refinements and details of the question! Why, my dear fellow, you are not a caligraphist, you are an artist! Eh, Gania?”  “Wonderful!” said Gania.
__label__di “And he knows it too,” he added, with a sarcastic smile.
__label__di “You may smile,--but there’s a career in this,” said the general.
__label__di “You don’t know what a great personage I shall show this to, prince.
__label__di Why, you can command a situation at thirty-five roubles per month to start with.
__label__di However, it’s half-past twelve,” he concluded, looking at his watch; “so to business, prince, for I must be setting to work and shall not see you again today.
__label__di Sit down a minute.
__label__di I have told you that I cannot receive you myself very often, but I should like to be of some assistance to you, some small assistance, of a kind that would give you satisfaction.
__label__di I shall find you a place in one of the State departments, an easy place--but you will require to be accurate.
__label__di Now, as to your plans--in the house, or rather in the family of Gania here--my young friend, whom I hope you will know better--his mother and sister have prepared two or three rooms for lodgers, and let them to highly recommended young fellows, with board and attendance.
__label__di I am sure Nina Alexandrovna will take you in on my recommendation.
__label__di There you will be comfortable and well taken care of; for I do not think, prince, that you are the sort of man to be left to the mercy of Fate in a town like Petersburg.
__label__di Nina Alexandrovna, Gania’s mother, and Varvara Alexandrovna, are ladies for whom I have the highest possible esteem and respect.
__label__di Nina Alexandrovna is the wife of General Ardalion Alexandrovitch, my old brother in arms, with whom, I regret to say, on account of certain circumstances, I am no longer acquainted.
__label__di I give you all this information, prince, in order to make it clear to you that I am personally recommending you to this family, and that in so doing, I am more or less taking upon myself to answer for you.
__label__di The terms are most reasonable, and I trust that your salary will very shortly prove amply sufficient for your expenditure.
__label__di Of course pocket-money is a necessity, if only a little; do not be angry, prince, if I strongly recommend you to avoid carrying money in your pocket.
__label__di But as your purse is quite empty at the present moment, you must allow me to press these twenty-five roubles upon your acceptance, as something to begin with.
__label__di Of course we will settle this little matter another time, and if you are the upright, honest man you look, I anticipate very little trouble between us on that score.
__label__di Taking so much interest in you as you may perceive I do, I am not without my object, and you shall know it in good time.
__label__di You see, I am perfectly candid with you.
__label__di I hope, Gania, you have nothing to say against the prince’s taking up his abode in your house?”  “Oh, on the contrary! my mother will be very glad,” said Gania, courteously and kindly.
__label__di “I think only one of your rooms is engaged as yet, is it not? That fellow Ferd-Ferd--”  “Ferdishenko.”  “Yes--I don’t like that Ferdishenko.
__label__di I can’t understand why Nastasia Philipovna encourages him so.
__label__di Is he really her cousin, as he says?”  “Oh dear no, it’s all a joke.
__label__di No more cousin than I am.”  “Well, what do you think of the arrangement, prince?”  “Thank you, general; you have behaved very kindly to me; all the more so since I did not ask you to help me.
__label__di I don’t say that out of pride.
__label__di I certainly did not know where to lay my head tonight.
__label__di Rogojin asked me to come to his house, of course, but--”  “Rogojin? No, no, my good fellow.
__label__di I should strongly recommend you, paternally,--or, if you prefer it, as a friend,--to forget all about Rogojin, and, in fact, to stick to the family into which you are about to enter.”  “Thank you,” began the prince; “and since you are so very kind there is just one matter which I--”  “You must really excuse me,” interrupted the general, “but I positively haven’t another moment now.
__label__di I shall just tell Elizabetha Prokofievna about you, and if she wishes to receive you at once--as I shall advise her--I strongly recommend you to ingratiate yourself with her at the first opportunity, for my wife may be of the greatest service to you in many ways.
__label__di If she cannot receive you now, you must be content to wait till another time.
__label__di Meanwhile you, Gania, just look over these accounts, will you? We mustn’t forget to finish off that matter--”  The general left the room, and the prince never succeeded in broaching the business which he had on hand, though he had endeavoured to do so four times.
__label__di Gania lit a cigarette and offered one to the prince.
__label__di The latter accepted the offer, but did not talk, being unwilling to disturb Gania’s work.
__label__di He commenced to examine the study and its contents.
__label__di But Gania hardly so much as glanced at the papers lying before him; he was absent and thoughtful, and his smile and general appearance struck the prince still more disagreeably now that the two were left alone together.
__label__di Suddenly Gania approached our hero who was at the moment standing over Nastasia Philipovna’s portrait, gazing at it.
__label__di “Do you admire that sort of woman, prince?” he asked, looking intently at him.
__label__di He seemed to have some special object in the question.
__label__di “It’s a wonderful face,” said the prince, “and I feel sure that her destiny is not by any means an ordinary, uneventful one.
__label__di Her face is smiling enough, but she must have suffered terribly--hasn’t she? Her eyes show it--those two bones there, the little points under her eyes, just where the cheek begins.
__label__di It’s a proud face too, terribly proud! And I--I can’t say whether she is good and kind, or not.
__label__di Oh, if she be but good! That would make all well!”  “And would you marry a woman like that, now?” continued Gania, never taking his excited eyes off the prince’s face.
__label__di “I cannot marry at all,” said the latter.
__label__di “I am an invalid.”  “Would Rogojin marry her, do you think?”  “Why not? Certainly he would, I should think.
__label__di He would marry her tomorrow!--marry her tomorrow and murder her in a week!”  Hardly had the prince uttered the last word when Gania gave such a fearful shudder that the prince almost cried out.
__label__di “What’s the matter?” said he, seizing Gania’s hand.
__label__di “Your highness! His excellency begs your presence in her excellency’s apartments!” announced the footman, appearing at the door.
__label__di The prince immediately followed the man out of the room.
__label__di IV.
__label__di All three of the Miss Epanchins were fine, healthy girls, well-grown, with good shoulders and busts, and strong--almost masculine--hands; and, of course, with all the above attributes, they enjoyed capital appetites, of which they were not in the least ashamed.
__label__di Elizabetha Prokofievna sometimes informed the girls that they were a little too candid in this matter, but in spite of their outward deference to their mother these three young women, in solemn conclave, had long agreed to modify the unquestioning obedience which they had been in the habit of according to her; and Mrs. General Epanchin had judged it better to say nothing about it, though, of course, she was well aware of the fact.
__label__di It is true that her nature sometimes rebelled against these dictates of reason, and that she grew yearly more capricious and impatient; but having a respectful and well-disciplined husband under her thumb at all times, she found it possible, as a rule, to empty any little accumulations of spleen upon his head, and therefore the harmony of the family was kept duly balanced, and things went as smoothly as family matters can.
__label__di Mrs. Epanchin had a fair appetite herself, and generally took her share of the capital mid-day lunch which was always served for the girls, and which was nearly as good as a dinner.
__label__di The young ladies used to have a cup of coffee each before this meal, at ten o’clock, while still in bed.
__label__di This was a favourite and unalterable arrangement with them.
__label__di At half-past twelve, the table was laid in the small dining-room, and occasionally the general himself appeared at the family gathering, if he had time.
__label__di Besides tea and coffee, cheese, honey, butter, pan-cakes of various kinds (the lady of the house loved these best), cutlets, and so on, there was generally strong beef soup, and other substantial delicacies.
__label__di On the particular morning on which our story has opened, the family had assembled in the dining-room, and were waiting the general’s appearance, the latter having promised to come this day.
__label__di If he had been one moment late, he would have been sent for at once; but he turned up punctually.
__label__di As he came forward to wish his wife good-morning and kiss her hands, as his custom was, he observed something in her look which boded ill.
__label__di He thought he knew the reason, and had expected it, but still, he was not altogether comfortable.
__label__di His daughters advanced to kiss him, too, and though they did not look exactly angry, there was something strange in their expression as well.
__label__di The general was, owing to certain circumstances, a little inclined to be too suspicious at home, and needlessly nervous; but, as an experienced father and husband, he judged it better to take measures at once to protect himself from any dangers there might be in the air.
__label__di However, I hope I shall not interfere with the proper sequence of my narrative too much, if I diverge for a moment at this point, in order to explain the mutual relations between General Epanchin’s family and others acting a part in this history, at the time when we take up the thread of their destiny.
__label__di I have already stated that the general, though he was a man of lowly origin, and of poor education, was, for all that, an experienced and talented husband and father.
__label__di Among other things, he considered it undesirable to hurry his daughters to the matrimonial altar and to worry them too much with assurances of his paternal wishes for their happiness, as is the custom among parents of many grown-up daughters.
__label__di He even succeeded in ranging his wife on his side on this question, though he found the feat very difficult to accomplish, because unnatural; but the general’s arguments were conclusive, and founded upon obvious facts.
__label__di The general considered that the girls’ taste and good sense should be allowed to develop and mature deliberately, and that the parents’ duty should merely be to keep watch, in order that no strange or undesirable choice be made; but that the selection once effected, both father and mother were bound from that moment to enter heart and soul into the cause, and to see that the matter progressed without hindrance until the altar should be happily reached.
__label__di Besides this, it was clear that the Epanchins’ position gained each year, with geometrical accuracy, both as to financial solidity and social weight; and, therefore, the longer the girls waited, the better was their chance of making a brilliant match.
__label__di But again, amidst the incontrovertible facts just recorded, one more, equally significant, rose up to confront the family; and this was, that the eldest daughter, Alexandra, had imperceptibly arrived at her twenty-fifth birthday.
__label__di Almost at the same moment, Afanasy Ivanovitch Totski, a man of immense wealth, high connections, and good standing, announced his intention of marrying.
__label__di Afanasy Ivanovitch was a gentleman of fifty-five years of age, artistically gifted, and of most refined tastes.
__label__di He wished to marry well, and, moreover, he was a keen admirer and judge of beauty.
