__label__di Now, since Totski had, of late, been upon terms of great cordiality with Epanchin, which excellent relations were intensified by the fact that they were, so to speak, partners in several financial enterprises, it so happened that the former now put in a friendly request to the general for counsel with regard to the important step he meditated.
__label__di Might he suggest, for instance, such a thing as a marriage between himself and one of the general’s daughters?  Evidently the quiet, pleasant current of the family life of the Epanchins was about to undergo a change.
__label__di The undoubted beauty of the family, _par excellence_, was the youngest, Aglaya, as aforesaid.
__label__di But Totski himself, though an egotist of the extremest type, realized that he had no chance there; Aglaya was clearly not for such as he.
__label__di Perhaps the sisterly love and friendship of the three girls had more or less exaggerated Aglaya’s chances of happiness.
__label__di In their opinion, the latter’s destiny was not merely to be very happy; she was to live in a heaven on earth.
__label__di Aglaya’s husband was to be a compendium of all the virtues, and of all success, not to speak of fabulous wealth.
__label__di The two elder sisters had agreed that all was to be sacrificed by them, if need be, for Aglaya’s sake; her dowry was to be colossal and unprecedented.
__label__di The general and his wife were aware of this agreement, and, therefore, when Totski suggested himself for one of the sisters, the parents made no doubt that one of the two elder girls would probably accept the offer, since Totski would certainly make no difficulty as to dowry.
__label__di The general valued the proposal very highly.
__label__di He knew life, and realized what such an offer was worth.
__label__di The answer of the sisters to the communication was, if not conclusive, at least consoling and hopeful.
__label__di It made known that the eldest, Alexandra, would very likely be disposed to listen to a proposal.
__label__di Alexandra was a good-natured girl, though she had a will of her own.
__label__di She was intelligent and kind-hearted, and, if she were to marry Totski, she would make him a good wife.
__label__di She did not care for a brilliant marriage; she was eminently a woman calculated to soothe and sweeten the life of any man; decidedly pretty, if not absolutely handsome.
__label__di What better could Totski wish?  So the matter crept slowly forward.
__label__di The general and Totski had agreed to avoid any hasty and irrevocable step.
__label__di Alexandra’s parents had not even begun to talk to their daughters freely upon the subject, when suddenly, as it were, a dissonant chord was struck amid the harmony of the proceedings.
__label__di Mrs. Epanchin began to show signs of discontent, and that was a serious matter.
__label__di A certain circumstance had crept in, a disagreeable and troublesome factor, which threatened to overturn the whole business.
__label__di This circumstance had come into existence eighteen years before.
__label__di Close to an estate of Totski’s, in one of the central provinces of Russia, there lived, at that time, a poor gentleman whose estate was of the wretchedest description.
__label__di This gentleman was noted in the district for his persistent ill-fortune; his name was Barashkoff, and, as regards family and descent, he was vastly superior to Totski, but his estate was mortgaged to the last acre.
__label__di One day, when he had ridden over to the town to see a creditor, the chief peasant of his village followed him shortly after, with the news that his house had been burnt down, and that his wife had perished with it, but his children were safe.
__label__di Even Barashkoff, inured to the storms of evil fortune as he was, could not stand this last stroke.
__label__di He went mad and died shortly after in the town hospital.
__label__di His estate was sold for the creditors; and the little girls--two of them, of seven and eight years of age respectively,--were adopted by Totski, who undertook their maintenance and education in the kindness of his heart.
__label__di They were brought up together with the children of his German bailiff.
__label__di Very soon, however, there was only one of them left--Nastasia Philipovna--for the other little one died of whooping-cough.
__label__di Totski, who was living abroad at this time, very soon forgot all about the child; but five years after, returning to Russia, it struck him that he would like to look over his estate and see how matters were going there, and, arrived at his bailiff’s house, he was not long in discovering that among the children of the latter there now dwelt a most lovely little girl of twelve, sweet and intelligent, and bright, and promising to develop beauty of most unusual quality--as to which last Totski was an undoubted authority.
__label__di He only stayed at his country seat a few days on this occasion, but he had time to make his arrangements.
__label__di Great changes took place in the child’s education; a good governess was engaged, a Swiss lady of experience and culture.
__label__di For four years this lady resided in the house with little Nastia, and then the education was considered complete.
__label__di The governess took her departure, and another lady came down to fetch Nastia, by Totski’s instructions.
__label__di The child was now transported to another of Totski’s estates in a distant part of the country.
__label__di Here she found a delightful little house, just built, and prepared for her reception with great care and taste; and here she took up her abode together with the lady who had accompanied her from her old home.
__label__di In the house there were two experienced maids, musical instruments of all sorts, a charming “young lady’s library,” pictures, paint-boxes, a lap-dog, and everything to make life agreeable.
__label__di Within a fortnight Totski himself arrived, and from that time he appeared to have taken a great fancy to this part of the world and came down each summer, staying two and three months at a time.
__label__di So passed four years peacefully and happily, in charming surroundings.
__label__di At the end of that time, and about four months after Totski’s last visit (he had stayed but a fortnight on this occasion), a report reached Nastasia Philipovna that he was about to be married in St. Petersburg, to a rich, eminent, and lovely woman.
__label__di The report was only partially true, the marriage project being only in an embryo condition; but a great change now came over Nastasia Philipovna.
__label__di She suddenly displayed unusual decision of character; and without wasting time in thought, she left her country home and came up to St. Petersburg, straight to Totski’s house, all alone.
__label__di The latter, amazed at her conduct, began to express his displeasure; but he very soon became aware that he must change his voice, style, and everything else, with this young lady; the good old times were gone.
__label__di An entirely new and different woman sat before him, between whom and the girl he had left in the country last July there seemed nothing in common.
__label__di In the first place, this new woman understood a good deal more than was usual for young people of her age; so much indeed, that Totski could not help wondering where she had picked up her knowledge.
__label__di Surely not from her “young lady’s library”? It even embraced legal matters, and the “world” in general, to a considerable extent.
__label__di Her character was absolutely changed.
__label__di No more of the girlish alternations of timidity and petulance, the adorable naivete, the reveries, the tears, the playfulness...
__label__di It was an entirely new and hitherto unknown being who now sat and laughed at him, and informed him to his face that she had never had the faintest feeling for him of any kind, except loathing and contempt--contempt which had followed closely upon her sensations of surprise and bewilderment after her first acquaintance with him.
__label__di This new woman gave him further to understand that though it was absolutely the same to her whom he married, yet she had decided to prevent this marriage--for no particular reason, but that she _chose_ to do so, and because she wished to amuse herself at his expense for that it was “quite her turn to laugh a little now!”  Such were her words--very likely she did not give her real reason for this eccentric conduct; but, at all events, that was all the explanation she deigned to offer.
__label__di Meanwhile, Totski thought the matter over as well as his scattered ideas would permit.
__label__di His meditations lasted a fortnight, however, and at the end of that time his resolution was taken.
__label__di The fact was, Totski was at that time a man of fifty years of age; his position was solid and respectable; his place in society had long been firmly fixed upon safe foundations; he loved himself, his personal comforts, and his position better than all the world, as every respectable gentleman should!  At the same time his grasp of things in general soon showed Totski that he now had to deal with a being who was outside the pale of the ordinary rules of traditional behaviour, and who would not only threaten mischief but would undoubtedly carry it out, and stop for no one.
__label__di There was evidently, he concluded, something at work here; some storm of the mind, some paroxysm of romantic anger, goodness knows against whom or what, some insatiable contempt--in a word, something altogether absurd and impossible, but at the same time most dangerous to be met with by any respectable person with a position in society to keep up.
__label__di For a man of Totski’s wealth and standing, it would, of course, have been the simplest possible matter to take steps which would rid him at once from all annoyance; while it was obviously impossible for Nastasia Philipovna to harm him in any way, either legally or by stirring up a scandal, for, in case of the latter danger, he could so easily remove her to a sphere of safety.
__label__di However, these arguments would only hold good in case of Nastasia acting as others might in such an emergency.
__label__di She was much more likely to overstep the bounds of reasonable conduct by some extraordinary eccentricity.
__label__di Here the sound judgment of Totski stood him in good stead.
__label__di He realized that Nastasia Philipovna must be well aware that she could do nothing by legal means to injure him, and that her flashing eyes betrayed some entirely different intention.
__label__di Nastasia Philipovna was quite capable of ruining herself, and even of perpetrating something which would send her to Siberia, for the mere pleasure of injuring a man for whom she had developed so inhuman a sense of loathing and contempt.
__label__di He had sufficient insight to understand that she valued nothing in the world--herself least of all--and he made no attempt to conceal the fact that he was a coward in some respects.
__label__di For instance, if he had been told that he would be stabbed at the altar, or publicly insulted, he would undoubtedly have been frightened; but not so much at the idea of being murdered, or wounded, or insulted, as at the thought that if such things were to happen he would be made to look ridiculous in the eyes of society.
__label__di He knew well that Nastasia thoroughly understood him and where to wound him and how, and therefore, as the marriage was still only in embryo, Totski decided to conciliate her by giving it up.
__label__di His decision was strengthened by the fact that Nastasia Philipovna had curiously altered of late.
__label__di It would be difficult to conceive how different she was physically, at the present time, to the girl of a few years ago.
__label__di She was pretty then...
__label__di but now!...
__label__di Totski laughed angrily when he thought how short-sighted he had been.
__label__di In days gone by he remembered how he had looked at her beautiful eyes, how even then he had marvelled at their dark mysterious depths, and at their wondering gaze which seemed to seek an answer to some unknown riddle.
__label__di Her complexion also had altered.
__label__di She was now exceedingly pale, but, curiously, this change only made her more beautiful.
__label__di Like most men of the world, Totski had rather despised such a cheaply-bought conquest, but of late years he had begun to think differently about it.
__label__di It had struck him as long ago as last spring that he ought to be finding a good match for Nastasia; for instance, some respectable and reasonable young fellow serving in a government office in another part of the country.
__label__di How maliciously Nastasia laughed at the idea of such a thing, now!  However, it appeared to Totski that he might make use of her in another way; and he determined to establish her in St. Petersburg, surrounding her with all the comforts and luxuries that his wealth could command.
__label__di In this way he might gain glory in certain circles.
__label__di Five years of this Petersburg life went by, and, of course, during that time a great deal happened.
__label__di Totski’s position was very uncomfortable; having “funked” once, he could not totally regain his ease.
__label__di He was afraid, he did not know why, but he was simply _afraid_ of Nastasia Philipovna.
__label__di For the first two years or so he had suspected that she wished to marry him herself, and that only her vanity prevented her telling him so.
__label__di He thought that she wanted him to approach her with a humble proposal from his own side.
__label__di But to his great, and not entirely pleasurable amazement, he discovered that this was by no means the case, and that were he to offer himself he would be refused.
__label__di He could not understand such a state of things, and was obliged to conclude that it was pride, the pride of an injured and imaginative woman, which had gone to such lengths that it preferred to sit and nurse its contempt and hatred in solitude rather than mount to heights of hitherto unattainable splendour.
__label__di To make matters worse, she was quite impervious to mercenary considerations, and could not be bribed in any way.
__label__di Finally, Totski took cunning means to try to break his chains and be free.
__label__di He tried to tempt her in various ways to lose her heart; he invited princes, hussars, secretaries of embassies, poets, novelists, even Socialists, to see her; but not one of them all made the faintest impression upon Nastasia.
__label__di It was as though she had a pebble in place of a heart, as though her feelings and affections were dried up and withered for ever.
__label__di She lived almost entirely alone; she read, she studied, she loved music.
__label__di Her principal acquaintances were poor women of various grades, a couple of actresses, and the family of a poor schoolteacher.
__label__di Among these people she was much beloved.
__label__di She received four or five friends sometimes, of an evening.
__label__di Totski often came.
__label__di Lately, too, General Epanchin had been enabled with great difficulty to introduce himself into her circle.
__label__di Gania made her acquaintance also, and others were Ferdishenko, an ill-bred, and would-be witty, young clerk, and Ptitsin, a money-lender of modest and polished manners, who had risen from poverty.
__label__di In fact, Nastasia Philipovna’s beauty became a thing known to all the town; but not a single man could boast of anything more than his own admiration for her; and this reputation of hers, and her wit and culture and grace, all confirmed Totski in the plan he had now prepared.
__label__di And it was at this moment that General Epanchin began to play so large and important a part in the story.
__label__di When Totski had approached the general with his request for friendly counsel as to a marriage with one of his daughters, he had made a full and candid confession.
__label__di He had said that he intended to stop at no means to obtain his freedom; even if Nastasia were to promise to leave him entirely alone in future, he would not (he said) believe and trust her; words were not enough for him; he must have solid guarantees of some sort.
__label__di So he and the general determined to try what an attempt to appeal to her heart would effect.
__label__di Having arrived at Nastasia’s house one day, with Epanchin, Totski immediately began to speak of the intolerable torment of his position.
__label__di He admitted that he was to blame for all, but candidly confessed that he could not bring himself to feel any remorse for his original guilt towards herself, because he was a man of sensual passions which were inborn and ineradicable, and that he had no power over himself in this respect; but that he wished, seriously, to marry at last, and that the whole fate of the most desirable social union which he contemplated, was in her hands; in a word, he confided his all to her generosity of heart.
__label__di General Epanchin took up his part and spoke in the character of father of a family; he spoke sensibly, and without wasting words over any attempt at sentimentality, he merely recorded his full admission of her right to be the arbiter of Totski’s destiny at this moment.
__label__di He then pointed out that the fate of his daughter, and very likely of both his other daughters, now hung upon her reply.
__label__di To Nastasia’s question as to what they wished her to do, Totski confessed that he had been so frightened by her, five years ago, that he could never now be entirely comfortable until she herself married.
__label__di He immediately added that such a suggestion from him would, of course, be absurd, unless accompanied by remarks of a more pointed nature.
__label__di He very well knew, he said, that a certain young gentleman of good family, namely, Gavrila Ardalionovitch Ivolgin, with whom she was acquainted, and whom she received at her house, had long loved her passionately, and would give his life for some response from her.
__label__di The young fellow had confessed this love of his to him (Totski) and had also admitted it in the hearing of his benefactor, General Epanchin.
__label__di Lastly, he could not help being of opinion that Nastasia must be aware of Gania’s love for her, and if he (Totski) mistook not, she had looked with some favour upon it, being often lonely, and rather tired of her present life.
__label__di Having remarked how difficult it was for him, of all people, to speak to her of these matters, Totski concluded by saying that he trusted Nastasia Philipovna would not look with contempt upon him if he now expressed his sincere desire to guarantee her future by a gift of seventy-five thousand roubles.
__label__di He added that the sum would have been left her all the same in his will, and that therefore she must not consider the gift as in any way an indemnification to her for anything, but that there was no reason, after all, why a man should not be allowed to entertain a natural desire to lighten his conscience, etc., etc.; in fact, all that would naturally be said under the circumstances.
__label__di Totski was very eloquent all through, and, in conclusion, just touched on the fact that not a soul in the world, not even General Epanchin, had ever heard a word about the above seventy-five thousand roubles, and that this was the first time he had ever given expression to his intentions in respect to them.
__label__di Nastasia Philipovna’s reply to this long rigmarole astonished both the friends considerably.
__label__di Not only was there no trace of her former irony, of her old hatred and enmity, and of that dreadful laughter, the very recollection of which sent a cold chill down Totski’s back to this very day; but she seemed charmed and really glad to have the opportunity of talking seriously with him for once in a way.
__label__di She confessed that she had long wished to have a frank and free conversation and to ask for friendly advice, but that pride had hitherto prevented her; now, however, that the ice was broken, nothing could be more welcome to her than this opportunity.
__label__di First, with a sad smile, and then with a twinkle of merriment in her eyes, she admitted that such a storm as that of five years ago was now quite out of the question.
__label__di She said that she had long since changed her views of things, and recognized that facts must be taken into consideration in spite of the feelings of the heart.
__label__di What was done was done and ended, and she could not understand why Totski should still feel alarmed.
__label__di She next turned to General Epanchin and observed, most courteously, that she had long since known of his daughters, and that she had heard none but good report; that she had learned to think of them with deep and sincere respect.
__label__di The idea alone that she could in any way serve them, would be to her both a pride and a source of real happiness.
__label__di It was true that she was lonely in her present life; Totski had judged her thoughts aright.
__label__di She longed to rise, if not to love, at least to family life and new hopes and objects, but as to Gavrila Ardalionovitch, she could not as yet say much.
__label__di She thought it must be the case that he loved her; she felt that she too might learn to love him, if she could be sure of the firmness of his attachment to herself; but he was very young, and it was a difficult question to decide.
__label__di What she specially liked about him was that he worked, and supported his family by his toil.
__label__di She had heard that he was proud and ambitious; she had heard much that was interesting of his mother and sister, she had heard of them from Mr. Ptitsin, and would much like to make their acquaintance, but--another question!--would they like to receive her into their house? At all events, though she did not reject the idea of this marriage, she desired not to be hurried.
__label__di As for the seventy-five thousand roubles, Mr. Totski need not have found any difficulty or awkwardness about the matter; she quite understood the value of money, and would, of course, accept the gift.
__label__di She thanked him for his delicacy, however, but saw no reason why Gavrila Ardalionovitch should not know about it.
__label__di She would not marry the latter, she said, until she felt persuaded that neither on his part nor on the part of his family did there exist any sort of concealed suspicions as to herself.
__label__di She did not intend to ask forgiveness for anything in the past, which fact she desired to be known.
__label__di She did not consider herself to blame for anything that had happened in former years, and she thought that Gavrila Ardalionovitch should be informed as to the relations which had existed between herself and Totski during the last five years.
__label__di If she accepted this money it was not to be considered as indemnification for her misfortune as a young girl, which had not been in any degree her own fault, but merely as compensation for her ruined life.
__label__di She became so excited and agitated during all these explanations and confessions that General Epanchin was highly gratified, and considered the matter satisfactorily arranged once for all.
__label__di But the once bitten Totski was twice shy, and looked for hidden snakes among the flowers.
__label__di However, the special point to which the two friends particularly trusted to bring about their object (namely, Gania’s attractiveness for Nastasia Philipovna), stood out more and more prominently; the pourparlers had commenced, and gradually even Totski began to believe in the possibility of success.
__label__di Before long Nastasia and Gania had talked the matter over.
__label__di Very little was said--her modesty seemed to suffer under the infliction of discussing such a question.
__label__di But she recognized his love, on the understanding that she bound herself to nothing whatever, and that she reserved the right to say “no” up to the very hour of the marriage ceremony.
__label__di Gania was to have the same right of refusal at the last moment.
__label__di It soon became clear to Gania, after scenes of wrath and quarrellings at the domestic hearth, that his family were seriously opposed to the match, and that Nastasia was aware of this fact was equally evident.
__label__di She said nothing about it, though he daily expected her to do so.
__label__di There were several rumours afloat, before long, which upset Totski’s equanimity a good deal, but we will not now stop to describe them; merely mentioning an instance or two.
__label__di One was that Nastasia had entered into close and secret relations with the Epanchin girls--a most unlikely rumour; another was that Nastasia had long satisfied herself of the fact that Gania was merely marrying her for money, and that his nature was gloomy and greedy, impatient and selfish, to an extraordinary degree; and that although he had been keen enough in his desire to achieve a conquest before, yet since the two friends had agreed to exploit his passion for their own purposes, it was clear enough that he had begun to consider the whole thing a nuisance and a nightmare.
__label__di In his heart passion and hate seemed to hold divided sway, and although he had at last given his consent to marry the woman (as he said), under the stress of circumstances, yet he promised himself that he would “take it out of her,” after marriage.
__label__di Nastasia seemed to Totski to have divined all this, and to be preparing something on her own account, which frightened him to such an extent that he did not dare communicate his views even to the general.
__label__di But at times he would pluck up his courage and be full of hope and good spirits again, acting, in fact, as weak men do act in such circumstances.
__label__di However, both the friends felt that the thing looked rosy indeed when one day Nastasia informed them that she would give her final answer on the evening of her birthday, which anniversary was due in a very short time.
__label__di A strange rumour began to circulate, meanwhile; no less than that the respectable and highly respected General Epanchin was himself so fascinated by Nastasia Philipovna that his feeling for her amounted almost to passion.
__label__di What he thought to gain by Gania’s marriage to the girl it was difficult to imagine.
__label__di Possibly he counted on Gania’s complaisance; for Totski had long suspected that there existed some secret understanding between the general and his secretary.
__label__di At all events the fact was known that he had prepared a magnificent present of pearls for Nastasia’s birthday, and that he was looking forward to the occasion when he should present his gift with the greatest excitement and impatience.
__label__di The day before her birthday he was in a fever of agitation.
__label__di Mrs. Epanchin, long accustomed to her husband’s infidelities, had heard of the pearls, and the rumour excited her liveliest curiosity and interest.
__label__di The general remarked her suspicions, and felt that a grand explanation must shortly take place--which fact alarmed him much.
__label__di This is the reason why he was so unwilling to take lunch (on the morning upon which we took up this narrative) with the rest of his family.
__label__di Before the prince’s arrival he had made up his mind to plead business, and “cut” the meal; which simply meant running away.
__label__di He was particularly anxious that this one day should be passed--especially the evening--without unpleasantness between himself and his family; and just at the right moment the prince turned up--“as though Heaven had sent him on purpose,” said the general to himself, as he left the study to seek out the wife of his bosom.
__label__di V.
__label__di Mrs. General Epanchin was a proud woman by nature.
__label__di What must her feelings have been when she heard that Prince Muishkin, the last of his and her line, had arrived in beggar’s guise, a wretched idiot, a recipient of charity--all of which details the general gave out for greater effect! He was anxious to steal her interest at the first swoop, so as to distract her thoughts from other matters nearer home.
__label__di Mrs. Epanchin was in the habit of holding herself very straight, and staring before her, without speaking, in moments of excitement.
__label__di She was a fine woman of the same age as her husband, with a slightly hooked nose, a high, narrow forehead, thick hair turning a little grey, and a sallow complexion.
__label__di Her eyes were grey and wore a very curious expression at times.
__label__di She believed them to be most effective--a belief that nothing could alter.
__label__di “What, receive him! Now, at once?” asked Mrs. Epanchin, gazing vaguely at her husband as he stood fidgeting before her.
__label__di “Oh, dear me, I assure you there is no need to stand on ceremony with him,” the general explained hastily.
__label__di “He is quite a child, not to say a pathetic-looking creature.
__label__di He has fits of some sort, and has just arrived from Switzerland, straight from the station, dressed like a German and without a farthing in his pocket.
__label__di I gave him twenty-five roubles to go on with, and am going to find him some easy place in one of the government offices.
__label__di I should like you to ply him well with the victuals, my dears, for I should think he must be very hungry.”  “You astonish me,” said the lady, gazing as before.
__label__di “Fits, and hungry too! What sort of fits?”  “Oh, they don’t come on frequently, besides, he’s a regular child, though he seems to be fairly educated.
__label__di I should like you, if possible, my dears,” the general added, making slowly for the door, “to put him through his paces a bit, and see what he is good for.
__label__di I think you should be kind to him; it is a good deed, you know--however, just as you like, of course--but he is a sort of relation, remember, and I thought it might interest you to see the young fellow, seeing that this is so.”  “Oh, of course, mamma, if we needn’t stand on ceremony with him, we must give the poor fellow something to eat after his journey; especially as he has not the least idea where to go to,” said Alexandra, the eldest of the girls.
__label__di “Besides, he’s quite a child; we can entertain him with a little hide-and-seek, in case of need,” said Adelaida.
__label__di “Hide-and-seek? What do you mean?” inquired Mrs. Epanchin.
__label__di “Oh, do stop pretending, mamma,” cried Aglaya, in vexation.
__label__di “Send him up, father; mother allows.”  The general rang the bell and gave orders that the prince should be shown in.
__label__di “Only on condition that he has a napkin under his chin at lunch, then,”  said Mrs. Epanchin, “and let Fedor, or Mavra, stand behind him while he eats.
__label__di Is he quiet when he has these fits? He doesn’t show violence, does he?”  “On the contrary, he seems to be very well brought up.
__label__di His manners are excellent--but here he is himself.
__label__di Here you are, prince--let me introduce you, the last of the Muishkins, a relative of your own, my dear, or at least of the same name.
__label__di Receive him kindly, please.
__label__di They’ll bring in lunch directly, prince; you must stop and have some, but you must excuse me.
__label__di I’m in a hurry, I must be off--”  “We all know where _you_ must be off to!” said Mrs. Epanchin, in a meaning voice.
__label__di “Yes, yes--I must hurry away, I’m late! Look here, dears, let him write you something in your albums; you’ve no idea what a wonderful caligraphist he is, wonderful talent! He has just written out ‘Abbot Pafnute signed this’ for me.
__label__di Well, _au revoir!_”  “Stop a minute; where are you off to? Who is this abbot?” cried Mrs. Epanchin to her retreating husband in a tone of excited annoyance.
__label__di “Yes, my dear, it was an old abbot of that name--I must be off to see the count, he’s waiting for me, I’m late--Good-bye! _Au revoir_, prince!”--and the general bolted at full speed.
__label__di “Oh, yes--I know what count you’re going to see!” remarked his wife in a cutting manner, as she turned her angry eyes on the prince.
__label__di “Now then, what’s all this about?--What abbot--Who’s Pafnute?” she added, brusquely.
__label__di “Mamma!” said Alexandra, shocked at her rudeness.
__label__di Aglaya stamped her foot.
__label__di “Nonsense! Let me alone!” said the angry mother.
__label__di “Now then, prince, sit down here, no, nearer, come nearer the light! I want to have a good look at you.
__label__di So, now then, who is this abbot?”  “Abbot Pafnute,” said our friend, seriously and with deference.
__label__di “Pafnute, yes.
__label__di And who was he?”  Mrs. Epanchin put these questions hastily and brusquely, and when the prince answered she nodded her head sagely at each word he said.
__label__di “The Abbot Pafnute lived in the fourteenth century,” began the prince; “he was in charge of one of the monasteries on the Volga, about where our present Kostroma government lies.
__label__di He went to Oreol and helped in the great matters then going on in the religious world; he signed an edict there, and I have seen a print of his signature; it struck me, so I copied it.
__label__di When the general asked me, in his study, to write something for him, to show my handwriting, I wrote ‘The Abbot Pafnute signed this,’ in the exact handwriting of the abbot.
__label__di The general liked it very much, and that’s why he recalled it just now.”  “Aglaya, make a note of ‘Pafnute,’ or we shall forget him.
__label__di H’m! and where is this signature?”  “I think it was left on the general’s table.”  “Let it be sent for at once!”  “Oh, I’ll write you a new one in half a minute,” said the prince, “if you like!”  “Of course, mamma!” said Alexandra.
__label__di “But let’s have lunch now, we are all hungry!”  “Yes; come along, prince,” said the mother, “are you very hungry?”  “Yes; I must say that I am pretty hungry, thanks very much.”  “H’m! I like to see that you know your manners; and you are by no means such a person as the general thought fit to describe you.
__label__di Come along; you sit here, opposite to me,” she continued, “I wish to be able to see your face.
__label__di Alexandra, Adelaida, look after the prince! He doesn’t seem so very ill, does he? I don’t think he requires a napkin under his chin, after all; are you accustomed to having one on, prince?”  “Formerly, when I was seven years old or so.
__label__di I believe I wore one; but now I usually hold my napkin on my knee when I eat.”  “Of course, of course! And about your fits?”  “Fits?” asked the prince, slightly surprised.
__label__di “I very seldom have fits nowadays.
__label__di I don’t know how it may be here, though; they say the climate may be bad for me.”  “He talks very well, you know!” said Mrs. Epanchin, who still continued to nod at each word the prince spoke.
__label__di “I really did not expect it at all; in fact, I suppose it was all stuff and nonsense on the general’s part, as usual.
__label__di Eat away, prince, and tell me where you were born, and where you were brought up.
__label__di I wish to know all about you, you interest me very much!”  The prince expressed his thanks once more, and eating heartily the while, recommenced the narrative of his life in Switzerland, all of which we have heard before.
__label__di Mrs. Epanchin became more and more pleased with her guest; the girls, too, listened with considerable attention.
__label__di In talking over the question of relationship it turned out that the prince was very well up in the matter and knew his pedigree off by heart.
__label__di It was found that scarcely any connection existed between himself and Mrs. Epanchin, but the talk, and the opportunity of conversing about her family tree, gratified the latter exceedingly, and she rose from the table in great good humour.
__label__di “Let’s all go to my boudoir,” she said, “and they shall bring some coffee in there.
__label__di That’s the room where we all assemble and busy ourselves as we like best,” she explained.
__label__di “Alexandra, my eldest, here, plays the piano, or reads or sews; Adelaida paints landscapes and portraits (but never finishes any); and Aglaya sits and does nothing.
__label__di I don’t work too much, either.
__label__di Here we are, now; sit down, prince, near the fire and talk to us.
__label__di I want to hear you relate something.
__label__di I wish to make sure of you first and then tell my old friend, Princess Bielokonski, about you.
__label__di I wish you to know all the good people and to interest them.
__label__di Now then, begin!”  “Mamma, it’s rather a strange order, that!” said Adelaida, who was fussing among her paints and paint-brushes at the easel.
__label__di Aglaya and Alexandra had settled themselves with folded hands on a sofa, evidently meaning to be listeners.
__label__di The prince felt that the general attention was concentrated upon himself.
__label__di “I should refuse to say a word if _I_ were ordered to tell a story like that!” observed Aglaya.
__label__di “Why? what’s there strange about it? He has a tongue.
__label__di Why shouldn’t he tell us something? I want to judge whether he is a good story-teller; anything you like, prince--how you liked Switzerland, what was your first impression, anything.
__label__di You’ll see, he’ll begin directly and tell us all about it beautifully.”  “The impression was forcible--” the prince began.
__label__di “There, you see, girls,” said the impatient lady, “he _has_ begun, you see.”  “Well, then, _let_ him talk, mamma,” said Alexandra.
__label__di “This prince is a great humbug and by no means an idiot,” she whispered to Aglaya.
__label__di “Oh, I saw that at once,” replied the latter.
__label__di “I don’t think it at all nice of him to play a part.
__label__di What does he wish to gain by it, I wonder?”  “My first impression was a very strong one,” repeated the prince.
__label__di “When they took me away from Russia, I remember I passed through many German towns and looked out of the windows, but did not trouble so much as to ask questions about them.
__label__di This was after a long series of fits.
__label__di I always used to fall into a sort of torpid condition after such a series, and lost my memory almost entirely; and though I was not altogether without reason at such times, yet I had no logical power of thought.
__label__di This would continue for three or four days, and then I would recover myself again.
__label__di I remember my melancholy was intolerable; I felt inclined to cry; I sat and wondered and wondered uncomfortably; the consciousness that everything was strange weighed terribly upon me; I could understand that it was all foreign and strange.
__label__di I recollect I awoke from this state for the first time at Basle, one evening; the bray of a donkey aroused me, a donkey in the town market.
__label__di I saw the donkey and was extremely pleased with it, and from that moment my head seemed to clear.”  “A donkey? How strange! Yet it is not strange.
__label__di Anyone of us might fall in love with a donkey! It happened in mythological times,” said Madame Epanchin, looking wrathfully at her daughters, who had begun to laugh.
__label__di “Go on, prince.”  “Since that evening I have been specially fond of donkeys.
__label__di I began to ask questions about them, for I had never seen one before; and I at once came to the conclusion that this must be one of the most useful of animals--strong, willing, patient, cheap; and, thanks to this donkey, I began to like the whole country I was travelling through; and my melancholy passed away.”  “All this is very strange and interesting,” said Mrs. Epanchin.
__label__di “Now let’s leave the donkey and go on to other matters.
__label__di What are you laughing at, Aglaya? and you too, Adelaida? The prince told us his experiences very cleverly; he saw the donkey himself, and what have you ever seen? _you_ have never been abroad.”  “I have seen a donkey though, mamma!” said Aglaya.
__label__di “And I’ve heard one!” said Adelaida.
__label__di All three of the girls laughed out loud, and the prince laughed with them.
__label__di “Well, it’s too bad of you,” said mamma.
__label__di “You must forgive them, prince; they are good girls.
__label__di I am very fond of them, though I often have to be scolding them; they are all as silly and mad as march hares.”  “Oh, why shouldn’t they laugh?” said the prince.
__label__di “I shouldn’t have let the chance go by in their place, I know.
__label__di But I stick up for the donkey, all the same; he’s a patient, good-natured fellow.”  “Are you a patient man, prince? I ask out of curiosity,” said Mrs. Epanchin.
__label__di All laughed again.
__label__di “Oh, that wretched donkey again, I see!” cried the lady.
__label__di “I assure you, prince, I was not guilty of the least--”  “Insinuation? Oh! I assure you, I take your word for it.” And the prince continued laughing merrily.
__label__di “I must say it’s very nice of you to laugh.
__label__di I see you really are a kind-hearted fellow,” said Mrs. Epanchin.
__label__di “I’m not always kind, though.”  “I am kind myself, and _always_ kind too, if you please!” she retorted, unexpectedly; “and that is my chief fault, for one ought not to be always kind.
__label__di I am often angry with these girls and their father; but the worst of it is, I am always kindest when I am cross.
__label__di I was very angry just before you came, and Aglaya there read me a lesson--thanks, Aglaya, dear--come and kiss me--there--that’s enough” she added, as Aglaya came forward and kissed her lips and then her hand.
__label__di “Now then, go on, prince.
__label__di Perhaps you can think of something more exciting than about the donkey, eh?”  “I must say, again, _I_ can’t understand how you can expect anyone to tell you stories straight away, so,” said Adelaida.
__label__di “I know I never could!”  “Yes, but the prince can, because he is clever--cleverer than you are by ten or twenty times, if you like.
__label__di There, that’s so, prince; and seriously, let’s drop the donkey now--what else did you see abroad, besides the donkey?”  “Yes, but the prince told us about the donkey very cleverly, all the same,” said Alexandra.
__label__di “I have always been most interested to hear how people go mad and get well again, and that sort of thing.
__label__di Especially when it happens suddenly.”  “Quite so, quite so!” cried Mrs. Epanchin, delighted.
__label__di “I see you _can_ be sensible now and then, Alexandra.
__label__di You were speaking of Switzerland, prince?”  “Yes.
__label__di We came to Lucerne, and I was taken out in a boat.
__label__di I felt how lovely it was, but the loveliness weighed upon me somehow or other, and made me feel melancholy.”  “Why?” asked Alexandra.
__label__di “I don’t know; I always feel like that when I look at the beauties of nature for the first time; but then, I was ill at that time, of course!”  “Oh, but I should like to see it!” said Adelaida; “and I don’t know _when_ we shall ever go abroad.
__label__di I’ve been two years looking out for a good subject for a picture.
__label__di I’ve done all I know.
__label__di ‘The North and South I know by heart,’ as our poet observes.
__label__di Do help me to a subject, prince.”  “Oh, but I know nothing about painting.
__label__di It seems to me one only has to look, and paint what one sees.”  “But I don’t know _how_ to see!”  “Nonsense, what rubbish you talk!” the mother struck in.
__label__di “Not know how to see! Open your eyes and look! If you can’t see here, you won’t see abroad either.
__label__di Tell us what you saw yourself, prince!”  “Yes, that’s better,” said Adelaida; “the prince _learned to see_ abroad.”  “Oh, I hardly know! You see, I only went to restore my health.
__label__di I don’t know whether I learned to see, exactly.
__label__di I was very happy, however, nearly all the time.”  “Happy! you can be happy?” cried Aglaya.
__label__di “Then how can you say you did not learn to see? I should think you could teach _us_ to see!”  “Oh! _do_ teach us,” laughed Adelaida.
__label__di “Oh! I can’t do that,” said the prince, laughing too.
__label__di “I lived almost all the while in one little Swiss village; what can I teach you? At first I was only just not absolutely dull; then my health began to improve--then every day became dearer and more precious to me, and the longer I stayed, the dearer became the time to me; so much so that I could not help observing it; but why this was so, it would be difficult to say.”  “So that you didn’t care to go away anywhere else?”  “Well, at first I did; I was restless; I didn’t know however I should manage to support life--you know there are such moments, especially in solitude.
__label__di There was a waterfall near us, such a lovely thin streak of water, like a thread but white and moving.
__label__di It fell from a great height, but it looked quite low, and it was half a mile away, though it did not seem fifty paces.
__label__di I loved to listen to it at night, but it was then that I became so restless.
__label__di Sometimes I went and climbed the mountain and stood there in the midst of the tall pines, all alone in the terrible silence, with our little village in the distance, and the sky so blue, and the sun so bright, and an old ruined castle on the mountain-side, far away.
__label__di I used to watch the line where earth and sky met, and longed to go and seek there the key of all mysteries, thinking that I might find there a new life, perhaps some great city where life should be grander and richer--and then it struck me that life may be grand enough even in a prison.”  “I read that last most praiseworthy thought in my manual, when I was twelve years old,” said Aglaya.
__label__di “All this is pure philosophy,” said Adelaida.
__label__di “You are a philosopher, prince, and have come here to instruct us in your views.”  “Perhaps you are right,” said the prince, smiling.
__label__di “I think I am a philosopher, perhaps, and who knows, perhaps I do wish to teach my views of things to those I meet with?”  “Your philosophy is rather like that of an old woman we know, who is rich and yet does nothing but try how little she can spend.
__label__di She talks of nothing but money all day.
__label__di Your great philosophical idea of a grand life in a prison and your four happy years in that Swiss village are like this, rather,” said Aglaya.
__label__di “As to life in a prison, of course there may be two opinions,” said the prince.
__label__di “I once heard the story of a man who lived twelve years in a prison--I heard it from the man himself.
__label__di He was one of the persons under treatment with my professor; he had fits, and attacks of melancholy, then he would weep, and once he tried to commit suicide.
__label__di _His_ life in prison was sad enough; his only acquaintances were spiders and a tree that grew outside his grating--but I think I had better tell you of another man I met last year.
__label__di There was a very strange feature in this case, strange because of its extremely rare occurrence.
__label__di This man had once been brought to the scaffold in company with several others, and had had the sentence of death by shooting passed upon him for some political crime.
__label__di Twenty minutes later he had been reprieved and some other punishment substituted; but the interval between the two sentences, twenty minutes, or at least a quarter of an hour, had been passed in the certainty that within a few minutes he must die.
__label__di I was very anxious to hear him speak of his impressions during that dreadful time, and I several times inquired of him as to what he thought and felt.
__label__di He remembered everything with the most accurate and extraordinary distinctness, and declared that he would never forget a single iota of the experience.
__label__di “About twenty paces from the scaffold, where he had stood to hear the sentence, were three posts, fixed in the ground, to which to fasten the criminals (of whom there were several).
__label__di The first three criminals were taken to the posts, dressed in long white tunics, with white caps drawn over their faces, so that they could not see the rifles pointed at them.
__label__di Then a group of soldiers took their stand opposite to each post.
__label__di My friend was the eighth on the list, and therefore he would have been among the third lot to go up.
__label__di A priest went about among them with a cross: and there was about five minutes of time left for him to live.
__label__di “He said that those five minutes seemed to him to be a most interminable period, an enormous wealth of time; he seemed to be living, in these minutes, so many lives that there was no need as yet to think of that last moment, so that he made several arrangements, dividing up the time into portions--one for saying farewell to his companions, two minutes for that; then a couple more for thinking over his own life and career and all about himself; and another minute for a last look around.
__label__di He remembered having divided his time like this quite well.
__label__di While saying good-bye to his friends he recollected asking one of them some very usual everyday question, and being much interested in the answer.
__label__di Then having bade farewell, he embarked upon those two minutes which he had allotted to looking into himself; he knew beforehand what he was going to think about.
__label__di He wished to put it to himself as quickly and clearly as possible, that here was he, a living, thinking man, and that in three minutes he would be nobody; or if somebody or something, then what and where? He thought he would decide this question once for all in these last three minutes.
__label__di A little way off there stood a church, and its gilded spire glittered in the sun.
__label__di He remembered staring stubbornly at this spire, and at the rays of light sparkling from it.
__label__di He could not tear his eyes from these rays of light; he got the idea that these rays were his new nature, and that in three minutes he would become one of them, amalgamated somehow with them.
__label__di “The repugnance to what must ensue almost immediately, and the uncertainty, were dreadful, he said; but worst of all was the idea, ‘What should I do if I were not to die now? What if I were to return to life again? What an eternity of days, and all mine! How I should grudge and count up every minute of it, so as to waste not a single instant!’ He said that this thought weighed so upon him and became such a terrible burden upon his brain that he could not bear it, and wished they would shoot him quickly and have done with it.”  The prince paused and all waited, expecting him to go on again and finish the story.
__label__di “Is that all?” asked Aglaya.
__label__di “All? Yes,” said the prince, emerging from a momentary reverie.
__label__di “And why did you tell us this?”  “Oh, I happened to recall it, that’s all! It fitted into the conversation--”  “You probably wish to deduce, prince,” said Alexandra, “that moments of time cannot be reckoned by money value, and that sometimes five minutes are worth priceless treasures.
__label__di All this is very praiseworthy; but may I ask about this friend of yours, who told you the terrible experience of his life? He was reprieved, you say; in other words, they did restore to him that ‘eternity of days.’ What did he do with these riches of time? Did he keep careful account of his minutes?”  “Oh no, he didn’t! I asked him myself.
__label__di He said that he had not lived a bit as he had intended, and had wasted many, and many a minute.”  “Very well, then there’s an experiment, and the thing is proved; one cannot live and count each moment; say what you like, but one _cannot_.”  “That is true,” said the prince, “I have thought so myself.
__label__di And yet, why shouldn’t one do it?”  “You think, then, that you could live more wisely than other people?”  said Aglaya.
__label__di “I have had that idea.”  “And you have it still?”  “Yes--I have it still,” the prince replied.
__label__di He had contemplated Aglaya until now, with a pleasant though rather timid smile, but as the last words fell from his lips he began to laugh, and looked at her merrily.
__label__di “You are not very modest!” said she.
__label__di “But how brave you are!” said he.
__label__di “You are laughing, and I--that man’s tale impressed me so much, that I dreamt of it afterwards; yes, I dreamt of those five minutes...”  He looked at his listeners again with that same serious, searching expression.
__label__di “You are not angry with me?” he asked suddenly, and with a kind of nervous hurry, although he looked them straight in the face.
__label__di “Why should we be angry?” they cried.
__label__di “Only because I seem to be giving you a lecture, all the time!”  At this they laughed heartily.
__label__di “Please don’t be angry with me,” continued the prince.
__label__di “I know very well that I have seen less of life than other people, and have less knowledge of it.
__label__di I must appear to speak strangely sometimes...”  He said the last words nervously.
__label__di “You say you have been happy, and that proves you have lived, not less, but more than other people.
__label__di Why make all these excuses?” interrupted Aglaya in a mocking tone of voice.
__label__di “Besides, you need not mind about lecturing us; you have nothing to boast of.
__label__di With your quietism, one could live happily for a hundred years at least.
__label__di One might show you the execution of a felon, or show you one’s little finger.
__label__di You could draw a moral from either, and be quite satisfied.
__label__di That sort of existence is easy enough.”  “I can’t understand why you always fly into a temper,” said Mrs. Epanchin, who had been listening to the conversation and examining the faces of the speakers in turn.
__label__di “I do not understand what you mean.
__label__di What has your little finger to do with it? The prince talks well, though he is not amusing.
__label__di He began all right, but now he seems sad.”  “Never mind, mamma! Prince, I wish you had seen an execution,” said Aglaya.
__label__di “I should like to ask you a question about that, if you had.”  “I have seen an execution,” said the prince.
__label__di “You have!” cried Aglaya.
__label__di “I might have guessed it.
__label__di That’s a fitting crown to the rest of the story.
__label__di If you have seen an execution, how can you say you lived happily all the while?”  “But is there capital punishment where you were?” asked Adelaida.
__label__di “I saw it at Lyons.
__label__di Schneider took us there, and as soon as we arrived we came in for that.”  “Well, and did you like it very much? Was it very edifying and instructive?” asked Aglaya.
__label__di “No, I didn’t like it at all, and was ill after seeing it; but I confess I stared as though my eyes were fixed to the sight.
__label__di I could not tear them away.”  “I, too, should have been unable to tear my eyes away,” said Aglaya.
__label__di “They do not at all approve of women going to see an execution there.
__label__di The women who do go are condemned for it afterwards in the newspapers.”  “That is, by contending that it is not a sight for women they admit that it is a sight for men.
__label__di I congratulate them on the deduction.
__label__di I suppose you quite agree with them, prince?”  “Tell us about the execution,” put in Adelaida.
__label__di “I would much rather not, just now,” said the prince, a little disturbed and frowning slightly.
__label__di “You don’t seem to want to tell us,” said Aglaya, with a mocking air.
__label__di “No,--the thing is, I was telling all about the execution a little while ago, and--”  “Whom did you tell about it?”  “The man-servant, while I was waiting to see the general.”  “Our man-servant?” exclaimed several voices at once.
__label__di “Yes, the one who waits in the entrance hall, a greyish, red-faced man--”  “The prince is clearly a democrat,” remarked Aglaya.
__label__di “Well, if you could tell Aleksey about it, surely you can tell us too.”  “I do so want to hear about it,” repeated Adelaida.
__label__di “Just now, I confess,” began the prince, with more animation, “when you asked me for a subject for a picture, I confess I had serious thoughts of giving you one.
__label__di I thought of asking you to draw the face of a criminal, one minute before the fall of the guillotine, while the wretched man is still standing on the scaffold, preparatory to placing his neck on the block.”  “What, his face? only his face?” asked Adelaida.
__label__di “That would be a strange subject indeed.
__label__di And what sort of a picture would that make?”  “Oh, why not?” the prince insisted, with some warmth.
__label__di “When I was in Basle I saw a picture very much in that style--I should like to tell you about it; I will some time or other; it struck me very forcibly.”  “Oh, you shall tell us about the Basle picture another time; now we must have all about the execution,” said Adelaida.
__label__di “Tell us about that face as it appeared to your imagination--how should it be drawn?--just the face alone, do you mean?”  “It was just a minute before the execution,” began the prince, readily, carried away by the recollection and evidently forgetting everything else in a moment; “just at the instant when he stepped off the ladder on to the scaffold.
__label__di He happened to look in my direction: I saw his eyes and understood all, at once--but how am I to describe it? I do so wish you or somebody else could draw it, you, if possible.
__label__di I thought at the time what a picture it would make.
__label__di You must imagine all that went before, of course, all--all.
__label__di He had lived in the prison for some time and had not expected that the execution would take place for at least a week yet--he had counted on all the formalities and so on taking time; but it so happened that his papers had been got ready quickly.
__label__di At five o’clock in the morning he was asleep--it was October, and at five in the morning it was cold and dark.
__label__di The governor of the prison comes in on tip-toe and touches the sleeping man’s shoulder gently.
__label__di He starts up.
__label__di ‘What is it?’ he says.
__label__di ‘The execution is fixed for ten o’clock.’ He was only just awake, and would not believe at first, but began to argue that his papers would not be out for a week, and so on.
__label__di When he was wide awake and realized the truth, he became very silent and argued no more--so they say; but after a bit he said: ‘It comes very hard on one so suddenly’ and then he was silent again and said nothing.
__label__di “The three or four hours went by, of course, in necessary preparations--the priest, breakfast, (coffee, meat, and some wine they gave him; doesn’t it seem ridiculous?) And yet I believe these people give them a good breakfast out of pure kindness of heart, and believe that they are doing a good action.
__label__di Then he is dressed, and then begins the procession through the town to the scaffold.
__label__di I think he, too, must feel that he has an age to live still while they cart him along.
__label__di Probably he thought, on the way, ‘Oh, I have a long, long time yet.
__label__di Three streets of life yet! When we’ve passed this street there’ll be that other one; and then that one where the baker’s shop is on the right; and when shall we get there? It’s ages, ages!’ Around him are crowds shouting, yelling--ten thousand faces, twenty thousand eyes.
__label__di All this has to be endured, and especially the thought: ‘Here are ten thousand men, and not one of them is going to be executed, and yet I am to die.’ Well, all that is preparatory.
__label__di “At the scaffold there is a ladder, and just there he burst into tears--and this was a strong man, and a terribly wicked one, they say! There was a priest with him the whole time, talking; even in the cart as they drove along, he talked and talked.
__label__di Probably the other heard nothing; he would begin to listen now and then, and at the third word or so he had forgotten all about it.
__label__di “At last he began to mount the steps; his legs were tied, so that he had to take very small steps.
__label__di The priest, who seemed to be a wise man, had stopped talking now, and only held the cross for the wretched fellow to kiss.
__label__di At the foot of the ladder he had been pale enough; but when he set foot on the scaffold at the top, his face suddenly became the colour of paper, positively like white notepaper.
__label__di His legs must have become suddenly feeble and helpless, and he felt a choking in his throat--you know the sudden feeling one has in moments of terrible fear, when one does not lose one’s wits, but is absolutely powerless to move? If some dreadful thing were suddenly to happen; if a house were just about to fall on one;--don’t you know how one would long to sit down and shut one’s eyes and wait, and wait? Well, when this terrible feeling came over him, the priest quickly pressed the cross to his lips, without a word--a little silver cross it was--and he kept on pressing it to the man’s lips every second.
__label__di And whenever the cross touched his lips, the eyes would open for a moment, and the legs moved once, and he kissed the cross greedily, hurriedly--just as though he were anxious to catch hold of something in case of its being useful to him afterwards, though he could hardly have had any connected religious thoughts at the time.
__label__di And so up to the very block.
__label__di “How strange that criminals seldom swoon at such a moment! On the contrary, the brain is especially active, and works incessantly--probably hard, hard, hard--like an engine at full pressure.
__label__di I imagine that various thoughts must beat loud and fast through his head--all unfinished ones, and strange, funny thoughts, very likely!--like this, for instance: ‘That man is looking at me, and he has a wart on his forehead! and the executioner has burst one of his buttons, and the lowest one is all rusty!’ And meanwhile he notices and remembers everything.
__label__di There is one point that cannot be forgotten, round which everything else dances and turns about; and because of this point he cannot faint, and this lasts until the very final quarter of a second, when the wretched neck is on the block and the victim listens and waits and _knows_--that’s the point, he _knows_ that he is just _now_ about to die, and listens for the rasp of the iron over his head.
__label__di If I lay there, I should certainly listen for that grating sound, and hear it, too! There would probably be but the tenth part of an instant left to hear it in, but one would certainly hear it.
__label__di And imagine, some people declare that when the head flies off it is _conscious_ of having flown off! Just imagine what a thing to realize! Fancy if consciousness were to last for even five seconds!  “Draw the scaffold so that only the top step of the ladder comes in clearly.
__label__di The criminal must be just stepping on to it, his face as white as note-paper.
__label__di The priest is holding the cross to his blue lips, and the criminal kisses it, and knows and sees and understands everything.
__label__di The cross and the head--there’s your picture; the priest and the executioner, with his two assistants, and a few heads and eyes below.
__label__di Those might come in as subordinate accessories--a sort of mist.
__label__di There’s a picture for you.” The prince paused, and looked around.
__label__di “Certainly that isn’t much like quietism,” murmured Alexandra, half to herself.
__label__di “Now tell us about your love affairs,” said Adelaida, after a moment’s pause.
__label__di The prince gazed at her in amazement.
__label__di “You know,” Adelaida continued, “you owe us a description of the Basle picture; but first I wish to hear how you fell in love.
__label__di Don’t deny the fact, for you did, of course.
__label__di Besides, you stop philosophizing when you are telling about anything.”  “Why are you ashamed of your stories the moment after you have told them?” asked Aglaya, suddenly.
__label__di “How silly you are!” said Mrs. Epanchin, looking indignantly towards the last speaker.
__label__di “Yes, that wasn’t a clever remark,” said Alexandra.
__label__di “Don’t listen to her, prince,” said Mrs. Epanchin; “she says that sort of thing out of mischief.
__label__di Don’t think anything of their nonsense, it means nothing.
__label__di They love to chaff, but they like you.
__label__di I can see it in their faces--I know their faces.”  “I know their faces, too,” said the prince, with a peculiar stress on the words.
__label__di “How so?” asked Adelaida, with curiosity.
__label__di “What do _you_ know about our faces?” exclaimed the other two, in chorus.
__label__di But the prince was silent and serious.
__label__di All awaited his reply.
__label__di “I’ll tell you afterwards,” he said quietly.
__label__di “Ah, you want to arouse our curiosity!” said Aglaya.
__label__di “And how terribly solemn you are about it!”  “Very well,” interrupted Adelaida, “then if you can read faces so well, you _must_ have been in love.
__label__di Come now; I’ve guessed--let’s have the secret!”  “I have not been in love,” said the prince, as quietly and seriously as before.
__label__di “I have been happy in another way.”  “How, how?”  “Well, I’ll tell you,” said the prince, apparently in a deep reverie.
__label__di VI.
__label__di “Here you all are,” began the prince, “settling yourselves down to listen to me with so much curiosity, that if I do not satisfy you you will probably be angry with me.
__label__di No, no! I’m only joking!” he added, hastily, with a smile.
__label__di “Well, then--they were all children there, and I was always among children and only with children.
__label__di They were the children of the village in which I lived, and they went to the school there--all of them.
__label__di I did not teach them, oh no; there was a master for that, one Jules Thibaut.
__label__di I may have taught them some things, but I was among them just as an outsider, and I passed all four years of my life there among them.
__label__di I wished for nothing better; I used to tell them everything and hid nothing from them.
__label__di Their fathers and relations were very angry with me, because the children could do nothing without me at last, and used to throng after me at all times.
__label__di The schoolmaster was my greatest enemy in the end! I had many enemies, and all because of the children.
__label__di Even Schneider reproached me.
__label__di What were they afraid of? One can tell a child everything, anything.
__label__di I have often been struck by the fact that parents know their children so little.
__label__di They should not conceal so much from them.
__label__di How well even little children understand that their parents conceal things from them, because they consider them too young to understand! Children are capable of giving advice in the most important matters.
__label__di How can one deceive these dear little birds, when they look at one so sweetly and confidingly? I call them birds because there is nothing in the world better than birds!  “However, most of the people were angry with me about one and the same thing; but Thibaut simply was jealous of me.
__label__di At first he had wagged his head and wondered how it was that the children understood what I told them so well, and could not learn from him; and he laughed like anything when I replied that neither he nor I could teach them very much, but that _they_ might teach us a good deal.
__label__di “How he could hate me and tell scandalous stories about me, living among children as he did, is what I cannot understand.
__label__di Children soothe and heal the wounded heart.
__label__di I remember there was one poor fellow at our professor’s who was being treated for madness, and you have no idea what those children did for him, eventually.
__label__di I don’t think he was mad, but only terribly unhappy.
__label__di But I’ll tell you all about him another day.
__label__di Now I must get on with this story.
__label__di “The children did not love me at first; I was such a sickly, awkward kind of a fellow then--and I know I am ugly.
__label__di Besides, I was a foreigner.
__label__di The children used to laugh at me, at first; and they even went so far as to throw stones at me, when they saw me kiss Marie.
__label__di I only kissed her once in my life--no, no, don’t laugh!” The prince hastened to suppress the smiles of his audience at this point.
__label__di “It was not a matter of _love_ at all! If only you knew what a miserable creature she was, you would have pitied her, just as I did.
__label__di She belonged to our village.
__label__di Her mother was an old, old woman, and they used to sell string and thread, and soap and tobacco, out of the window of their little house, and lived on the pittance they gained by this trade.
__label__di The old woman was ill and very old, and could hardly move.
__label__di Marie was her daughter, a girl of twenty, weak and thin and consumptive; but still she did heavy work at the houses around, day by day.
__label__di Well, one fine day a commercial traveller betrayed her and carried her off; and a week later he deserted her.
__label__di She came home dirty, draggled, and shoeless; she had walked for a whole week without shoes; she had slept in the fields, and caught a terrible cold; her feet were swollen and sore, and her hands torn and scratched all over.
__label__di She never had been pretty even before; but her eyes were quiet, innocent, kind eyes.
__label__di “She was very quiet always--and I remember once, when she had suddenly begun singing at her work, everyone said, ‘Marie tried to sing today!’ and she got so chaffed that she was silent for ever after.
__label__di She had been treated kindly in the place before; but when she came back now--ill and shunned and miserable--not one of them all had the slightest sympathy for her.
__label__di Cruel people! Oh, what hazy understandings they have on such matters! Her mother was the first to show the way.
__label__di She received her wrathfully, unkindly, and with contempt.
__label__di ‘You have disgraced me,’ she said.
__label__di She was the first to cast her into ignominy; but when they all heard that Marie had returned to the village, they ran out to see her and crowded into the little cottage--old men, children, women, girls--such a hurrying, stamping, greedy crowd.
__label__di Marie was lying on the floor at the old woman’s feet, hungry, torn, draggled, crying, miserable.
__label__di “When everyone crowded into the room she hid her face in her dishevelled hair and lay cowering on the floor.
__label__di Everyone looked at her as though she were a piece of dirt off the road.
__label__di The old men scolded and condemned, and the young ones laughed at her.
__label__di The women condemned her too, and looked at her contemptuously, just as though she were some loathsome insect.
__label__di “Her mother allowed all this to go on, and nodded her head and encouraged them.
__label__di The old woman was very ill at that time, and knew she was dying (she really did die a couple of months later), and though she felt the end approaching she never thought of forgiving her daughter, to the very day of her death.
__label__di She would not even speak to her.
__label__di She made her sleep on straw in a shed, and hardly gave her food enough to support life.
__label__di “Marie was very gentle to her mother, and nursed her, and did everything for her; but the old woman accepted all her services without a word and never showed her the slightest kindness.
__label__di Marie bore all this; and I could see when I got to know her that she thought it quite right and fitting, considering herself the lowest and meanest of creatures.
__label__di “When the old woman took to her bed finally, the other old women in the village sat with her by turns, as the custom is there; and then Marie was quite driven out of the house.
__label__di They gave her no food at all, and she could not get any work in the village; none would employ her.
__label__di The men seemed to consider her no longer a woman, they said such dreadful things to her.
__label__di Sometimes on Sundays, if they were drunk enough, they used to throw her a penny or two, into the mud, and Marie would silently pick up the money.
__label__di She had began to spit blood at that time.
__label__di “At last her rags became so tattered and torn that she was ashamed of appearing in the village any longer.
__label__di The children used to pelt her with mud; so she begged to be taken on as assistant cowherd, but the cowherd would not have her.
__label__di Then she took to helping him without leave; and he saw how valuable her assistance was to him, and did not drive her away again; on the contrary, he occasionally gave her the remnants of his dinner, bread and cheese.
__label__di He considered that he was being very kind.
__label__di When the mother died, the village parson was not ashamed to hold Marie up to public derision and shame.
__label__di Marie was standing at the coffin’s head, in all her rags, crying.
__label__di “A crowd of people had collected to see how she would cry.
__label__di The parson, a young fellow ambitious of becoming a great preacher, began his sermon and pointed to Marie.
__label__di ‘There,’ he said, ‘there is the cause of the death of this venerable woman’--(which was a lie, because she had been ill for at least two years)--‘there she stands before you, and dares not lift her eyes from the ground, because she knows that the finger of God is upon her.
__label__di Look at her tatters and rags--the badge of those who lose their virtue.
__label__di Who is she? her daughter!’ and so on to the end.
__label__di “And just fancy, this infamy pleased them, all of them, nearly.
__label__di Only the children had altered--for then they were all on my side and had learned to love Marie.
__label__di “This is how it was: I had wished to do something for Marie; I longed to give her some money, but I never had a farthing while I was there.
__label__twp Well, Prince, so Genoa and Lucca are now just family estates of the Buonapartes.
__label__twp But I warn you, if you don’t tell me that this means war, if you still try to defend the infamies and horrors perpetrated by that Antichrist—I really believe he is Antichrist—I will have nothing more to do with you and you are no longer my friend, no longer my ‘faithful slave,’ as you call yourself! But how do you do? I see I have frightened you—sit down and tell me all the news.”  It was in July, 1805, and the speaker was the well-known Anna Pávlovna Schérer, maid of honor and favorite of the Empress Márya Fëdorovna.
__label__twp With these words she greeted Prince Vasíli Kurágin, a man of high rank and importance, who was the first to arrive at her reception.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna had had a cough for some days.
__label__twp She was, as she said, suffering from la grippe; grippe being then a new word in St.
__label__twp Petersburg, used only by the elite.
__label__twp All her invitations without exception, written in French, and delivered by a scarlet-liveried footman that morning, ran as follows:  “If you have nothing better to do, Count (or Prince), and if the prospect of spending an evening with a poor invalid is not too terrible, I shall be very charmed to see you tonight between 7 and 10—Annette Schérer.”  “Heavens! what a virulent attack!” replied the prince, not in the least disconcerted by this reception.
__label__twp He had just entered, wearing an embroidered court uniform, knee breeches, and shoes, and had stars on his breast and a serene expression on his flat face.
__label__twp He spoke in that refined French in which our grandfathers not only spoke but thought, and with the gentle, patronizing intonation natural to a man of importance who had grown old in society and at court.
__label__twp He went up to Anna Pávlovna, kissed her hand, presenting to her his bald, scented, and shining head, and complacently seated himself on the sofa.
__label__twp “First of all, dear friend, tell me how you are.
__label__twp Set your friend’s mind at rest,” said he without altering his tone, beneath the politeness and affected sympathy of which indifference and even irony could be discerned.
__label__twp “Can one be well while suffering morally? Can one be calm in times like these if one has any feeling?” said Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp “You are staying the whole evening, I hope?”  “And the fete at the English ambassador’s? Today is Wednesday.
__label__twp I must put in an appearance there,” said the prince.
__label__twp “My daughter is coming for me to take me there.”
__label__twp “I thought today’s fete had been canceled.
__label__twp I confess all these festivities and fireworks are becoming wearisome.”  “If they had known that you wished it, the entertainment would have been put off,” said the prince, who, like a wound-up clock, by force of habit said things he did not even wish to be believed.
__label__twp “Don’t tease! Well, and what has been decided about Novosíltsev’s dispatch? You know everything.”  “What can one say about it?” replied the prince in a cold, listless tone.
__label__twp “What has been decided? They have decided that Buonaparte has burnt his boats, and I believe that we are ready to burn ours.”  Prince Vasíli always spoke languidly, like an actor repeating a stale part.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna Schérer on the contrary, despite her forty years, overflowed with animation and impulsiveness.
__label__twp To be an enthusiast had become her social vocation and, sometimes even when she did not feel like it, she became enthusiastic in order not to disappoint the expectations of those who knew her.
__label__twp The subdued smile which, though it did not suit her faded features, always played round her lips expressed, as in a spoiled child, a continual consciousness of her charming defect, which she neither wished, nor could, nor considered it necessary, to correct.
__label__twp In the midst of a conversation on political matters Anna Pávlovna burst out:  “Oh, don’t speak to me of Austria.
__label__twp Perhaps I don’t understand things, but Austria never has wished, and does not wish, for war.
__label__twp She is betraying us! Russia alone must save Europe.
__label__twp Our gracious sovereign recognizes his high vocation and will be true to it.
__label__twp That is the one thing I have faith in! Our good and wonderful sovereign has to perform the noblest role on earth, and he is so virtuous and noble that God will not forsake him.
