__label__twp He will fulfill his vocation and crush the hydra of revolution, which has become more terrible than ever in the person of this murderer and villain! We alone must avenge the blood of the just one....
__label__twp Whom, I ask you, can we rely on?...
__label__twp England with her commercial spirit will not and cannot understand the Emperor Alexander’s loftiness of soul.
__label__twp She has refused to evacuate Malta.
__label__twp She wanted to find, and still seeks, some secret motive in our actions.
__label__twp What answer did Novosíltsev get? None.
__label__twp The English have not understood and cannot understand the self-abnegation of our Emperor who wants nothing for himself, but only desires the good of mankind.
__label__twp And what have they promised? Nothing! And what little they have promised they will not perform! Prussia has always declared that Buonaparte is invincible, and that all Europe is powerless before him....
__label__twp And I don’t believe a word that Hardenburg says, or Haugwitz either.
__label__twp This famous Prussian neutrality is just a trap.
__label__twp I have faith only in God and the lofty destiny of our adored monarch.
__label__twp He will save Europe!”  She suddenly paused, smiling at her own impetuosity.
__label__twp “I think,” said the prince with a smile, “that if you had been sent instead of our dear Wintzingerode you would have captured the King of Prussia’s consent by assault.
__label__twp You are so eloquent.
__label__twp Will you give me a cup of tea?”  “In a moment.
__label__twp À propos,” she added, becoming calm again, “I am expecting two very interesting men tonight, le Vicomte de Mortemart, who is connected with the Montmorencys through the Rohans, one of the best French families.
__label__twp He is one of the genuine émigrés, the good ones.
__label__twp And also the Abbé Morio.
__label__twp Do you know that profound thinker? He has been received by the Emperor.
__label__twp Had you heard?”  “I shall be delighted to meet them,” said the prince.
__label__twp “But tell me,” he added with studied carelessness as if it had only just occurred to him, though the question he was about to ask was the chief motive of his visit, “is it true that the Dowager Empress wants Baron Funke to be appointed first secretary at Vienna? The baron by all accounts is a poor creature.”  Prince Vasíli wished to obtain this post for his son, but others were trying through the Dowager Empress Márya Fëdorovna to secure it for the baron.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna almost closed her eyes to indicate that neither she nor anyone else had a right to criticize what the Empress desired or was pleased with.
__label__twp “Baron Funke has been recommended to the Dowager Empress by her sister,” was all she said, in a dry and mournful tone.
__label__twp As she named the Empress, Anna Pávlovna’s face suddenly assumed an expression of profound and sincere devotion and respect mingled with sadness, and this occurred every time she mentioned her illustrious patroness.
__label__twp She added that Her Majesty had deigned to show Baron Funke beaucoup d’estime, and again her face clouded over with sadness.
__label__twp The prince was silent and looked indifferent.
__label__twp But, with the womanly and courtierlike quickness and tact habitual to her, Anna Pávlovna wished both to rebuke him (for daring to speak as he had done of a man recommended to the Empress) and at the same time to console him, so she said:  “Now about your family.
__label__twp Do you know that since your daughter came out everyone has been enraptured by her? They say she is amazingly beautiful.”  The prince bowed to signify his respect and gratitude.
__label__twp “I often think,” she continued after a short pause, drawing nearer to the prince and smiling amiably at him as if to show that political and social topics were ended and the time had come for intimate conversation—“I often think how unfairly sometimes the joys of life are distributed.
__label__twp Why has fate given you two such splendid children? I don’t speak of Anatole, your youngest.
__label__twp I don’t like him,” she added in a tone admitting of no rejoinder and raising her eyebrows.
__label__twp “Two such charming children.
__label__twp And really you appreciate them less than anyone, and so you don’t deserve to have them.”  And she smiled her ecstatic smile.
__label__twp “I can’t help it,” said the prince.
__label__twp “Lavater would have said I lack the bump of paternity.”  “Don’t joke; I mean to have a serious talk with you.
__label__twp Do you know I am dissatisfied with your younger son? Between ourselves” (and her face assumed its melancholy expression), “he was mentioned at Her Majesty’s and you were pitied....”  The prince answered nothing, but she looked at him significantly, awaiting a reply.
__label__twp He frowned.
__label__twp “What would you have me do?” he said at last.
__label__twp “You know I did all a father could for their education, and they have both turned out fools.
__label__twp Hippolyte is at least a quiet fool, but Anatole is an active one.
__label__twp That is the only difference between them.” He said this smiling in a way more natural and animated than usual, so that the wrinkles round his mouth very clearly revealed something unexpectedly coarse and unpleasant.
__label__twp “And why are children born to such men as you? If you were not a father there would be nothing I could reproach you with,” said Anna Pávlovna, looking up pensively.
__label__twp “I am your faithful slave and to you alone I can confess that my children are the bane of my life.
__label__twp It is the cross I have to bear.
__label__twp That is how I explain it to myself.
__label__twp It can’t be helped!”  He said no more, but expressed his resignation to cruel fate by a gesture.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna meditated.
__label__twp “Have you never thought of marrying your prodigal son Anatole?” she asked.
__label__twp “They say old maids have a mania for matchmaking, and though I don’t feel that weakness in myself as yet, I know a little person who is very unhappy with her father.
__label__twp She is a relation of yours, Princess Mary Bolkónskaya.”  Prince Vasíli did not reply, though, with the quickness of memory and perception befitting a man of the world, he indicated by a movement of the head that he was considering this information.
__label__twp “Do you know,” he said at last, evidently unable to check the sad current of his thoughts, “that Anatole is costing me forty thousand rubles a year? And,” he went on after a pause, “what will it be in five years, if he goes on like this?” Presently he added: “That’s what we fathers have to put up with....
__label__twp Is this princess of yours rich?”  “Her father is very rich and stingy.
__label__twp He lives in the country.
__label__twp He is the well-known Prince Bolkónski who had to retire from the army under the late Emperor, and was nicknamed ‘the King of Prussia.’ He is very clever but eccentric, and a bore.
__label__twp The poor girl is very unhappy.
__label__twp She has a brother; I think you know him, he married Lise Meinen lately.
__label__twp He is an aide-de-camp of Kutúzov’s and will be here tonight.”  “Listen, dear Annette,” said the prince, suddenly taking Anna Pávlovna’s hand and for some reason drawing it downwards.
__label__twp “Arrange that affair for me and I shall always be your most devoted slave-slafe with an f, as a village elder of mine writes in his reports.
__label__twp She is rich and of good family and that’s all I want.”  And with the familiarity and easy grace peculiar to him, he raised the maid of honor’s hand to his lips, kissed it, and swung it to and fro as he lay back in his armchair, looking in another direction.
__label__twp “Attendez,” said Anna Pávlovna, reflecting, “I’ll speak to Lise, young Bolkónski’s wife, this very evening, and perhaps the thing can be arranged.
__label__twp It shall be on your family’s behalf that I’ll start my apprenticeship as old maid.”      CHAPTER II  Anna Pávlovna’s drawing room was gradually filling.
__label__twp The highest Petersburg society was assembled there: people differing widely in age and character but alike in the social circle to which they belonged.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli’s daughter, the beautiful Hélène, came to take her father to the ambassador’s entertainment; she wore a ball dress and her badge as maid of honor.
__label__twp The youthful little Princess Bolkónskaya, known as la femme la plus séduisante de Pétersbourg, * was also there.
__label__twp She had been married during the previous winter, and being pregnant did not go to any large gatherings, but only to small receptions.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli’s son, Hippolyte, had come with Mortemart, whom he introduced.
__label__twp The Abbé Morio and many others had also come.
__label__twp * The most fascinating woman in Petersburg.
__label__twp To each new arrival Anna Pávlovna said, “You have not yet seen my aunt,” or “You do not know my aunt?” and very gravely conducted him or her to a little old lady, wearing large bows of ribbon in her cap, who had come sailing in from another room as soon as the guests began to arrive; and slowly turning her eyes from the visitor to her aunt, Anna Pávlovna mentioned each one’s name and then left them.
__label__twp Each visitor performed the ceremony of greeting this old aunt whom not one of them knew, not one of them wanted to know, and not one of them cared about; Anna Pávlovna observed these greetings with mournful and solemn interest and silent approval.
__label__twp The aunt spoke to each of them in the same words, about their health and her own, and the health of Her Majesty, “who, thank God, was better today.” And each visitor, though politeness prevented his showing impatience, left the old woman with a sense of relief at having performed a vexatious duty and did not return to her the whole evening.
__label__twp The young Princess Bolkónskaya had brought some work in a gold-embroidered velvet bag.
__label__twp Her pretty little upper lip, on which a delicate dark down was just perceptible, was too short for her teeth, but it lifted all the more sweetly, and was especially charming when she occasionally drew it down to meet the lower lip.
__label__twp As is always the case with a thoroughly attractive woman, her defect—the shortness of her upper lip and her half-open mouth—seemed to be her own special and peculiar form of beauty.
__label__twp Everyone brightened at the sight of this pretty young woman, so soon to become a mother, so full of life and health, and carrying her burden so lightly.
__label__twp Old men and dull dispirited young ones who looked at her, after being in her company and talking to her a little while, felt as if they too were becoming, like her, full of life and health.
__label__twp All who talked to her, and at each word saw her bright smile and the constant gleam of her white teeth, thought that they were in a specially amiable mood that day.
__label__twp The little princess went round the table with quick, short, swaying steps, her workbag on her arm, and gaily spreading out her dress sat down on a sofa near the silver samovar, as if all she was doing was a pleasure to herself and to all around her.
__label__twp “I have brought my work,” said she in French, displaying her bag and addressing all present.
__label__twp “Mind, Annette, I hope you have not played a wicked trick on me,” she added, turning to her hostess.
__label__twp “You wrote that it was to be quite a small reception, and just see how badly I am dressed.” And she spread out her arms to show her short-waisted, lace-trimmed, dainty gray dress, girdled with a broad ribbon just below the breast.
__label__twp “Soyez tranquille, Lise, you will always be prettier than anyone else,” replied Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp “You know,” said the princess in the same tone of voice and still in French, turning to a general, “my husband is deserting me? He is going to get himself killed.
__label__twp Tell me what this wretched war is for?” she added, addressing Prince Vasíli, and without waiting for an answer she turned to speak to his daughter, the beautiful Hélène.
__label__twp “What a delightful woman this little princess is!” said Prince Vasíli to Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp One of the next arrivals was a stout, heavily built young man with close-cropped hair, spectacles, the light-colored breeches fashionable at that time, a very high ruffle, and a brown dress coat.
__label__twp This stout young man was an illegitimate son of Count Bezúkhov, a well-known grandee of Catherine’s time who now lay dying in Moscow.
__label__twp The young man had not yet entered either the military or civil service, as he had only just returned from abroad where he had been educated, and this was his first appearance in society.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna greeted him with the nod she accorded to the lowest hierarchy in her drawing room.
__label__twp But in spite of this lowest-grade greeting, a look of anxiety and fear, as at the sight of something too large and unsuited to the place, came over her face when she saw Pierre enter.
__label__twp Though he was certainly rather bigger than the other men in the room, her anxiety could only have reference to the clever though shy, but observant and natural, expression which distinguished him from everyone else in that drawing room.
__label__twp “It is very good of you, Monsieur Pierre, to come and visit a poor invalid,” said Anna Pávlovna, exchanging an alarmed glance with her aunt as she conducted him to her.
__label__twp Pierre murmured something unintelligible, and continued to look round as if in search of something.
__label__twp On his way to the aunt he bowed to the little princess with a pleased smile, as to an intimate acquaintance.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna’s alarm was justified, for Pierre turned away from the aunt without waiting to hear her speech about Her Majesty’s health.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna in dismay detained him with the words: “Do you know the Abbé Morio? He is a most interesting man.”  “Yes, I have heard of his scheme for perpetual peace, and it is very interesting but hardly feasible.”  “You think so?” rejoined Anna Pávlovna in order to say something and get away to attend to her duties as hostess.
__label__twp But Pierre now committed a reverse act of impoliteness.
__label__twp First he had left a lady before she had finished speaking to him, and now he continued to speak to another who wished to get away.
__label__twp With his head bent, and his big feet spread apart, he began explaining his reasons for thinking the abbé’s plan chimerical.
__label__twp “We will talk of it later,” said Anna Pávlovna with a smile.
__label__twp And having got rid of this young man who did not know how to behave, she resumed her duties as hostess and continued to listen and watch, ready to help at any point where the conversation might happen to flag.
__label__twp As the foreman of a spinning mill, when he has set the hands to work, goes round and notices here a spindle that has stopped or there one that creaks or makes more noise than it should, and hastens to check the machine or set it in proper motion, so Anna Pávlovna moved about her drawing room, approaching now a silent, now a too-noisy group, and by a word or slight rearrangement kept the conversational machine in steady, proper, and regular motion.
__label__twp But amid these cares her anxiety about Pierre was evident.
__label__twp She kept an anxious watch on him when he approached the group round Mortemart to listen to what was being said there, and again when he passed to another group whose center was the abbé.
__label__twp Pierre had been educated abroad, and this reception at Anna Pávlovna’s was the first he had attended in Russia.
__label__twp He knew that all the intellectual lights of Petersburg were gathered there and, like a child in a toyshop, did not know which way to look, afraid of missing any clever conversation that was to be heard.
__label__twp Seeing the self-confident and refined expression on the faces of those present he was always expecting to hear something very profound.
__label__twp At last he came up to Morio.
__label__twp Here the conversation seemed interesting and he stood waiting for an opportunity to express his own views, as young people are fond of doing.
__label__twp CHAPTER III  Anna Pávlovna’s reception was in full swing.
__label__twp The spindles hummed steadily and ceaselessly on all sides.
__label__twp With the exception of the aunt, beside whom sat only one elderly lady, who with her thin careworn face was rather out of place in this brilliant society, the whole company had settled into three groups.
__label__twp One, chiefly masculine, had formed round the abbé.
__label__twp Another, of young people, was grouped round the beautiful Princess Hélène, Prince Vasíli’s daughter, and the little Princess Bolkónskaya, very pretty and rosy, though rather too plump for her age.
__label__twp The third group was gathered round Mortemart and Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp The vicomte was a nice-looking young man with soft features and polished manners, who evidently considered himself a celebrity but out of politeness modestly placed himself at the disposal of the circle in which he found himself.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna was obviously serving him up as a treat to her guests.
__label__twp As a clever maître d’hôtel serves up as a specially choice delicacy a piece of meat that no one who had seen it in the kitchen would have cared to eat, so Anna Pávlovna served up to her guests, first the vicomte and then the abbé, as peculiarly choice morsels.
__label__twp The group about Mortemart immediately began discussing the murder of the Duc d’Enghien.
__label__twp The vicomte said that the Duc d’Enghien had perished by his own magnanimity, and that there were particular reasons for Buonaparte’s hatred of him.
__label__twp “Ah, yes! Do tell us all about it, Vicomte,” said Anna Pávlovna, with a pleasant feeling that there was something à la Louis XV in the sound of that sentence: “Contez nous çela, Vicomte.”  The vicomte bowed and smiled courteously in token of his willingness to comply.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna arranged a group round him, inviting everyone to listen to his tale.
__label__twp “The vicomte knew the duc personally,” whispered Anna Pávlovna to one of the guests.
__label__twp “The vicomte is a wonderful raconteur,” said she to another.
__label__twp “How evidently he belongs to the best society,” said she to a third; and the vicomte was served up to the company in the choicest and most advantageous style, like a well-garnished joint of roast beef on a hot dish.
__label__twp The vicomte wished to begin his story and gave a subtle smile.
__label__twp “Come over here, Hélène, dear,” said Anna Pávlovna to the beautiful young princess who was sitting some way off, the center of another group.
__label__twp The princess smiled.
__label__twp She rose with the same unchanging smile with which she had first entered the room—the smile of a perfectly beautiful woman.
__label__twp With a slight rustle of her white dress trimmed with moss and ivy, with a gleam of white shoulders, glossy hair, and sparkling diamonds, she passed between the men who made way for her, not looking at any of them but smiling on all, as if graciously allowing each the privilege of admiring her beautiful figure and shapely shoulders, back, and bosom—which in the fashion of those days were very much exposed—and she seemed to bring the glamour of a ballroom with her as she moved toward Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp Hélène was so lovely that not only did she not show any trace of coquetry, but on the contrary she even appeared shy of her unquestionable and all too victorious beauty.
__label__twp She seemed to wish, but to be unable, to diminish its effect.
__label__twp “How lovely!” said everyone who saw her; and the vicomte lifted his shoulders and dropped his eyes as if startled by something extraordinary when she took her seat opposite and beamed upon him also with her unchanging smile.
__label__twp “Madame, I doubt my ability before such an audience,” said he, smilingly inclining his head.
__label__twp The princess rested her bare round arm on a little table and considered a reply unnecessary.
__label__twp She smilingly waited.
__label__twp All the time the story was being told she sat upright, glancing now at her beautiful round arm, altered in shape by its pressure on the table, now at her still more beautiful bosom, on which she readjusted a diamond necklace.
__label__twp From time to time she smoothed the folds of her dress, and whenever the story produced an effect she glanced at Anna Pávlovna, at once adopted just the expression she saw on the maid of honor’s face, and again relapsed into her radiant smile.
__label__twp The little princess had also left the tea table and followed Hélène.
__label__twp “Wait a moment, I’ll get my work....
__label__twp Now then, what are you thinking of?” she went on, turning to Prince Hippolyte.
__label__twp “Fetch me my workbag.”  There was a general movement as the princess, smiling and talking merrily to everyone at once, sat down and gaily arranged herself in her seat.
__label__twp “Now I am all right,” she said, and asking the vicomte to begin, she took up her work.
__label__twp Prince Hippolyte, having brought the workbag, joined the circle and moving a chair close to hers seated himself beside her.
__label__twp Le charmant Hippolyte was surprising by his extraordinary resemblance to his beautiful sister, but yet more by the fact that in spite of this resemblance he was exceedingly ugly.
__label__twp His features were like his sister’s, but while in her case everything was lit up by a joyous, self-satisfied, youthful, and constant smile of animation, and by the wonderful classic beauty of her figure, his face on the contrary was dulled by imbecility and a constant expression of sullen self-confidence, while his body was thin and weak.
__label__twp His eyes, nose, and mouth all seemed puckered into a vacant, wearied grimace, and his arms and legs always fell into unnatural positions.
__label__twp “It’s not going to be a ghost story?” said he, sitting down beside the princess and hastily adjusting his lorgnette, as if without this instrument he could not begin to speak.
__label__twp “Why no, my dear fellow,” said the astonished narrator, shrugging his shoulders.
__label__twp “Because I hate ghost stories,” said Prince Hippolyte in a tone which showed that he only understood the meaning of his words after he had uttered them.
__label__twp He spoke with such self-confidence that his hearers could not be sure whether what he said was very witty or very stupid.
__label__twp He was dressed in a dark-green dress coat, knee breeches of the color of cuisse de nymphe effrayée, as he called it, shoes, and silk stockings.
__label__twp The vicomte told his tale very neatly.
__label__twp It was an anecdote, then current, to the effect that the Duc d’Enghien had gone secretly to Paris to visit Mademoiselle George; that at her house he came upon Bonaparte, who also enjoyed the famous actress’ favors, and that in his presence Napoleon happened to fall into one of the fainting fits to which he was subject, and was thus at the duc’s mercy.
__label__twp The latter spared him, and this magnanimity Bonaparte subsequently repaid by death.
__label__twp The story was very pretty and interesting, especially at the point where the rivals suddenly recognized one another; and the ladies looked agitated.
__label__twp “Charming!” said Anna Pávlovna with an inquiring glance at the little princess.
__label__twp “Charming!” whispered the little princess, sticking the needle into her work as if to testify that the interest and fascination of the story prevented her from going on with it.
__label__twp The vicomte appreciated this silent praise and smiling gratefully prepared to continue, but just then Anna Pávlovna, who had kept a watchful eye on the young man who so alarmed her, noticed that he was talking too loudly and vehemently with the abbé, so she hurried to the rescue.
__label__twp Pierre had managed to start a conversation with the abbé about the balance of power, and the latter, evidently interested by the young man’s simple-minded eagerness, was explaining his pet theory.
__label__twp Both were talking and listening too eagerly and too naturally, which was why Anna Pávlovna disapproved.
__label__twp “The means are ...
__label__twp the balance of power in Europe and the rights of the people,” the abbé was saying.
__label__twp “It is only necessary for one powerful nation like Russia—barbaric as she is said to be—to place herself disinterestedly at the head of an alliance having for its object the maintenance of the balance of power of Europe, and it would save the world!”  “But how are you to get that balance?” Pierre was beginning.
__label__twp At that moment Anna Pávlovna came up and, looking severely at Pierre, asked the Italian how he stood Russian climate.
__label__twp The Italian’s face instantly changed and assumed an offensively affected, sugary expression, evidently habitual to him when conversing with women.
__label__twp “I am so enchanted by the brilliancy of the wit and culture of the society, more especially of the feminine society, in which I have had the honor of being received, that I have not yet had time to think of the climate,” said he.
__label__twp Not letting the abbé and Pierre escape, Anna Pávlovna, the more conveniently to keep them under observation, brought them into the larger circle.
__label__twp CHAPTER IV  Just then another visitor entered the drawing room: Prince Andrew Bolkónski, the little princess’ husband.
__label__twp He was a very handsome young man, of medium height, with firm, clearcut features.
__label__twp Everything about him, from his weary, bored expression to his quiet, measured step, offered a most striking contrast to his quiet, little wife.
__label__twp It was evident that he not only knew everyone in the drawing room, but had found them to be so tiresome that it wearied him to look at or listen to them.
__label__twp And among all these faces that he found so tedious, none seemed to bore him so much as that of his pretty wife.
__label__twp He turned away from her with a grimace that distorted his handsome face, kissed Anna Pávlovna’s hand, and screwing up his eyes scanned the whole company.
__label__twp “You are off to the war, Prince?” said Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp “General Kutúzov,” said Bolkónski, speaking French and stressing the last syllable of the general’s name like a Frenchman, “has been pleased to take me as an aide-de-camp....”  “And Lise, your wife?”  “She will go to the country.”  “Are you not ashamed to deprive us of your charming wife?”  “André,” said his wife, addressing her husband in the same coquettish manner in which she spoke to other men, “the vicomte has been telling us such a tale about Mademoiselle George and Buonaparte!”  Prince Andrew screwed up his eyes and turned away.
__label__twp Pierre, who from the moment Prince Andrew entered the room had watched him with glad, affectionate eyes, now came up and took his arm.
__label__twp Before he looked round Prince Andrew frowned again, expressing his annoyance with whoever was touching his arm, but when he saw Pierre’s beaming face he gave him an unexpectedly kind and pleasant smile.
__label__twp “There now!...
__label__twp So you, too, are in the great world?” said he to Pierre.
__label__twp “I knew you would be here,” replied Pierre.
__label__twp “I will come to supper with you.
__label__twp May I?” he added in a low voice so as not to disturb the vicomte who was continuing his story.
__label__twp “No, impossible!” said Prince Andrew, laughing and pressing Pierre’s hand to show that there was no need to ask the question.
__label__twp He wished to say something more, but at that moment Prince Vasíli and his daughter got up to go and the two young men rose to let them pass.
__label__twp “You must excuse me, dear Vicomte,” said Prince Vasíli to the Frenchman, holding him down by the sleeve in a friendly way to prevent his rising.
__label__twp “This unfortunate fete at the ambassador’s deprives me of a pleasure, and obliges me to interrupt you.
__label__twp I am very sorry to leave your enchanting party,” said he, turning to Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp His daughter, Princess Hélène, passed between the chairs, lightly holding up the folds of her dress, and the smile shone still more radiantly on her beautiful face.
__label__twp Pierre gazed at her with rapturous, almost frightened, eyes as she passed him.
__label__twp “Very lovely,” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “Very,” said Pierre.
__label__twp In passing Prince Vasíli seized Pierre’s hand and said to Anna Pávlovna: “Educate this bear for me! He has been staying with me a whole month and this is the first time I have seen him in society.
__label__twp Nothing is so necessary for a young man as the society of clever women.”   Anna Pávlovna smiled and promised to take Pierre in hand.
__label__twp She knew his father to be a connection of Prince Vasíli’s.
__label__twp The elderly lady who had been sitting with the old aunt rose hurriedly and overtook Prince Vasíli in the anteroom.
__label__twp All the affectation of interest she had assumed had left her kindly and tear-worn face and it now expressed only anxiety and fear.
__label__twp “How about my son Borís, Prince?” said she, hurrying after him into the anteroom.
__label__twp “I can’t remain any longer in Petersburg.
__label__twp Tell me what news I may take back to my poor boy.”  Although Prince Vasíli listened reluctantly and not very politely to the elderly lady, even betraying some impatience, she gave him an ingratiating and appealing smile, and took his hand that he might not go away.
__label__twp “What would it cost you to say a word to the Emperor, and then he would be transferred to the Guards at once?” said she.
__label__twp “Believe me, Princess, I am ready to do all I can,” answered Prince Vasíli, “but it is difficult for me to ask the Emperor.
__label__twp I should advise you to appeal to Rumyántsev through Prince Golítsyn.
__label__twp That would be the best way.”  The elderly lady was a Princess Drubetskáya, belonging to one of the best families in Russia, but she was poor, and having long been out of society had lost her former influential connections.
__label__twp She had now come to Petersburg to procure an appointment in the Guards for her only son.
__label__twp It was, in fact, solely to meet Prince Vasíli that she had obtained an invitation to Anna Pávlovna’s reception and had sat listening to the vicomte’s story.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli’s words frightened her, an embittered look clouded her once handsome face, but only for a moment; then she smiled again and clutched Prince Vasíli’s arm more tightly.
__label__twp “Listen to me, Prince,” said she.
__label__twp “I have never yet asked you for anything and I never will again, nor have I ever reminded you of my father’s friendship for you; but now I entreat you for God’s sake to do this for my son—and I shall always regard you as a benefactor,” she added hurriedly.
__label__twp “No, don’t be angry, but promise! I have asked Golítsyn and he has refused.
__label__twp Be the kindhearted man you always were,” she said, trying to smile though tears were in her eyes.
__label__twp “Papa, we shall be late,” said Princess Hélène, turning her beautiful head and looking over her classically molded shoulder as she stood waiting by the door.
__label__twp Influence in society, however, is a capital which has to be economized if it is to last.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli knew this, and having once realized that if he asked on behalf of all who begged of him, he would soon be unable to ask for himself, he became chary of using his influence.
__label__twp But in Princess Drubetskáya’s case he felt, after her second appeal, something like qualms of conscience.
__label__twp She had reminded him of what was quite true; he had been indebted to her father for the first steps in his career.
__label__twp Moreover, he could see by her manners that she was one of those women—mostly mothers—who, having once made up their minds, will not rest until they have gained their end, and are prepared if necessary to go on insisting day after day and hour after hour, and even to make scenes.
__label__twp This last consideration moved him.
__label__twp “My dear Anna Mikháylovna,” said he with his usual familiarity and weariness of tone, “it is almost impossible for me to do what you ask; but to prove my devotion to you and how I respect your father’s memory, I will do the impossible—your son shall be transferred to the Guards.
__label__twp Here is my hand on it.
__label__twp Are you satisfied?”  “My dear benefactor! This is what I expected from you—I knew your kindness!” He turned to go.
__label__twp “Wait—just a word! When he has been transferred to the Guards...” she faltered.
__label__twp “You are on good terms with Michael Ilariónovich Kutúzov ...
__label__twp recommend Borís to him as adjutant! Then I shall be at rest, and then...”  Prince Vasíli smiled.
__label__twp “No, I won’t promise that.
__label__twp You don’t know how Kutúzov is pestered since his appointment as Commander in Chief.
__label__twp He told me himself that all the Moscow ladies have conspired to give him all their sons as adjutants.”  “No, but do promise! I won’t let you go! My dear benefactor...”  “Papa,” said his beautiful daughter in the same tone as before, “we shall be late.”  “Well, au revoir! Good-by! You hear her?”  “Then tomorrow you will speak to the Emperor?”  “Certainly; but about Kutúzov, I don’t promise.”  “Do promise, do promise, Vasíli!” cried Anna Mikháylovna as he went, with the smile of a coquettish girl, which at one time probably came naturally to her, but was now very ill-suited to her careworn face.
__label__twp Apparently she had forgotten her age and by force of habit employed all the old feminine arts.
__label__twp But as soon as the prince had gone her face resumed its former cold, artificial expression.
__label__twp She returned to the group where the vicomte was still talking, and again pretended to listen, while waiting till it would be time to leave.
__label__twp Her task was accomplished.
__label__twp “And what do you think of this latest comedy, the coronation at Milan?” asked Anna Pávlovna, “and of the comedy of the people of Genoa and Lucca laying their petitions before Monsieur Buonaparte, and Monsieur Buonaparte sitting on a throne and granting the petitions of the nations? Adorable! It is enough to make one’s head whirl! It is as if the whole world had gone crazy.”  Prince Andrew looked Anna Pávlovna straight in the face with a sarcastic smile.
__label__twp “‘Dieu me la donne, gare à qui la touche!’’ * They say he was very fine when he said that,” he remarked, repeating the words in Italian: “‘Dio mi l’ha dato.
__label__twp Guai a chi la tocchi!’’       * God has given it to me, let him who touches it beware!  “I hope this will prove the last drop that will make the glass run over,” Anna Pávlovna continued.
__label__twp “The sovereigns will not be able to endure this man who is a menace to everything.”  “The sovereigns? I do not speak of Russia,” said the vicomte, polite but hopeless: “The sovereigns, madame...
__label__twp What have they done for Louis XVII, for the Queen, or for Madame Elizabeth? Nothing!” and he became more animated.
__label__twp “And believe me, they are reaping the reward of their betrayal of the Bourbon cause.
__label__twp The sovereigns! Why, they are sending ambassadors to compliment the usurper.”  And sighing disdainfully, he again changed his position.
__label__twp Prince Hippolyte, who had been gazing at the vicomte for some time through his lorgnette, suddenly turned completely round toward the little princess, and having asked for a needle began tracing the Condé coat of arms on the table.
__label__twp He explained this to her with as much gravity as if she had asked him to do it.
__label__twp “Bâton de gueules, engrêlé de gueules d’azur—maison Condé,” said he.
__label__twp The princess listened, smiling.
__label__twp “If Buonaparte remains on the throne of France a year longer,” the vicomte continued, with the air of a man who, in a matter with which he is better acquainted than anyone else, does not listen to others but follows the current of his own thoughts, “things will have gone too far.
__label__twp By intrigues, violence, exile, and executions, French society—I mean good French society—will have been forever destroyed, and then....”  He shrugged his shoulders and spread out his hands.
__label__twp Pierre wished to make a remark, for the conversation interested him, but Anna Pávlovna, who had him under observation, interrupted:  “The Emperor Alexander,” said she, with the melancholy which always accompanied any reference of hers to the Imperial family, “has declared that he will leave it to the French people themselves to choose their own form of government; and I believe that once free from the usurper, the whole nation will certainly throw itself into the arms of its rightful king,” she concluded, trying to be amiable to the royalist emigrant.
__label__twp “That is doubtful,” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “Monsieur le Vicomte quite rightly supposes that matters have already gone too far.
__label__twp I think it will be difficult to return to the old regime.”  “From what I have heard,” said Pierre, blushing and breaking into the conversation, “almost all the aristocracy has already gone over to Bonaparte’s side.”  “It is the Buonapartists who say that,” replied the vicomte without looking at Pierre.
__label__twp “At the present time it is difficult to know the real state of French public opinion.”  “Bonaparte has said so,” remarked Prince Andrew with a sarcastic smile.
__label__twp It was evident that he did not like the vicomte and was aiming his remarks at him, though without looking at him.
__label__twp “‘I showed them the path to glory, but they did not follow it,’” Prince Andrew continued after a short silence, again quoting Napoleon’s words.
__label__twp “‘I opened my antechambers and they crowded in.’ I do not know how far he was justified in saying so.”  “Not in the least,” replied the vicomte.
__label__twp “After the murder of the duc even the most partial ceased to regard him as a hero.
__label__twp If to some people,” he went on, turning to Anna Pávlovna, “he ever was a hero, after the murder of the duc there was one martyr more in heaven and one hero less on earth.”  Before Anna Pávlovna and the others had time to smile their appreciation of the vicomte’s epigram, Pierre again broke into the conversation, and though Anna Pávlovna felt sure he would say something inappropriate, she was unable to stop him.
__label__twp “The execution of the Duc d’Enghien,” declared Monsieur Pierre, “was a political necessity, and it seems to me that Napoleon showed greatness of soul by not fearing to take on himself the whole responsibility of that deed.”  “Dieu! Mon Dieu!” muttered Anna Pávlovna in a terrified whisper.
__label__twp “What, Monsieur Pierre...
__label__twp Do you consider that assassination shows greatness of soul?” said the little princess, smiling and drawing her work nearer to her.
__label__twp “Oh! Oh!” exclaimed several voices.
__label__twp “Capital!” said Prince Hippolyte in English, and began slapping his knee with the palm of his hand.
__label__twp The vicomte merely shrugged his shoulders.
__label__twp Pierre looked solemnly at his audience over his spectacles and continued.
__label__twp “I say so,” he continued desperately, “because the Bourbons fled from the Revolution leaving the people to anarchy, and Napoleon alone understood the Revolution and quelled it, and so for the general good, he could not stop short for the sake of one man’s life.”  “Won’t you come over to the other table?” suggested Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp But Pierre continued his speech without heeding her.
__label__twp “No,” cried he, becoming more and more eager, “Napoleon is great because he rose superior to the Revolution, suppressed its abuses, preserved all that was good in it—equality of citizenship and freedom of speech and of the press—and only for that reason did he obtain power.”  “Yes, if having obtained power, without availing himself of it to commit murder he had restored it to the rightful king, I should have called him a great man,” remarked the vicomte.
__label__twp “He could not do that.
__label__twp The people only gave him power that he might rid them of the Bourbons and because they saw that he was a great man.
__label__twp The Revolution was a grand thing!” continued Monsieur Pierre, betraying by this desperate and provocative proposition his extreme youth and his wish to express all that was in his mind.
__label__twp “What? Revolution and regicide a grand thing?...
__label__twp Well, after that...
__label__twp But won’t you come to this other table?” repeated Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp “Rousseau’s Contrat Social,” said the vicomte with a tolerant smile.
__label__twp “I am not speaking of regicide, I am speaking about ideas.”  “Yes: ideas of robbery, murder, and regicide,” again interjected an ironical voice.
__label__twp “Those were extremes, no doubt, but they are not what is most important.
__label__twp What is important are the rights of man, emancipation from prejudices, and equality of citizenship, and all these ideas Napoleon has retained in full force.”  “Liberty and equality,” said the vicomte contemptuously, as if at last deciding seriously to prove to this youth how foolish his words were, “high-sounding words which have long been discredited.
__label__twp Who does not love liberty and equality? Even our Saviour preached liberty and equality.
__label__twp Have people since the Revolution become happier? On the contrary.
__label__twp We wanted liberty, but Buonaparte has destroyed it.”  Prince Andrew kept looking with an amused smile from Pierre to the vicomte and from the vicomte to their hostess.
__label__twp In the first moment of Pierre’s outburst Anna Pávlovna, despite her social experience, was horror-struck.
__label__twp But when she saw that Pierre’s sacrilegious words had not exasperated the vicomte, and had convinced herself that it was impossible to stop him, she rallied her forces and joined the vicomte in a vigorous attack on the orator.
__label__twp “But, my dear Monsieur Pierre,” said she, “how do you explain the fact of a great man executing a duc—or even an ordinary man who—is innocent and untried?”  “I should like,” said the vicomte, “to ask how monsieur explains the 18th Brumaire; was not that an imposture? It was a swindle, and not at all like the conduct of a great man!”  “And the prisoners he killed in Africa? That was horrible!” said the little princess, shrugging her shoulders.
__label__twp “He’s a low fellow, say what you will,” remarked Prince Hippolyte.
__label__twp Pierre, not knowing whom to answer, looked at them all and smiled.
__label__twp His smile was unlike the half-smile of other people.
__label__twp When he smiled, his grave, even rather gloomy, look was instantaneously replaced by another—a childlike, kindly, even rather silly look, which seemed to ask forgiveness.
__label__twp The vicomte who was meeting him for the first time saw clearly that this young Jacobin was not so terrible as his words suggested.
__label__twp All were silent.
__label__twp “How do you expect him to answer you all at once?” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “Besides, in the actions of a statesman one has to distinguish between his acts as a private person, as a general, and as an emperor.
__label__twp So it seems to me.”  “Yes, yes, of course!” Pierre chimed in, pleased at the arrival of this reinforcement.
__label__twp “One must admit,” continued Prince Andrew, “that Napoleon as a man was great on the bridge of Arcola, and in the hospital at Jaffa where he gave his hand to the plague-stricken; but ...
__label__twp but there are other acts which it is difficult to justify.”  Prince Andrew, who had evidently wished to tone down the awkwardness of Pierre’s remarks, rose and made a sign to his wife that it was time to go.
__label__twp Suddenly Prince Hippolyte started up making signs to everyone to attend, and asking them all to be seated began:  “I was told a charming Moscow story today and must treat you to it.
__label__twp Excuse me, Vicomte—I must tell it in Russian or the point will be lost....” And Prince Hippolyte began to tell his story in such Russian as a Frenchman would speak after spending about a year in Russia.
__label__twp Everyone waited, so emphatically and eagerly did he demand their attention to his story.
__label__twp “There is in Moscow a lady, une dame, and she is very stingy.
__label__twp She must have two footmen behind her carriage, and very big ones.
__label__twp That was her taste.
__label__twp And she had a lady’s maid, also big.
__label__twp She said....”  Here Prince Hippolyte paused, evidently collecting his ideas with difficulty.
__label__twp “She said....
__label__twp Oh yes! She said, ‘Girl,’ to the maid, ‘put on a livery, get up behind the carriage, and come with me while I make some calls.’”  Here Prince Hippolyte spluttered and burst out laughing long before his audience, which produced an effect unfavorable to the narrator.
__label__twp Several persons, among them the elderly lady and Anna Pávlovna, did however smile.
__label__twp “She went.
__label__twp Suddenly there was a great wind.
__label__twp The girl lost her hat and her long hair came down....” Here he could contain himself no longer and went on, between gasps of laughter: “And the whole world knew....”  And so the anecdote ended.
__label__twp Though it was unintelligible why he had told it, or why it had to be told in Russian, still Anna Pávlovna and the others appreciated Prince Hippolyte’s social tact in so agreeably ending Pierre’s unpleasant and unamiable outburst.
__label__twp After the anecdote the conversation broke up into insignificant small talk about the last and next balls, about theatricals, and who would meet whom, and when and where.
__label__twp CHAPTER VI  Having thanked Anna Pávlovna for her charming soiree, the guests began to take their leave.
__label__twp Pierre was ungainly.
__label__twp Stout, about the average height, broad, with huge red hands; he did not know, as the saying is, how to enter a drawing room and still less how to leave one; that is, how to say something particularly agreeable before going away.
__label__twp Besides this he was absent-minded.
__label__twp When he rose to go, he took up instead of his own, the general’s three-cornered hat, and held it, pulling at the plume, till the general asked him to restore it.
__label__twp All his absent-mindedness and inability to enter a room and converse in it was, however, redeemed by his kindly, simple, and modest expression.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna turned toward him and, with a Christian mildness that expressed forgiveness of his indiscretion, nodded and said: “I hope to see you again, but I also hope you will change your opinions, my dear Monsieur Pierre.”  When she said this, he did not reply and only bowed, but again everybody saw his smile, which said nothing, unless perhaps, “Opinions are opinions, but you see what a capital, good-natured fellow I am.” And everyone, including Anna Pávlovna, felt this.
__label__twp Prince Andrew had gone out into the hall, and, turning his shoulders to the footman who was helping him on with his cloak, listened indifferently to his wife’s chatter with Prince Hippolyte who had also come into the hall.
__label__twp Prince Hippolyte stood close to the pretty, pregnant princess, and stared fixedly at her through his eyeglass.
__label__twp “Go in, Annette, or you will catch cold,” said the little princess, taking leave of Anna Pávlovna.
__label__twp “It is settled,” she added in a low voice.
__label__twp Anna Pávlovna had already managed to speak to Lise about the match she contemplated between Anatole and the little princess’ sister-in-law.
__label__twp “I rely on you, my dear,” said Anna Pávlovna, also in a low tone.
__label__twp “Write to her and let me know how her father looks at the matter.
__label__twp Au revoir! ”—and she left the hall.
__label__twp Prince Hippolyte approached the little princess and, bending his face close to her, began to whisper something.
__label__twp Two footmen, the princess’ and his own, stood holding a shawl and a cloak, waiting for the conversation to finish.
__label__twp They listened to the French sentences which to them were meaningless, with an air of understanding but not wishing to appear to do so.
__label__twp The princess as usual spoke smilingly and listened with a laugh.
__label__twp “I am very glad I did not go to the ambassador’s,” said Prince Hippolyte “—so dull—.
__label__twp It has been a delightful evening, has it not? Delightful!”  “They say the ball will be very good,” replied the princess, drawing up her downy little lip.
__label__twp “All the pretty women in society will be there.”  “Not all, for you will not be there; not all,” said Prince Hippolyte smiling joyfully; and snatching the shawl from the footman, whom he even pushed aside, he began wrapping it round the princess.
__label__twp Either from awkwardness or intentionally (no one could have said which) after the shawl had been adjusted he kept his arm around her for a long time, as though embracing her.
__label__twp Still smiling, she gracefully moved away, turning and glancing at her husband.
__label__twp Prince Andrew’s eyes were closed, so weary and sleepy did he seem.
__label__twp “Are you ready?” he asked his wife, looking past her.
__label__twp Prince Hippolyte hurriedly put on his cloak, which in the latest fashion reached to his very heels, and, stumbling in it, ran out into the porch following the princess, whom a footman was helping into the carriage.
__label__twp “Princesse, au revoir,” cried he, stumbling with his tongue as well as with his feet.
__label__twp The princess, picking up her dress, was taking her seat in the dark carriage, her husband was adjusting his saber; Prince Hippolyte, under pretense of helping, was in everyone’s way.
__label__twp “Allow me, sir,” said Prince Andrew in Russian in a cold, disagreeable tone to Prince Hippolyte who was blocking his path.
__label__twp “I am expecting you, Pierre,” said the same voice, but gently and affectionately.
__label__twp The postilion started, the carriage wheels rattled.
__label__twp Prince Hippolyte laughed spasmodically as he stood in the porch waiting for the vicomte whom he had promised to take home.
__label__twp “Well, mon cher,” said the vicomte, having seated himself beside Hippolyte in the carriage, “your little princess is very nice, very nice indeed, quite French,” and he kissed the tips of his fingers.
__label__twp Hippolyte burst out laughing.
__label__twp “Do you know, you are a terrible chap for all your innocent airs,” continued the vicomte.
__label__twp “I pity the poor husband, that little officer who gives himself the airs of a monarch.”  Hippolyte spluttered again, and amid his laughter said, “And you were saying that the Russian ladies are not equal to the French? One has to know how to deal with them.”  Pierre reaching the house first went into Prince Andrew’s study like one quite at home, and from habit immediately lay down on the sofa, took from the shelf the first book that came to his hand (it was Caesar’s Commentaries), and resting on his elbow, began reading it in the middle.
__label__twp “What have you done to Mlle Schérer? She will be quite ill now,” said Prince Andrew, as he entered the study, rubbing his small white hands.
__label__twp Pierre turned his whole body, making the sofa creak.
__label__twp He lifted his eager face to Prince Andrew, smiled, and waved his hand.
__label__twp “That abbé is very interesting but he does not see the thing in the right light....
__label__twp In my opinion perpetual peace is possible but—I do not know how to express it ...
__label__twp not by a balance of political power....”  It was evident that Prince Andrew was not interested in such abstract conversation.
__label__twp “One can’t everywhere say all one thinks, mon cher.
__label__twp Well, have you at last decided on anything? Are you going to be a guardsman or a diplomatist?” asked Prince Andrew after a momentary silence.
__label__twp Pierre sat up on the sofa, with his legs tucked under him.
__label__twp “Really, I don’t yet know.
__label__twp I don’t like either the one or the other.”  “But you must decide on something! Your father expects it.”  Pierre at the age of ten had been sent abroad with an abbé as tutor, and had remained away till he was twenty.
__label__twp When he returned to Moscow his father dismissed the abbé and said to the young man, “Now go to Petersburg, look round, and choose your profession.
__label__twp I will agree to anything.
__label__twp Here is a letter to Prince Vasíli, and here is money.
__label__twp Write to me all about it, and I will help you in everything.” Pierre had already been choosing a career for three months, and had not decided on anything.
__label__twp It was about this choice that Prince Andrew was speaking.
__label__twp Pierre rubbed his forehead.
__label__twp “But he must be a Freemason,” said he, referring to the abbé whom he had met that evening.
__label__twp “That is all nonsense.” Prince Andrew again interrupted him, “let us talk business.
__label__twp Have you been to the Horse Guards?”  “No, I have not; but this is what I have been thinking and wanted to tell you.
__label__twp There is a war now against Napoleon.
__label__twp If it were a war for freedom I could understand it and should be the first to enter the army; but to help England and Austria against the greatest man in the world is not right.”  Prince Andrew only shrugged his shoulders at Pierre’s childish words.
__label__twp He put on the air of one who finds it impossible to reply to such nonsense, but it would in fact have been difficult to give any other answer than the one Prince Andrew gave to this naïve question.
__label__twp “If no one fought except on his own conviction, there would be no wars,” he said.
__label__twp “And that would be splendid,” said Pierre.
__label__twp Prince Andrew smiled ironically.
__label__twp “Very likely it would be splendid, but it will never come about....”  “Well, why are you going to the war?” asked Pierre.
__label__twp “What for? I don’t know.
__label__twp I must.
__label__twp Besides that I am going....” He paused.
__label__twp “I am going because the life I am leading here does not suit me!”      CHAPTER VII  The rustle of a woman’s dress was heard in the next room.
__label__twp Prince Andrew shook himself as if waking up, and his face assumed the look it had had in Anna Pávlovna’s drawing room.
__label__twp Pierre removed his feet from the sofa.
__label__twp The princess came in.
__label__twp She had changed her gown for a house dress as fresh and elegant as the other.
__label__twp Prince Andrew rose and politely placed a chair for her.
__label__twp “How is it,” she began, as usual in French, settling down briskly and fussily in the easy chair, “how is it Annette never got married? How stupid you men all are not to have married her! Excuse me for saying so, but you have no sense about women.
__label__twp What an argumentative fellow you are, Monsieur Pierre!”  “And I am still arguing with your husband.
__label__twp I can’t understand why he wants to go to the war,” replied Pierre, addressing the princess with none of the embarrassment so commonly shown by young men in their intercourse with young women.
__label__twp The princess started.
__label__twp Evidently Pierre’s words touched her to the quick.
__label__twp “Ah, that is just what I tell him!” said she.
__label__twp “I don’t understand it; I don’t in the least understand why men can’t live without wars.
__label__twp How is it that we women don’t want anything of the kind, don’t need it? Now you shall judge between us.
__label__twp I always tell him: Here he is Uncle’s aide-de-camp, a most brilliant position.
__label__twp He is so well known, so much appreciated by everyone.
__label__twp The other day at the Apráksins’ I heard a lady asking, ‘Is that the famous Prince Andrew?’ I did indeed.” She laughed.
__label__twp “He is so well received everywhere.
__label__twp He might easily become aide-de-camp to the Emperor.
__label__twp You know the Emperor spoke to him most graciously.
__label__twp Annette and I were speaking of how to arrange it.
__label__twp What do you think?”  Pierre looked at his friend and, noticing that he did not like the conversation, gave no reply.
__label__twp “When are you starting?” he asked.
__label__twp “Oh, don’t speak of his going, don’t! I won’t hear it spoken of,” said the princess in the same petulantly playful tone in which she had spoken to Hippolyte in the drawing room and which was so plainly ill-suited to the family circle of which Pierre was almost a member.
__label__twp “Today when I remembered that all these delightful associations must be broken off ...
__label__twp and then you know, André...” (she looked significantly at her husband) “I’m afraid, I’m afraid!” she whispered, and a shudder ran down her back.
__label__twp Her husband looked at her as if surprised to notice that someone besides Pierre and himself was in the room, and addressed her in a tone of frigid politeness.
__label__twp “What is it you are afraid of, Lise? I don’t understand,” said he.
__label__twp “There, what egotists men all are: all, all egotists! Just for a whim of his own, goodness only knows why, he leaves me and locks me up alone in the country.”  “With my father and sister, remember,” said Prince Andrew gently.
__label__twp “Alone all the same, without my friends....
__label__twp And he expects me not to be afraid.”  Her tone was now querulous and her lip drawn up, giving her not a joyful, but an animal, squirrel-like expression.
__label__twp She paused as if she felt it indecorous to speak of her pregnancy before Pierre, though the gist of the matter lay in that.
__label__twp “I still can’t understand what you are afraid of,” said Prince Andrew slowly, not taking his eyes off his wife.
__label__twp The princess blushed, and raised her arms with a gesture of despair.
__label__twp “No, Andrew, I must say you have changed.
__label__twp Oh, how you have....”  “Your doctor tells you to go to bed earlier,” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “You had better go.”  The princess said nothing, but suddenly her short downy lip quivered.
__label__twp Prince Andrew rose, shrugged his shoulders, and walked about the room.
__label__twp Pierre looked over his spectacles with naïve surprise, now at him and now at her, moved as if about to rise too, but changed his mind.
__label__twp “Why should I mind Monsieur Pierre being here?” exclaimed the little princess suddenly, her pretty face all at once distorted by a tearful grimace.
__label__twp “I have long wanted to ask you, Andrew, why you have changed so to me? What have I done to you? You are going to the war and have no pity for me.
__label__twp Why is it?”  “Lise!” was all Prince Andrew said.
__label__twp But that one word expressed an entreaty, a threat, and above all conviction that she would herself regret her words.
__label__twp But she went on hurriedly:  “You treat me like an invalid or a child.
__label__twp I see it all! Did you behave like that six months ago?”  “Lise, I beg you to desist,” said Prince Andrew still more emphatically.
__label__twp Pierre, who had been growing more and more agitated as he listened to all this, rose and approached the princess.
__label__twp He seemed unable to bear the sight of tears and was ready to cry himself.
__label__twp “Calm yourself, Princess! It seems so to you because....
__label__twp I assure you I myself have experienced ...
__label__twp and so ...
__label__twp because ...
__label__twp No, excuse me! An outsider is out of place here....
__label__twp No, don’t distress yourself....
__label__twp Good-by!”  Prince Andrew caught him by the hand.
__label__twp “No, wait, Pierre! The princess is too kind to wish to deprive me of the pleasure of spending the evening with you.”  “No, he thinks only of himself,” muttered the princess without restraining her angry tears.
__label__twp “Lise!” said Prince Andrew dryly, raising his voice to the pitch which indicates that patience is exhausted.
__label__twp Suddenly the angry, squirrel-like expression of the princess’ pretty face changed into a winning and piteous look of fear.
__label__twp Her beautiful eyes glanced askance at her husband’s face, and her own assumed the timid, deprecating expression of a dog when it rapidly but feebly wags its drooping tail.
__label__twp “Mon Dieu, mon Dieu!” she muttered, and lifting her dress with one hand she went up to her husband and kissed him on the forehead.
__label__twp “Good night, Lise,” said he, rising and courteously kissing her hand as he would have done to a stranger.
__label__twp CHAPTER VIII  The friends were silent.
__label__twp Neither cared to begin talking.
__label__twp Pierre continually glanced at Prince Andrew; Prince Andrew rubbed his forehead with his small hand.
__label__twp “Let us go and have supper,” he said with a sigh, going to the door.
__label__twp They entered the elegant, newly decorated, and luxurious dining room.
__label__twp Everything from the table napkins to the silver, china, and glass bore that imprint of newness found in the households of the newly married.
__label__twp Halfway through supper Prince Andrew leaned his elbows on the table and, with a look of nervous agitation such as Pierre had never before seen on his face, began to talk—as one who has long had something on his mind and suddenly determines to speak out.
__label__twp “Never, never marry, my dear fellow! That’s my advice: never marry till you can say to yourself that you have done all you are capable of, and until you have ceased to love the woman of your choice and have seen her plainly as she is, or else you will make a cruel and irrevocable mistake.
__label__twp Marry when you are old and good for nothing—or all that is good and noble in you will be lost.
__label__twp It will all be wasted on trifles.
__label__twp Yes! Yes! Yes! Don’t look at me with such surprise.
__label__twp If you marry expecting anything from yourself in the future, you will feel at every step that for you all is ended, all is closed except the drawing room, where you will be ranged side by side with a court lackey and an idiot!...
__label__twp But what’s the good?...” and he waved his arm.
__label__twp Pierre took off his spectacles, which made his face seem different and the good-natured expression still more apparent, and gazed at his friend in amazement.
__label__twp “My wife,” continued Prince Andrew, “is an excellent woman, one of those rare women with whom a man’s honor is safe; but, O God, what would I not give now to be unmarried! You are the first and only one to whom I mention this, because I like you.”  As he said this Prince Andrew was less than ever like that Bolkónski who had lolled in Anna Pávlovna’s easy chairs and with half-closed eyes had uttered French phrases between his teeth.
__label__twp Every muscle of his thin face was now quivering with nervous excitement; his eyes, in which the fire of life had seemed extinguished, now flashed with brilliant light.
__label__twp It was evident that the more lifeless he seemed at ordinary times, the more impassioned he became in these moments of almost morbid irritation.
__label__twp “You don’t understand why I say this,” he continued, “but it is the whole story of life.
__label__twp You talk of Bonaparte and his career,” said he (though Pierre had not mentioned Bonaparte), “but Bonaparte when he worked went step by step toward his goal.
__label__twp He was free, he had nothing but his aim to consider, and he reached it.
__label__twp But tie yourself up with a woman and, like a chained convict, you lose all freedom! And all you have of hope and strength merely weighs you down and torments you with regret.
__label__twp Drawing rooms, gossip, balls, vanity, and triviality—these are the enchanted circle I cannot escape from.
__label__twp I am now going to the war, the greatest war there ever was, and I know nothing and am fit for nothing.
__label__twp I am very amiable and have a caustic wit,” continued Prince Andrew, “and at Anna Pávlovna’s they listen to me.
__label__twp And that stupid set without whom my wife cannot exist, and those women....
__label__twp If you only knew what those society women are, and women in general! My father is right.
__label__twp Selfish, vain, stupid, trivial in everything—that’s what women are when you see them in their true colors! When you meet them in society it seems as if there were something in them, but there’s nothing, nothing, nothing! No, don’t marry, my dear fellow; don’t marry!” concluded Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “It seems funny to me,” said Pierre, “that you, you should consider yourself incapable and your life a spoiled life.
__label__twp You have everything before you, everything.
__label__twp And you....”  He did not finish his sentence, but his tone showed how highly he thought of his friend and how much he expected of him in the future.
__label__twp “How can he talk like that?” thought Pierre.
__label__twp He considered his friend a model of perfection because Prince Andrew possessed in the highest degree just the very qualities Pierre lacked, and which might be best described as strength of will.
__label__twp Pierre was always astonished at Prince Andrew’s calm manner of treating everybody, his extraordinary memory, his extensive reading (he had read everything, knew everything, and had an opinion about everything), but above all at his capacity for work and study.
__label__twp And if Pierre was often struck by Andrew’s lack of capacity for philosophical meditation (to which he himself was particularly addicted), he regarded even this not as a defect but as a sign of strength.
__label__twp Even in the best, most friendly and simplest relations of life, praise and commendation are essential, just as grease is necessary to wheels that they may run smoothly.
__label__twp “My part is played out,” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “What’s the use of talking about me? Let us talk about you,” he added after a silence, smiling at his reassuring thoughts.
__label__twp That smile was immediately reflected on Pierre’s face.
__label__twp “But what is there to say about me?” said Pierre, his face relaxing into a careless, merry smile.
__label__twp “What am I? An illegitimate son!” He suddenly blushed crimson, and it was plain that he had made a great effort to say this.
__label__twp “Without a name and without means...
__label__twp And it really...” But he did not say what “it really” was.
__label__twp “For the present I am free and am all right.
__label__twp Only I haven’t the least idea what I am to do; I wanted to consult you seriously.”  Prince Andrew looked kindly at him, yet his glance—friendly and affectionate as it was—expressed a sense of his own superiority.
__label__twp “I am fond of you, especially as you are the one live man among our whole set.
__label__twp Yes, you’re all right! Choose what you will; it’s all the same.
__label__twp You’ll be all right anywhere.
__label__twp But look here: give up visiting those Kurágins and leading that sort of life.
__label__twp It suits you so badly—all this debauchery, dissipation, and the rest of it!”  “What would you have, my dear fellow?” answered Pierre, shrugging his shoulders.
__label__twp “Women, my dear fellow; women!”  “I don’t understand it,” replied Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “Women who are comme il faut, that’s a different matter; but the Kurágins’ set of women, ‘women and wine’ I don’t understand!”  Pierre was staying at Prince Vasíli Kurágin’s and sharing the dissipated life of his son Anatole, the son whom they were planning to reform by marrying him to Prince Andrew’s sister.
__label__twp “Do you know?” said Pierre, as if suddenly struck by a happy thought, “seriously, I have long been thinking of it....
__label__twp Leading such a life I can’t decide or think properly about anything.
__label__twp One’s head aches, and one spends all one’s money.
__label__twp He asked me for tonight, but I won’t go.”  “You give me your word of honor not to go?”  “On my honor!”      CHAPTER IX  It was past one o’clock when Pierre left his friend.
__label__twp It was a cloudless, northern, summer night.
__label__twp Pierre took an open cab intending to drive straight home.
__label__twp But the nearer he drew to the house the more he felt the impossibility of going to sleep on such a night.
__label__twp It was light enough to see a long way in the deserted street and it seemed more like morning or evening than night.
__label__twp On the way Pierre remembered that Anatole Kurágin was expecting the usual set for cards that evening, after which there was generally a drinking bout, finishing with visits of a kind Pierre was very fond of.
__label__twp “I should like to go to Kurágin’s,” thought he.
__label__twp But he immediately recalled his promise to Prince Andrew not to go there.
__label__twp Then, as happens to people of weak character, he desired so passionately once more to enjoy that dissipation he was so accustomed to that he decided to go.
__label__twp The thought immediately occurred to him that his promise to Prince Andrew was of no account, because before he gave it he had already promised Prince Anatole to come to his gathering; “besides,” thought he, “all such ‘words of honor’ are conventional things with no definite meaning, especially if one considers that by tomorrow one may be dead, or something so extraordinary may happen to one that honor and dishonor will be all the same!” Pierre often indulged in reflections of this sort, nullifying all his decisions and intentions.
__label__twp He went to Kurágin’s.
__label__twp Reaching the large house near the Horse Guards’ barracks, in which Anatole lived, Pierre entered the lighted porch, ascended the stairs, and went in at the open door.
__label__twp There was no one in the anteroom; empty bottles, cloaks, and overshoes were lying about; there was a smell of alcohol, and sounds of voices and shouting in the distance.
__label__twp Cards and supper were over, but the visitors had not yet dispersed.
__label__twp Pierre threw off his cloak and entered the first room, in which were the remains of supper.
__label__twp A footman, thinking no one saw him, was drinking on the sly what was left in the glasses.
__label__twp From the third room came sounds of laughter, the shouting of familiar voices, the growling of a bear, and general commotion.
__label__twp Some eight or nine young men were crowding anxiously round an open window.
__label__twp Three others were romping with a young bear, one pulling him by the chain and trying to set him at the others.
__label__twp “I bet a hundred on Stevens!” shouted one.
__label__twp “Mind, no holding on!” cried another.
__label__twp “I bet on Dólokhov!” cried a third.
__label__twp “Kurágin, you part our hands.”  “There, leave Bruin alone; here’s a bet on.”  “At one draught, or he loses!” shouted a fourth.
__label__twp “Jacob, bring a bottle!” shouted the host, a tall, handsome fellow who stood in the midst of the group, without a coat, and with his fine linen shirt unfastened in front.
__label__twp “Wait a bit, you fellows....
__label__twp Here is Pétya! Good man!” cried he, addressing Pierre.
__label__twp Another voice, from a man of medium height with clear blue eyes, particularly striking among all these drunken voices by its sober ring, cried from the window: “Come here; part the bets!” This was Dólokhov, an officer of the Semënov regiment, a notorious gambler and duelist, who was living with Anatole.
__label__twp Pierre smiled, looking about him merrily.
__label__twp “I don’t understand.
__label__twp What’s it all about?”  “Wait a bit, he is not drunk yet! A bottle here,” said Anatole, and taking a glass from the table he went up to Pierre.
__label__twp “First of all you must drink!”  Pierre drank one glass after another, looking from under his brows at the tipsy guests who were again crowding round the window, and listening to their chatter.
__label__twp Anatole kept on refilling Pierre’s glass while explaining that Dólokhov was betting with Stevens, an English naval officer, that he would drink a bottle of rum sitting on the outer ledge of the third floor window with his legs hanging out.
__label__twp “Go on, you must drink it all,” said Anatole, giving Pierre the last glass, “or I won’t let you go!”  “No, I won’t,” said Pierre, pushing Anatole aside, and he went up to the window.
__label__twp Dólokhov was holding the Englishman’s hand and clearly and distinctly repeating the terms of the bet, addressing himself particularly to Anatole and Pierre.
__label__twp Dólokhov was of medium height, with curly hair and light-blue eyes.
__label__twp He was about twenty-five.
__label__twp Like all infantry officers he wore no mustache, so that his mouth, the most striking feature of his face, was clearly seen.
__label__twp The lines of that mouth were remarkably finely curved.
__label__twp The middle of the upper lip formed a sharp wedge and closed firmly on the firm lower one, and something like two distinct smiles played continually round the two corners of the mouth; this, together with the resolute, insolent intelligence of his eyes, produced an effect which made it impossible not to notice his face.
__label__twp Dólokhov was a man of small means and no connections.
__label__twp Yet, though Anatole spent tens of thousands of rubles, Dólokhov lived with him and had placed himself on such a footing that all who knew them, including Anatole himself, respected him more than they did Anatole.
__label__twp Dólokhov could play all games and nearly always won.
__label__twp However much he drank, he never lost his clearheadedness.
__label__twp Both Kurágin and Dólokhov were at that time notorious among the rakes and scapegraces of Petersburg.
__label__twp The bottle of rum was brought.
__label__twp The window frame which prevented anyone from sitting on the outer sill was being forced out by two footmen, who were evidently flurried and intimidated by the directions and shouts of the gentlemen around.
__label__twp Anatole with his swaggering air strode up to the window.
__label__twp He wanted to smash something.
__label__twp Pushing away the footmen he tugged at the frame, but could not move it.
__label__twp He smashed a pane.
__label__twp “You have a try, Hercules,” said he, turning to Pierre.
__label__twp Pierre seized the crossbeam, tugged, and wrenched the oak frame out with a crash.
__label__twp “Take it right out, or they’ll think I’m holding on,” said Dólokhov.
__label__twp “Is the Englishman bragging?...
__label__twp Eh? Is it all right?” said Anatole.
__label__twp “First-rate,” said Pierre, looking at Dólokhov, who with a bottle of rum in his hand was approaching the window, from which the light of the sky, the dawn merging with the afterglow of sunset, was visible.
__label__twp Dólokhov, the bottle of rum still in his hand, jumped onto the window sill.
__label__twp “Listen!” cried he, standing there and addressing those in the room.
__label__twp All were silent.
__label__twp “I bet fifty imperials”—he spoke French that the Englishman might understand him, but he did not speak it very well—“I bet fifty imperials ...
__label__twp or do you wish to make it a hundred?” added he, addressing the Englishman.
__label__twp “No, fifty,” replied the latter.
__label__twp “All right.
__label__twp Fifty imperials ...
__label__twp that I will drink a whole bottle of rum without taking it from my mouth, sitting outside the window on this spot” (he stooped and pointed to the sloping ledge outside the window) “and without holding on to anything.
__label__twp Is that right?”  “Quite right,” said the Englishman.
__label__twp Anatole turned to the Englishman and taking him by one of the buttons of his coat and looking down at him—the Englishman was short—began repeating the terms of the wager to him in English.
__label__twp “Wait!” cried Dólokhov, hammering with the bottle on the window sill to attract attention.
__label__twp “Wait a bit, Kurágin.
__label__twp Listen! If anyone else does the same, I will pay him a hundred imperials.
__label__twp Do you understand?”  The Englishman nodded, but gave no indication whether he intended to accept this challenge or not.
__label__twp Anatole did not release him, and though he kept nodding to show that he understood, Anatole went on translating Dólokhov’s words into English.
__label__twp A thin young lad, an hussar of the Life Guards, who had been losing that evening, climbed on the window sill, leaned over, and looked down.
__label__twp “Oh! Oh! Oh!” he muttered, looking down from the window at the stones of the pavement.
__label__twp “Shut up!” cried Dólokhov, pushing him away from the window.
__label__twp The lad jumped awkwardly back into the room, tripping over his spurs.
__label__twp Placing the bottle on the window sill where he could reach it easily, Dólokhov climbed carefully and slowly through the window and lowered his legs.
__label__twp Pressing against both sides of the window, he adjusted himself on his seat, lowered his hands, moved a little to the right and then to the left, and took up the bottle.
__label__twp Anatole brought two candles and placed them on the window sill, though it was already quite light.
__label__twp Dólokhov’s back in his white shirt, and his curly head, were lit up from both sides.
__label__twp Everyone crowded to the window, the Englishman in front.
__label__twp Pierre stood smiling but silent.
__label__twp One man, older than the others present, suddenly pushed forward with a scared and angry look and wanted to seize hold of Dólokhov’s shirt.
__label__twp “I say, this is folly! He’ll be killed,” said this more sensible man.
__label__twp Anatole stopped him.
__label__twp “Don’t touch him! You’ll startle him and then he’ll be killed.
__label__twp Eh?...
__label__twp What then?...
__label__twp Eh?”  Dólokhov turned round and, again holding on with both hands, arranged himself on his seat.
__label__twp “If anyone comes meddling again,” said he, emitting the words separately through his thin compressed lips, “I will throw him down there.
__label__twp Now then!”  Saying this he again turned round, dropped his hands, took the bottle and lifted it to his lips, threw back his head, and raised his free hand to balance himself.
