__label__twp One of the footmen who had stooped to pick up some broken glass remained in that position without taking his eyes from the window and from Dólokhov’s back.
__label__twp Anatole stood erect with staring eyes.
__label__twp The Englishman looked on sideways, pursing up his lips.
__label__twp The man who had wished to stop the affair ran to a corner of the room and threw himself on a sofa with his face to the wall.
__label__twp Pierre hid his face, from which a faint smile forgot to fade though his features now expressed horror and fear.
__label__twp All were still.
__label__twp Pierre took his hands from his eyes.
__label__twp Dólokhov still sat in the same position, only his head was thrown further back till his curly hair touched his shirt collar, and the hand holding the bottle was lifted higher and higher and trembled with the effort.
__label__twp The bottle was emptying perceptibly and rising still higher and his head tilting yet further back.
__label__twp “Why is it so long?” thought Pierre.
__label__twp It seemed to him that more than half an hour had elapsed.
__label__twp Suddenly Dólokhov made a backward movement with his spine, and his arm trembled nervously; this was sufficient to cause his whole body to slip as he sat on the sloping ledge.
__label__twp As he began slipping down, his head and arm wavered still more with the strain.
__label__twp One hand moved as if to clutch the window sill, but refrained from touching it.
__label__twp Pierre again covered his eyes and thought he would never open them again.
__label__twp Suddenly he was aware of a stir all around.
__label__twp He looked up: Dólokhov was standing on the window sill, with a pale but radiant face.
__label__twp “It’s empty.”  He threw the bottle to the Englishman, who caught it neatly.
__label__twp Dólokhov jumped down.
__label__twp He smelt strongly of rum.
__label__twp “Well done!...
__label__twp Fine fellow!...
__label__twp There’s a bet for you!...
__label__twp Devil take you!” came from different sides.
__label__twp The Englishman took out his purse and began counting out the money.
__label__twp Dólokhov stood frowning and did not speak.
__label__twp Pierre jumped upon the window sill.
__label__twp “Gentlemen, who wishes to bet with me? I’ll do the same thing!” he suddenly cried.
__label__twp “Even without a bet, there! Tell them to bring me a bottle.
__label__twp I’ll do it....
__label__twp Bring a bottle!”  “Let him do it, let him do it,” said Dólokhov, smiling.
__label__twp “What next? Have you gone mad?...
__label__twp No one would let you!...
__label__twp Why, you go giddy even on a staircase,” exclaimed several voices.
__label__twp “I’ll drink it! Let’s have a bottle of rum!” shouted Pierre, banging the table with a determined and drunken gesture and preparing to climb out of the window.
__label__twp They seized him by his arms; but he was so strong that everyone who touched him was sent flying.
__label__twp “No, you’ll never manage him that way,” said Anatole.
__label__twp “Wait a bit and I’ll get round him....
__label__twp Listen! I’ll take your bet tomorrow, but now we are all going to ——’s.”  “Come on then,” cried Pierre.
__label__twp “Come on!...
__label__twp And we’ll take Bruin with us.”  And he caught the bear, took it in his arms, lifted it from the ground, and began dancing round the room with it.
__label__twp CHAPTER X  Prince Vasíli kept the promise he had given to Princess Drubetskáya who had spoken to him on behalf of her only son Borís on the evening of Anna Pávlovna’s soiree.
__label__twp The matter was mentioned to the Emperor, an exception made, and Borís transferred into the regiment of Semënov Guards with the rank of cornet.
__label__twp He received, however, no appointment to Kutúzov’s staff despite all Anna Mikháylovna’s endeavors and entreaties.
__label__twp Soon after Anna Pávlovna’s reception Anna Mikháylovna returned to Moscow and went straight to her rich relations, the Rostóvs, with whom she stayed when in the town and where her darling Bóry, who had only just entered a regiment of the line and was being at once transferred to the Guards as a cornet, had been educated from childhood and lived for years at a time.
__label__twp The Guards had already left Petersburg on the tenth of August, and her son, who had remained in Moscow for his equipment, was to join them on the march to Radzivílov.
__label__twp It was St.
__label__twp Natalia’s day and the name day of two of the Rostóvs—the mother and the youngest daughter—both named Nataly.
__label__twp Ever since the morning, carriages with six horses had been coming and going continually, bringing visitors to the Countess Rostóva’s big house on the Povarskáya, so well known to all Moscow.
__label__twp The countess herself and her handsome eldest daughter were in the drawing room with the visitors who came to congratulate, and who constantly succeeded one another in relays.
__label__twp The countess was a woman of about forty-five, with a thin Oriental type of face, evidently worn out with childbearing—she had had twelve.
__label__twp A languor of motion and speech, resulting from weakness, gave her a distinguished air which inspired respect.
__label__twp Princess Anna Mikháylovna Drubetskáya, who as a member of the household was also seated in the drawing room, helped to receive and entertain the visitors.
__label__twp The young people were in one of the inner rooms, not considering it necessary to take part in receiving the visitors.
__label__twp The count met the guests and saw them off, inviting them all to dinner.
__label__twp “I am very, very grateful to you, mon cher,” or “ma chère”—he called everyone without exception and without the slightest variation in his tone, “my dear,” whether they were above or below him in rank—“I thank you for myself and for our two dear ones whose name day we are keeping.
__label__twp But mind you come to dinner or I shall be offended, ma chère! On behalf of the whole family I beg you to come, mon cher!” These words he repeated to everyone without exception or variation, and with the same expression on his full, cheerful, clean-shaven face, the same firm pressure of the hand and the same quick, repeated bows.
__label__twp As soon as he had seen a visitor off he returned to one of those who were still in the drawing room, drew a chair toward him or her, and jauntily spreading out his legs and putting his hands on his knees with the air of a man who enjoys life and knows how to live, he swayed to and fro with dignity, offered surmises about the weather, or touched on questions of health, sometimes in Russian and sometimes in very bad but self-confident French; then again, like a man weary but unflinching in the fulfillment of duty, he rose to see some visitors off and, stroking his scanty gray hairs over his bald patch, also asked them to dinner.
__label__twp Sometimes on his way back from the anteroom he would pass through the conservatory and pantry into the large marble dining hall, where tables were being set out for eighty people; and looking at the footmen, who were bringing in silver and china, moving tables, and unfolding damask table linen, he would call Dmítri Vasílevich, a man of good family and the manager of all his affairs, and while looking with pleasure at the enormous table would say: “Well, Dmítri, you’ll see that things are all as they should be? That’s right! The great thing is the serving, that’s it.” And with a complacent sigh he would return to the drawing room.
__label__twp “Márya Lvóvna Karágina and her daughter!” announced the countess’ gigantic footman in his bass voice, entering the drawing room.
__label__twp The countess reflected a moment and took a pinch from a gold snuffbox with her husband’s portrait on it.
__label__twp “I’m quite worn out by these callers.
__label__twp However, I’ll see her and no more.
__label__twp She is so affected.
__label__twp Ask her in,” she said to the footman in a sad voice, as if saying: “Very well, finish me off.”  A tall, stout, and proud-looking woman, with a round-faced smiling daughter, entered the drawing room, their dresses rustling.
__label__twp “Dear Countess, what an age...
__label__twp She has been laid up, poor child ...
__label__twp at the Razumóvski’s ball ...
__label__twp and Countess Apráksina ...
__label__twp I was so delighted...” came the sounds of animated feminine voices, interrupting one another and mingling with the rustling of dresses and the scraping of chairs.
__label__twp Then one of those conversations began which last out until, at the first pause, the guests rise with a rustle of dresses and say, “I am so delighted...
__label__twp Mamma’s health...
__label__twp and Countess Apráksina...” and then, again rustling, pass into the anteroom, put on cloaks or mantles, and drive away.
__label__twp The conversation was on the chief topic of the day: the illness of the wealthy and celebrated beau of Catherine’s day, Count Bezúkhov, and about his illegitimate son Pierre, the one who had behaved so improperly at Anna Pávlovna’s reception.
__label__twp “I am so sorry for the poor count,” said the visitor.
__label__twp “He is in such bad health, and now this vexation about his son is enough to kill him!”  “What is that?” asked the countess as if she did not know what the visitor alluded to, though she had already heard about the cause of Count Bezúkhov’s distress some fifteen times.
__label__twp “That’s what comes of a modern education,” exclaimed the visitor.
__label__twp “It seems that while he was abroad this young man was allowed to do as he liked, now in Petersburg I hear he has been doing such terrible things that he has been expelled by the police.”  “You don’t say so!” replied the countess.
__label__twp “He chose his friends badly,” interposed Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp “Prince Vasíli’s son, he, and a certain Dólokhov have, it is said, been up to heaven only knows what! And they have had to suffer for it.
__label__twp Dólokhov has been degraded to the ranks and Bezúkhov’s son sent back to Moscow.
__label__twp Anatole Kurágin’s father managed somehow to get his son’s affair hushed up, but even he was ordered out of Petersburg.”  “But what have they been up to?” asked the countess.
__label__twp “They are regular brigands, especially Dólokhov,” replied the visitor.
__label__twp “He is a son of Márya Ivánovna Dólokhova, such a worthy woman, but there, just fancy! Those three got hold of a bear somewhere, put it in a carriage, and set off with it to visit some actresses! The police tried to interfere, and what did the young men do? They tied a policeman and the bear back to back and put the bear into the Moyka Canal.
__label__twp And there was the bear swimming about with the policeman on his back!”  “What a nice figure the policeman must have cut, my dear!” shouted the count, dying with laughter.
__label__twp “Oh, how dreadful! How can you laugh at it, Count?”  Yet the ladies themselves could not help laughing.
__label__twp “It was all they could do to rescue the poor man,” continued the visitor.
__label__twp “And to think it is Cyril Vladímirovich Bezúkhov’s son who amuses himself in this sensible manner! And he was said to be so well educated and clever.
__label__twp This is all that his foreign education has done for him! I hope that here in Moscow no one will receive him, in spite of his money.
__label__twp They wanted to introduce him to me, but I quite declined: I have my daughters to consider.”  “Why do you say this young man is so rich?” asked the countess, turning away from the girls, who at once assumed an air of inattention.
__label__twp “His children are all illegitimate.
__label__twp I think Pierre also is illegitimate.”  The visitor made a gesture with her hand.
__label__twp “I should think he has a score of them.”  Princess Anna Mikháylovna intervened in the conversation, evidently wishing to show her connections and knowledge of what went on in society.
__label__twp “The fact of the matter is,” said she significantly, and also in a half whisper, “everyone knows Count Cyril’s reputation....
__label__twp He has lost count of his children, but this Pierre was his favorite.”  “How handsome the old man still was only a year ago!” remarked the countess.
__label__twp “I have never seen a handsomer man.”  “He is very much altered now,” said Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp “Well, as I was saying, Prince Vasíli is the next heir through his wife, but the count is very fond of Pierre, looked after his education, and wrote to the Emperor about him; so that in the case of his death—and he is so ill that he may die at any moment, and Dr.
__label__twp Lorrain has come from Petersburg—no one knows who will inherit his immense fortune, Pierre or Prince Vasíli.
__label__twp Forty thousand serfs and millions of rubles! I know it all very well for Prince Vasíli told me himself.
__label__twp Besides, Cyril Vladímirovich is my mother’s second cousin.
__label__twp He’s also my Bóry’s godfather,” she added, as if she attached no importance at all to the fact.
__label__twp “Prince Vasíli arrived in Moscow yesterday.
__label__twp I hear he has come on some inspection business,” remarked the visitor.
__label__twp “Yes, but between ourselves,” said the princess, “that is a pretext.
__label__twp The fact is he has come to see Count Cyril Vladímirovich, hearing how ill he is.”  “But do you know, my dear, that was a capital joke,” said the count; and seeing that the elder visitor was not listening, he turned to the young ladies.
__label__twp “I can just imagine what a funny figure that policeman cut!”  And as he waved his arms to impersonate the policeman, his portly form again shook with a deep ringing laugh, the laugh of one who always eats well and, in particular, drinks well.
__label__twp “So do come and dine with us!” he said.
__label__twp CHAPTER XI  Silence ensued.
__label__twp The countess looked at her callers, smiling affably, but not concealing the fact that she would not be distressed if they now rose and took their leave.
__label__twp The visitor’s daughter was already smoothing down her dress with an inquiring look at her mother, when suddenly from the next room were heard the footsteps of boys and girls running to the door and the noise of a chair falling over, and a girl of thirteen, hiding something in the folds of her short muslin frock, darted in and stopped short in the middle of the room.
__label__twp It was evident that she had not intended her flight to bring her so far.
__label__twp Behind her in the doorway appeared a student with a crimson coat collar, an officer of the Guards, a girl of fifteen, and a plump rosy-faced boy in a short jacket.
__label__twp The count jumped up and, swaying from side to side, spread his arms wide and threw them round the little girl who had run in.
__label__twp “Ah, here she is!” he exclaimed laughing.
__label__twp “My pet, whose name day it is.
__label__twp My dear pet!”  “Ma chère, there is a time for everything,” said the countess with feigned severity.
__label__twp “You spoil her, Ilyá,” she added, turning to her husband.
__label__twp “How do you do, my dear? I wish you many happy returns of your name day,” said the visitor.
__label__twp “What a charming child,” she added, addressing the mother.
__label__twp This black-eyed, wide-mouthed girl, not pretty but full of life—with childish bare shoulders which after her run heaved and shook her bodice, with black curls tossed backward, thin bare arms, little legs in lace-frilled drawers, and feet in low slippers—was just at that charming age when a girl is no longer a child, though the child is not yet a young woman.
__label__twp Escaping from her father she ran to hide her flushed face in the lace of her mother’s mantilla—not paying the least attention to her severe remark—and began to laugh.
__label__twp She laughed, and in fragmentary sentences tried to explain about a doll which she produced from the folds of her frock.
__label__twp “Do you see?...
__label__twp My doll...
__label__twp Mimi...
__label__twp You see...” was all Natásha managed to utter (to her everything seemed funny).
__label__twp She leaned against her mother and burst into such a loud, ringing fit of laughter that even the prim visitor could not help joining in.
__label__twp “Now then, go away and take your monstrosity with you,” said the mother, pushing away her daughter with pretended sternness, and turning to the visitor she added: “She is my youngest girl.”  Natásha, raising her face for a moment from her mother’s mantilla, glanced up at her through tears of laughter, and again hid her face.
__label__twp The visitor, compelled to look on at this family scene, thought it necessary to take some part in it.
__label__twp “Tell me, my dear,” said she to Natásha, “is Mimi a relation of yours? A daughter, I suppose?”  Natásha did not like the visitor’s tone of condescension to childish things.
__label__twp She did not reply, but looked at her seriously.
__label__twp Meanwhile the younger generation: Borís, the officer, Anna Mikháylovna’s son; Nicholas, the undergraduate, the count’s eldest son; Sónya, the count’s fifteen-year-old niece, and little Pétya, his youngest boy, had all settled down in the drawing room and were obviously trying to restrain within the bounds of decorum the excitement and mirth that shone in all their faces.
__label__twp Evidently in the back rooms, from which they had dashed out so impetuously, the conversation had been more amusing than the drawing room talk of society scandals, the weather, and Countess Apráksina.
__label__twp Now and then they glanced at one another, hardly able to suppress their laughter.
__label__twp The two young men, the student and the officer, friends from childhood, were of the same age and both handsome fellows, though not alike.
__label__twp Borís was tall and fair, and his calm and handsome face had regular, delicate features.
__label__twp Nicholas was short with curly hair and an open expression.
__label__twp Dark hairs were already showing on his upper lip, and his whole face expressed impetuosity and enthusiasm.
__label__twp Nicholas blushed when he entered the drawing room.
__label__twp He evidently tried to find something to say, but failed.
__label__twp Borís on the contrary at once found his footing, and related quietly and humorously how he had known that doll Mimi when she was still quite a young lady, before her nose was broken; how she had aged during the five years he had known her, and how her head had cracked right across the skull.
__label__twp Having said this he glanced at Natásha.
__label__twp She turned away from him and glanced at her younger brother, who was screwing up his eyes and shaking with suppressed laughter, and unable to control herself any longer, she jumped up and rushed from the room as fast as her nimble little feet would carry her.
__label__twp Borís did not laugh.
__label__twp “You were meaning to go out, weren’t you, Mamma? Do you want the carriage?” he asked his mother with a smile.
__label__twp “Yes, yes, go and tell them to get it ready,” she answered, returning his smile.
__label__twp Borís quietly left the room and went in search of Natásha.
__label__twp The plump boy ran after them angrily, as if vexed that their program had been disturbed.
__label__twp CHAPTER XII  The only young people remaining in the drawing room, not counting the young lady visitor and the countess’ eldest daughter (who was four years older than her sister and behaved already like a grown-up person), were Nicholas and Sónya, the niece.
__label__twp Sónya was a slender little brunette with a tender look in her eyes which were veiled by long lashes, thick black plaits coiling twice round her head, and a tawny tint in her complexion and especially in the color of her slender but graceful and muscular arms and neck.
__label__twp By the grace of her movements, by the softness and flexibility of her small limbs, and by a certain coyness and reserve of manner, she reminded one of a pretty, half-grown kitten which promises to become a beautiful little cat.
__label__twp She evidently considered it proper to show an interest in the general conversation by smiling, but in spite of herself her eyes under their thick long lashes watched her cousin who was going to join the army, with such passionate girlish adoration that her smile could not for a single instant impose upon anyone, and it was clear that the kitten had settled down only to spring up with more energy and again play with her cousin as soon as they too could, like Natásha and Borís, escape from the drawing room.
__label__twp “Ah yes, my dear,” said the count, addressing the visitor and pointing to Nicholas, “his friend Borís has become an officer, and so for friendship’s sake he is leaving the university and me, his old father, and entering the military service, my dear.
__label__twp And there was a place and everything waiting for him in the Archives Department! Isn’t that friendship?” remarked the count in an inquiring tone.
__label__twp “But they say that war has been declared,” replied the visitor.
__label__twp “They’ve been saying so a long while,” said the count, “and they’ll say so again and again, and that will be the end of it.
__label__twp My dear, there’s friendship for you,” he repeated.
__label__twp “He’s joining the hussars.”  The visitor, not knowing what to say, shook her head.
__label__twp “It’s not at all from friendship,” declared Nicholas, flaring up and turning away as if from a shameful aspersion.
__label__twp “It is not from friendship at all; I simply feel that the army is my vocation.”  He glanced at his cousin and the young lady visitor; and they were both regarding him with a smile of approbation.
__label__twp “Schubert, the colonel of the Pávlograd Hussars, is dining with us today.
__label__twp He has been here on leave and is taking Nicholas back with him.
__label__twp It can’t be helped!” said the count, shrugging his shoulders and speaking playfully of a matter that evidently distressed him.
__label__twp “I have already told you, Papa,” said his son, “that if you don’t wish to let me go, I’ll stay.
__label__twp But I know I am no use anywhere except in the army; I am not a diplomat or a government clerk.—I don’t know how to hide what I feel.” As he spoke he kept glancing with the flirtatiousness of a handsome youth at Sónya and the young lady visitor.
__label__twp The little kitten, feasting her eyes on him, seemed ready at any moment to start her gambols again and display her kittenish nature.
__label__twp “All right, all right!” said the old count.
__label__twp “He always flares up! This Buonaparte has turned all their heads; they all think of how he rose from an ensign and became Emperor.
__label__twp Well, well, God grant it,” he added, not noticing his visitor’s sarcastic smile.
__label__twp The elders began talking about Bonaparte.
__label__twp Julie Karágina turned to young Rostóv.
__label__twp “What a pity you weren’t at the Arkhárovs’ on Thursday.
__label__twp It was so dull without you,” said she, giving him a tender smile.
__label__twp The young man, flattered, sat down nearer to her with a coquettish smile, and engaged the smiling Julie in a confidential conversation without at all noticing that his involuntary smile had stabbed the heart of Sónya, who blushed and smiled unnaturally.
__label__twp In the midst of his talk he glanced round at her.
__label__twp She gave him a passionately angry glance, and hardly able to restrain her tears and maintain the artificial smile on her lips, she got up and left the room.
__label__twp All Nicholas’ animation vanished.
__label__twp He waited for the first pause in the conversation, and then with a distressed face left the room to find Sónya.
__label__twp “How plainly all these young people wear their hearts on their sleeves!” said Anna Mikháylovna, pointing to Nicholas as he went out.
__label__twp “Cousinage—dangereux voisinage,” * she added.
__label__twp * Cousinhood is a dangerous neighborhood.
__label__twp “Yes,” said the countess when the brightness these young people had brought into the room had vanished; and as if answering a question no one had put but which was always in her mind, “and how much suffering, how much anxiety one has had to go through that we might rejoice in them now! And yet really the anxiety is greater now than the joy.
__label__twp One is always, always anxious! Especially just at this age, so dangerous both for girls and boys.”  “It all depends on the bringing up,” remarked the visitor.
__label__twp “Yes, you’re quite right,” continued the countess.
__label__twp “Till now I have always, thank God, been my children’s friend and had their full confidence,” said she, repeating the mistake of so many parents who imagine that their children have no secrets from them.
__label__twp “I know I shall always be my daughters’ first confidante, and that if Nicholas, with his impulsive nature, does get into mischief (a boy can’t help it), he will all the same never be like those Petersburg young men.”  “Yes, they are splendid, splendid youngsters,” chimed in the count, who always solved questions that seemed to him perplexing by deciding that everything was splendid.
__label__twp “Just fancy: wants to be an hussar.
__label__twp What’s one to do, my dear?”  “What a charming creature your younger girl is,” said the visitor; “a little volcano!”  “Yes, a regular volcano,” said the count.
__label__twp “Takes after me! And what a voice she has; though she’s my daughter, I tell the truth when I say she’ll be a singer, a second Salomoni! We have engaged an Italian to give her lessons.”  “Isn’t she too young? I have heard that it harms the voice to train it at that age.”  “Oh no, not at all too young!” replied the count.
__label__twp “Why, our mothers used to be married at twelve or thirteen.”  “And she’s in love with Borís already.
__label__twp Just fancy!” said the countess with a gentle smile, looking at Borís and went on, evidently concerned with a thought that always occupied her: “Now you see if I were to be severe with her and to forbid it ...
__label__twp goodness knows what they might be up to on the sly” (she meant that they would be kissing), “but as it is, I know every word she utters.
__label__twp She will come running to me of her own accord in the evening and tell me everything.
__label__twp Perhaps I spoil her, but really that seems the best plan.
__label__twp With her elder sister I was stricter.”  “Yes, I was brought up quite differently,” remarked the handsome elder daughter, Countess Véra, with a smile.
__label__twp But the smile did not enhance Véra’s beauty as smiles generally do; on the contrary it gave her an unnatural, and therefore unpleasant, expression.
__label__twp Véra was good-looking, not at all stupid, quick at learning, was well brought up, and had a pleasant voice; what she said was true and appropriate, yet, strange to say, everyone—the visitors and countess alike—turned to look at her as if wondering why she had said it, and they all felt awkward.
__label__twp “People are always too clever with their eldest children and try to make something exceptional of them,” said the visitor.
__label__twp “What’s the good of denying it, my dear? Our dear countess was too clever with Véra,” said the count.
__label__twp “Well, what of that? She’s turned out splendidly all the same,” he added, winking at Véra.
__label__twp The guests got up and took their leave, promising to return to dinner.
__label__twp “What manners! I thought they would never go,” said the countess, when she had seen her guests out.
__label__twp CHAPTER XIII  When Natásha ran out of the drawing room she only went as far as the conservatory.
__label__twp There she paused and stood listening to the conversation in the drawing room, waiting for Borís to come out.
__label__twp She was already growing impatient, and stamped her foot, ready to cry at his not coming at once, when she heard the young man’s discreet steps approaching neither quickly nor slowly.
__label__twp At this Natásha dashed swiftly among the flower tubs and hid there.
__label__twp Borís paused in the middle of the room, looked round, brushed a little dust from the sleeve of his uniform, and going up to a mirror examined his handsome face.
__label__twp Natásha, very still, peered out from her ambush, waiting to see what he would do.
__label__twp He stood a little while before the glass, smiled, and walked toward the other door.
__label__twp Natásha was about to call him but changed her mind.
__label__twp “Let him look for me,” thought she.
__label__twp Hardly had Borís gone than Sónya, flushed, in tears, and muttering angrily, came in at the other door.
__label__twp Natásha checked her first impulse to run out to her, and remained in her hiding place, watching—as under an invisible cap—to see what went on in the world.
__label__twp She was experiencing a new and peculiar pleasure.
__label__twp Sónya, muttering to herself, kept looking round toward the drawing room door.
__label__twp It opened and Nicholas came in.
__label__twp “Sónya, what is the matter with you? How can you?” said he, running up to her.
__label__twp “It’s nothing, nothing; leave me alone!” sobbed Sónya.
__label__twp “Ah, I know what it is.”  “Well, if you do, so much the better, and you can go back to her!”  “Só-o-onya! Look here! How can you torture me and yourself like that, for a mere fancy?” said Nicholas taking her hand.
__label__twp Sónya did not pull it away, and left off crying.
__label__twp Natásha, not stirring and scarcely breathing, watched from her ambush with sparkling eyes.
__label__twp “What will happen now?” thought she.
__label__twp “Sónya! What is anyone in the world to me? You alone are everything!” said Nicholas.
__label__twp “And I will prove it to you.”  “I don’t like you to talk like that.”  “Well, then, I won’t; only forgive me, Sónya!” He drew her to him and kissed her.
__label__twp “Oh, how nice,” thought Natásha; and when Sónya and Nicholas had gone out of the conservatory she followed and called Borís to her.
__label__twp “Borís, come here,” said she with a sly and significant look.
__label__twp “I have something to tell you.
__label__twp Here, here!” and she led him into the conservatory to the place among the tubs where she had been hiding.
__label__twp Borís followed her, smiling.
__label__twp “What is the something?” asked he.
__label__twp She grew confused, glanced round, and, seeing the doll she had thrown down on one of the tubs, picked it up.
__label__twp “Kiss the doll,” said she.
__label__twp Borís looked attentively and kindly at her eager face, but did not reply.
__label__twp “Don’t you want to? Well, then, come here,” said she, and went further in among the plants and threw down the doll.
__label__twp “Closer, closer!” she whispered.
__label__twp She caught the young officer by his cuffs, and a look of solemnity and fear appeared on her flushed face.
__label__twp “And me? Would you like to kiss me?” she whispered almost inaudibly, glancing up at him from under her brows, smiling, and almost crying from excitement.
__label__twp Borís blushed.
__label__twp “How funny you are!” he said, bending down to her and blushing still more, but he waited and did nothing.
__label__twp Suddenly she jumped up onto a tub to be higher than he, embraced him so that both her slender bare arms clasped him above his neck, and, tossing back her hair, kissed him full on the lips.
__label__twp Then she slipped down among the flowerpots on the other side of the tubs and stood, hanging her head.
__label__twp “Natásha,” he said, “you know that I love you, but....”  “You are in love with me?” Natásha broke in.
__label__twp “Yes, I am, but please don’t let us do like that....
__label__twp In another four years ...
__label__twp then I will ask for your hand.”  Natásha considered.
__label__twp “Thirteen, fourteen, fifteen, sixteen,” she counted on her slender little fingers.
__label__twp “All right! Then it’s settled?”  A smile of joy and satisfaction lit up her eager face.
__label__twp “Settled!” replied Borís.
__label__twp “Forever?” said the little girl.
__label__twp “Till death itself?”  She took his arm and with a happy face went with him into the adjoining sitting room.
__label__twp CHAPTER XIV  After receiving her visitors, the countess was so tired that she gave orders to admit no more, but the porter was told to be sure to invite to dinner all who came “to congratulate.” The countess wished to have a tête-à-tête talk with the friend of her childhood, Princess Anna Mikháylovna, whom she had not seen properly since she returned from Petersburg.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna, with her tear-worn but pleasant face, drew her chair nearer to that of the countess.
__label__twp “With you I will be quite frank,” said Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp “There are not many left of us old friends! That’s why I so value your friendship.”  Anna Mikháylovna looked at Véra and paused.
__label__twp The countess pressed her friend’s hand.
__label__twp “Véra,” she said to her eldest daughter who was evidently not a favorite, “how is it you have so little tact? Don’t you see you are not wanted here? Go to the other girls, or...”  The handsome Véra smiled contemptuously but did not seem at all hurt.
__label__twp “If you had told me sooner, Mamma, I would have gone,” she replied as she rose to go to her own room.
__label__twp But as she passed the sitting room she noticed two couples sitting, one pair at each window.
__label__twp She stopped and smiled scornfully.
__label__twp Sónya was sitting close to Nicholas who was copying out some verses for her, the first he had ever written.
__label__twp Borís and Natásha were at the other window and ceased talking when Véra entered.
__label__twp Sónya and Natásha looked at Véra with guilty, happy faces.
__label__twp It was pleasant and touching to see these little girls in love; but apparently the sight of them roused no pleasant feeling in Véra.
__label__twp “How often have I asked you not to take my things?” she said.
__label__twp “You have a room of your own,” and she took the inkstand from Nicholas.
__label__twp “In a minute, in a minute,” he said, dipping his pen.
__label__twp “You always manage to do things at the wrong time,” continued Véra.
__label__twp “You came rushing into the drawing room so that everyone felt ashamed of you.”  Though what she said was quite just, perhaps for that very reason no one replied, and the four simply looked at one another.
__label__twp She lingered in the room with the inkstand in her hand.
__label__twp “And at your age what secrets can there be between Natásha and Borís, or between you two? It’s all nonsense!”  “Now, Véra, what does it matter to you?” said Natásha in defense, speaking very gently.
__label__twp She seemed that day to be more than ever kind and affectionate to everyone.
__label__twp “Very silly,” said Véra.
__label__twp “I am ashamed of you.
__label__twp Secrets indeed!”  “All have secrets of their own,” answered Natásha, getting warmer.
__label__twp “We don’t interfere with you and Berg.”  “I should think not,” said Véra, “because there can never be anything wrong in my behavior.
__label__twp But I’ll just tell Mamma how you are behaving with Borís.”  “Natálya Ilyníchna behaves very well to me,” remarked Borís.
__label__twp “I have nothing to complain of.”  “Don’t, Borís! You are such a diplomat that it is really tiresome,” said Natásha in a mortified voice that trembled slightly.
__label__twp (She used the word “diplomat,” which was just then much in vogue among the children, in the special sense they attached to it.) “Why does she bother me?” And she added, turning to Véra, “You’ll never understand it, because you’ve never loved anyone.
__label__twp You have no heart! You are a Madame de Genlis and nothing more” (this nickname, bestowed on Véra by Nicholas, was considered very stinging), “and your greatest pleasure is to be unpleasant to people! Go and flirt with Berg as much as you please,” she finished quickly.
__label__twp “I shall at any rate not run after a young man before visitors...”  “Well, now you’ve done what you wanted,” put in Nicholas—“said unpleasant things to everyone and upset them.
__label__twp Let’s go to the nursery.”  All four, like a flock of scared birds, got up and left the room.
__label__twp “The unpleasant things were said to me,” remarked Véra, “I said none to anyone.”  “Madame de Genlis! Madame de Genlis!” shouted laughing voices through the door.
__label__twp The handsome Véra, who produced such an irritating and unpleasant effect on everyone, smiled and, evidently unmoved by what had been said to her, went to the looking glass and arranged her hair and scarf.
__label__twp Looking at her own handsome face she seemed to become still colder and calmer.
__label__twp In the drawing room the conversation was still going on.
__label__twp “Ah, my dear,” said the countess, “my life is not all roses either.
__label__twp Don’t I know that at the rate we are living our means won’t last long? It’s all the Club and his easygoing nature.
__label__twp Even in the country do we get any rest? Theatricals, hunting, and heaven knows what besides! But don’t let’s talk about me; tell me how you managed everything.
__label__twp I often wonder at you, Annette—how at your age you can rush off alone in a carriage to Moscow, to Petersburg, to those ministers and great people, and know how to deal with them all! It’s quite astonishing.
__label__twp How did you get things settled? I couldn’t possibly do it.”  “Ah, my love,” answered Anna Mikháylovna, “God grant you never know what it is to be left a widow without means and with a son you love to distraction! One learns many things then,” she added with a certain pride.
__label__twp “That lawsuit taught me much.
__label__twp When I want to see one of those big people I write a note: ‘Princess So-and-So desires an interview with So and-So,’ and then I take a cab and go myself two, three, or four times—till I get what I want.
__label__twp I don’t mind what they think of me.”  “Well, and to whom did you apply about Bóry?” asked the countess.
__label__twp “You see yours is already an officer in the Guards, while my Nicholas is going as a cadet.
__label__twp There’s no one to interest himself for him.
__label__twp To whom did you apply?”  “To Prince Vasíli.
__label__twp He was so kind.
__label__twp He at once agreed to everything, and put the matter before the Emperor,” said Princess Anna Mikháylovna enthusiastically, quite forgetting all the humiliation she had endured to gain her end.
__label__twp “Has Prince Vasíli aged much?” asked the countess.
__label__twp “I have not seen him since we acted together at the Rumyántsovs’ theatricals.
__label__twp I expect he has forgotten me.
__label__twp He paid me attentions in those days,” said the countess, with a smile.
__label__twp “He is just the same as ever,” replied Anna Mikháylovna, “overflowing with amiability.
__label__twp His position has not turned his head at all.
__label__twp He said to me, ‘I am sorry I can do so little for you, dear Princess.
__label__twp I am at your command.’ Yes, he is a fine fellow and a very kind relation.
__label__twp But, Nataly, you know my love for my son: I would do anything for his happiness! And my affairs are in such a bad way that my position is now a terrible one,” continued Anna Mikháylovna, sadly, dropping her voice.
__label__twp “My wretched lawsuit takes all I have and makes no progress.
__label__twp Would you believe it, I have literally not a penny and don’t know how to equip Borís.” She took out her handkerchief and began to cry.
__label__twp “I need five hundred rubles, and have only one twenty-five-ruble note.
__label__twp I am in such a state....
__label__twp My only hope now is in Count Cyril Vladímirovich Bezúkhov.
__label__twp If he will not assist his godson—you know he is Bóry’s godfather—and allow him something for his maintenance, all my trouble will have been thrown away....
__label__twp I shall not be able to equip him.”  The countess’ eyes filled with tears and she pondered in silence.
__label__twp “I often think, though, perhaps it’s a sin,” said the princess, “that here lives Count Cyril Vladímirovich Bezúkhov so rich, all alone...
__label__twp that tremendous fortune...
__label__twp and what is his life worth? It’s a burden to him, and Bóry’s life is only just beginning....”  “Surely he will leave something to Borís,” said the countess.
__label__twp “Heaven only knows, my dear! These rich grandees are so selfish.
__label__twp Still, I will take Borís and go to see him at once, and I shall speak to him straight out.
__label__twp Let people think what they will of me, it’s really all the same to me when my son’s fate is at stake.” The princess rose.
__label__twp “It’s now two o’clock and you dine at four.
__label__twp There will just be time.”  And like a practical Petersburg lady who knows how to make the most of time, Anna Mikháylovna sent someone to call her son, and went into the anteroom with him.
__label__twp “Good-by, my dear,” said she to the countess who saw her to the door, and added in a whisper so that her son should not hear, “Wish me good luck.”  “Are you going to Count Cyril Vladímirovich, my dear?” said the count coming out from the dining hall into the anteroom, and he added: “If he is better, ask Pierre to dine with us.
__label__twp He has been to the house, you know, and danced with the children.
__label__twp Be sure to invite him, my dear.
__label__twp We will see how Tarás distinguishes himself today.
__label__twp He says Count Orlóv never gave such a dinner as ours will be!”      CHAPTER XV  “My dear Borís,” said Princess Anna Mikháylovna to her son as Countess Rostóva’s carriage in which they were seated drove over the straw covered street and turned into the wide courtyard of Count Cyril Vladímirovich Bezúkhov’s house.
__label__twp “My dear Borís,” said the mother, drawing her hand from beneath her old mantle and laying it timidly and tenderly on her son’s arm, “be affectionate and attentive to him.
__label__twp Count Cyril Vladímirovich is your godfather after all, and your future depends on him.
__label__twp Remember that, my dear, and be nice to him, as you so well know how to be.”  “If only I knew that anything besides humiliation would come of it...” answered her son coldly.
__label__twp “But I have promised and will do it for your sake.”  Although the hall porter saw someone’s carriage standing at the entrance, after scrutinizing the mother and son (who without asking to be announced had passed straight through the glass porch between the rows of statues in niches) and looking significantly at the lady’s old cloak, he asked whether they wanted the count or the princesses, and, hearing that they wished to see the count, said his excellency was worse today, and that his excellency was not receiving anyone.
__label__twp “We may as well go back,” said the son in French.
__label__twp “My dear!” exclaimed his mother imploringly, again laying her hand on his arm as if that touch might soothe or rouse him.
__label__twp Borís said no more, but looked inquiringly at his mother without taking off his cloak.
__label__twp “My friend,” said Anna Mikháylovna in gentle tones, addressing the hall porter, “I know Count Cyril Vladímirovich is very ill...
__label__twp that’s why I have come...
__label__twp I am a relation.
__label__twp I shall not disturb him, my friend...
__label__twp I only need see Prince Vasíli Sergéevich: he is staying here, is he not? Please announce me.”  The hall porter sullenly pulled a bell that rang upstairs, and turned away.
__label__twp “Princess Drubetskáya to see Prince Vasíli Sergéevich,” he called to a footman dressed in knee breeches, shoes, and a swallow-tail coat, who ran downstairs and looked over from the halfway landing.
__label__twp The mother smoothed the folds of her dyed silk dress before a large Venetian mirror in the wall, and in her trodden-down shoes briskly ascended the carpeted stairs.
__label__twp “My dear,” she said to her son, once more stimulating him by a touch, “you promised me!”  The son, lowering his eyes, followed her quietly.
__label__twp They entered the large hall, from which one of the doors led to the apartments assigned to Prince Vasíli.
__label__twp Just as the mother and son, having reached the middle of the hall, were about to ask their way of an elderly footman who had sprung up as they entered, the bronze handle of one of the doors turned and Prince Vasíli came out—wearing a velvet coat with a single star on his breast, as was his custom when at home—taking leave of a good-looking, dark-haired man.
__label__twp This was the celebrated Petersburg doctor, Lorrain.
__label__twp “Then it is certain?” said the prince.
__label__twp “Prince, humanum est errare, * but...” replied the doctor, swallowing his r’s, and pronouncing the Latin words with a French accent.
__label__twp * To err is human.
__label__twp “Very well, very well...”  Seeing Anna Mikháylovna and her son, Prince Vasíli dismissed the doctor with a bow and approached them silently and with a look of inquiry.
__label__twp The son noticed that an expression of profound sorrow suddenly clouded his mother’s face, and he smiled slightly.
__label__twp “Ah, Prince! In what sad circumstances we meet again! And how is our dear invalid?” said she, as though unaware of the cold offensive look fixed on her.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli stared at her and at Borís questioningly and perplexed.
__label__twp Borís bowed politely.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli without acknowledging the bow turned to Anna Mikháylovna, answering her query by a movement of the head and lips indicating very little hope for the patient.
__label__twp “Is it possible?” exclaimed Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp “Oh, how awful! It is terrible to think....
__label__twp This is my son,” she added, indicating Borís.
__label__twp “He wanted to thank you himself.”  Borís bowed again politely.
__label__twp “Believe me, Prince, a mother’s heart will never forget what you have done for us.”  “I am glad I was able to do you a service, my dear Anna Mikháylovna,” said Prince Vasíli, arranging his lace frill, and in tone and manner, here in Moscow to Anna Mikháylovna whom he had placed under an obligation, assuming an air of much greater importance than he had done in Petersburg at Anna Schérer’s reception.
__label__twp “Try to serve well and show yourself worthy,” added he, addressing Borís with severity.
__label__twp “I am glad....
__label__twp Are you here on leave?” he went on in his usual tone of indifference.
__label__twp “I am awaiting orders to join my new regiment, your excellency,” replied Borís, betraying neither annoyance at the prince’s brusque manner nor a desire to enter into conversation, but speaking so quietly and respectfully that the prince gave him a searching glance.
__label__twp “Are you living with your mother?”  “I am living at Countess Rostóva’s,” replied Borís, again adding, “your excellency.”  “That is, with Ilyá Rostóv who married Nataly Shinshiná,” said Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp “I know, I know,” answered Prince Vasíli in his monotonous voice.
__label__twp “I never could understand how Nataly made up her mind to marry that unlicked bear! A perfectly absurd and stupid fellow, and a gambler too, I am told.”  “But a very kind man, Prince,” said Anna Mikháylovna with a pathetic smile, as though she too knew that Count Rostóv deserved this censure, but asked him not to be too hard on the poor old man.
__label__twp “What do the doctors say?” asked the princess after a pause, her worn face again expressing deep sorrow.
__label__twp “They give little hope,” replied the prince.
__label__twp “And I should so like to thank Uncle once for all his kindness to me and Borís.
__label__twp He is his godson,” she added, her tone suggesting that this fact ought to give Prince Vasíli much satisfaction.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli became thoughtful and frowned.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna saw that he was afraid of finding in her a rival for Count Bezúkhov’s fortune, and hastened to reassure him.
__label__twp “If it were not for my sincere affection and devotion to Uncle,” said she, uttering the word with peculiar assurance and unconcern, “I know his character: noble, upright ...
__label__twp but you see he has no one with him except the young princesses....
__label__twp They are still young....” She bent her head and continued in a whisper: “Has he performed his final duty, Prince? How priceless are those last moments! It can make things no worse, and it is absolutely necessary to prepare him if he is so ill.
__label__twp We women, Prince,” and she smiled tenderly, “always know how to say these things.
__label__twp I absolutely must see him, however painful it may be for me.
__label__twp I am used to suffering.”  Evidently the prince understood her, and also understood, as he had done at Anna Pávlovna’s, that it would be difficult to get rid of Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp “Would not such a meeting be too trying for him, dear Anna Mikháylovna?” said he.
__label__twp “Let us wait until evening.
__label__twp The doctors are expecting a crisis.”  “But one cannot delay, Prince, at such a moment! Consider that the welfare of his soul is at stake.
__label__twp Ah, it is awful: the duties of a Christian...”  A door of one of the inner rooms opened and one of the princesses, the count’s niece, entered with a cold, stern face.
__label__twp The length of her body was strikingly out of proportion to her short legs.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli turned to her.
__label__twp “Well, how is he?”  “Still the same; but what can you expect, this noise...” said the princess, looking at Anna Mikháylovna as at a stranger.
__label__twp “Ah, my dear, I hardly knew you,” said Anna Mikháylovna with a happy smile, ambling lightly up to the count’s niece.
__label__twp “I have come, and am at your service to help you nurse my uncle.
__label__twp I imagine what you have gone through,” and she sympathetically turned up her eyes.
__label__twp The princess gave no reply and did not even smile, but left the room as Anna Mikháylovna took off her gloves and, occupying the position she had conquered, settled down in an armchair, inviting Prince Vasíli to take a seat beside her.
__label__twp “Borís,” she said to her son with a smile, “I shall go in to see the count, my uncle; but you, my dear, had better go to Pierre meanwhile and don’t forget to give him the Rostóvs’ invitation.
__label__twp They ask him to dinner.
__label__twp I suppose he won’t go?” she continued, turning to the prince.
__label__twp “On the contrary,” replied the prince, who had plainly become depressed, “I shall be only too glad if you relieve me of that young man....
__label__twp Here he is, and the count has not once asked for him.”  He shrugged his shoulders.
__label__twp A footman conducted Borís down one flight of stairs and up another, to Pierre’s rooms.
__label__twp CHAPTER XVI  Pierre, after all, had not managed to choose a career for himself in Petersburg, and had been expelled from there for riotous conduct and sent to Moscow.
__label__twp The story told about him at Count Rostóv’s was true.
__label__twp Pierre had taken part in tying a policeman to a bear.
__label__twp He had now been for some days in Moscow and was staying as usual at his father’s house.
__label__twp Though he expected that the story of his escapade would be already known in Moscow and that the ladies about his father—who were never favorably disposed toward him—would have used it to turn the count against him, he nevertheless on the day of his arrival went to his father’s part of the house.
__label__twp Entering the drawing room, where the princesses spent most of their time, he greeted the ladies, two of whom were sitting at embroidery frames while a third read aloud.
__label__twp It was the eldest who was reading—the one who had met Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp The two younger ones were embroidering: both were rosy and pretty and they differed only in that one had a little mole on her lip which made her much prettier.
__label__twp Pierre was received as if he were a corpse or a leper.
__label__twp The eldest princess paused in her reading and silently stared at him with frightened eyes; the second assumed precisely the same expression; while the youngest, the one with the mole, who was of a cheerful and lively disposition, bent over her frame to hide a smile probably evoked by the amusing scene she foresaw.
__label__twp She drew her wool down through the canvas and, scarcely able to refrain from laughing, stooped as if trying to make out the pattern.
__label__twp “How do you do, cousin?” said Pierre.
__label__twp “You don’t recognize me?”  “I recognize you only too well, too well.”  “How is the count? Can I see him?” asked Pierre, awkwardly as usual, but unabashed.
__label__twp “The count is suffering physically and mentally, and apparently you have done your best to increase his mental sufferings.”  “Can I see the count?” Pierre again asked.
__label__twp “Hm....
__label__twp If you wish to kill him, to kill him outright, you can see him...
__label__twp Olga, go and see whether Uncle’s beef tea is ready—it is almost time,” she added, giving Pierre to understand that they were busy, and busy making his father comfortable, while evidently he, Pierre, was only busy causing him annoyance.
__label__twp Olga went out.
__label__twp Pierre stood looking at the sisters; then he bowed and said: “Then I will go to my rooms.
__label__twp You will let me know when I can see him.”  And he left the room, followed by the low but ringing laughter of the sister with the mole.
__label__twp Next day Prince Vasíli had arrived and settled in the count’s house.
__label__twp He sent for Pierre and said to him: “My dear fellow, if you are going to behave here as you did in Petersburg, you will end very badly; that is all I have to say to you.
__label__twp The count is very, very ill, and you must not see him at all.”  Since then Pierre had not been disturbed and had spent the whole time in his rooms upstairs.
__label__twp When Borís appeared at his door Pierre was pacing up and down his room, stopping occasionally at a corner to make menacing gestures at the wall, as if running a sword through an invisible foe, and glaring savagely over his spectacles, and then again resuming his walk, muttering indistinct words, shrugging his shoulders and gesticulating.
__label__twp “England is done for,” said he, scowling and pointing his finger at someone unseen.
__label__twp “Mr.
__label__twp Pitt, as a traitor to the nation and to the rights of man, is sentenced to...” But before Pierre—who at that moment imagined himself to be Napoleon in person and to have just effected the dangerous crossing of the Straits of Dover and captured London—could pronounce Pitt’s sentence, he saw a well-built and handsome young officer entering his room.
__label__twp Pierre paused.
__label__twp He had left Moscow when Borís was a boy of fourteen, and had quite forgotten him, but in his usual impulsive and hearty way he took Borís by the hand with a friendly smile.
__label__twp “Do you remember me?” asked Borís quietly with a pleasant smile.
__label__twp “I have come with my mother to see the count, but it seems he is not well.”  “Yes, it seems he is ill.
__label__twp People are always disturbing him,” answered Pierre, trying to remember who this young man was.
__label__twp Borís felt that Pierre did not recognize him but did not consider it necessary to introduce himself, and without experiencing the least embarrassment looked Pierre straight in the face.
__label__twp “Count Rostóv asks you to come to dinner today,” said he, after a considerable pause which made Pierre feel uncomfortable.
__label__twp “Ah, Count Rostóv!” exclaimed Pierre joyfully.
__label__twp “Then you are his son, Ilyá? Only fancy, I didn’t know you at first.
__label__twp Do you remember how we went to the Sparrow Hills with Madame Jacquot?...
__label__twp It’s such an age...”  “You are mistaken,” said Borís deliberately, with a bold and slightly sarcastic smile.
__label__twp “I am Borís, son of Princess Anna Mikháylovna Drubetskáya.
__label__twp Rostóv, the father, is Ilyá, and his son is Nicholas.
__label__twp I never knew any Madame Jacquot.”  Pierre shook his head and arms as if attacked by mosquitoes or bees.
__label__twp “Oh dear, what am I thinking about? I’ve mixed everything up.
__label__twp One has so many relatives in Moscow! So you are Borís? Of course.
__label__twp Well, now we know where we are.
__label__twp And what do you think of the Boulogne expedition? The English will come off badly, you know, if Napoleon gets across the Channel.
__label__twp I think the expedition is quite feasible.
__label__twp If only Villeneuve doesn’t make a mess of things!”  Borís knew nothing about the Boulogne expedition; he did not read the papers and it was the first time he had heard Villeneuve’s name.
__label__twp “We here in Moscow are more occupied with dinner parties and scandal than with politics,” said he in his quiet ironical tone.
__label__twp “I know nothing about it and have not thought about it.
__label__twp Moscow is chiefly busy with gossip,” he continued.
__label__twp “Just now they are talking about you and your father.”  Pierre smiled in his good-natured way as if afraid for his companion’s sake that the latter might say something he would afterwards regret.
__label__twp But Borís spoke distinctly, clearly, and dryly, looking straight into Pierre’s eyes.
__label__twp “Moscow has nothing else to do but gossip,” Borís went on.
__label__twp “Everybody is wondering to whom the count will leave his fortune, though he may perhaps outlive us all, as I sincerely hope he will...”  “Yes, it is all very horrid,” interrupted Pierre, “very horrid.”  Pierre was still afraid that this officer might inadvertently say something disconcerting to himself.
__label__twp “And it must seem to you,” said Borís flushing slightly, but not changing his tone or attitude, “it must seem to you that everyone is trying to get something out of the rich man?”  “So it does,” thought Pierre.
__label__twp “But I just wish to say, to avoid misunderstandings, that you are quite mistaken if you reckon me or my mother among such people.
__label__twp We are very poor, but for my own part at any rate, for the very reason that your father is rich, I don’t regard myself as a relation of his, and neither I nor my mother would ever ask or take anything from him.”  For a long time Pierre could not understand, but when he did, he jumped up from the sofa, seized Borís under the elbow in his quick, clumsy way, and, blushing far more than Borís, began to speak with a feeling of mingled shame and vexation.
__label__twp “Well, this is strange! Do you suppose I...
__label__twp who could think?...
__label__twp I know very well...”  But Borís again interrupted him.
__label__twp “I am glad I have spoken out fully.
__label__twp Perhaps you did not like it? You must excuse me,” said he, putting Pierre at ease instead of being put at ease by him, “but I hope I have not offended you.
__label__twp I always make it a rule to speak out...
__label__twp Well, what answer am I to take? Will you come to dinner at the Rostóvs’?”  And Borís, having apparently relieved himself of an onerous duty and extricated himself from an awkward situation and placed another in it, became quite pleasant again.
__label__twp “No, but I say,” said Pierre, calming down, “you are a wonderful fellow! What you have just said is good, very good.
__label__twp Of course you don’t know me.
__label__twp We have not met for such a long time...
__label__twp not since we were children.
__label__twp You might think that I...
__label__twp I understand, quite understand.
__label__twp I could not have done it myself, I should not have had the courage, but it’s splendid.
__label__twp I am very glad to have made your acquaintance.
__label__twp It’s queer,” he added after a pause, “that you should have suspected me!” He began to laugh.
__label__twp “Well, what of it! I hope we’ll get better acquainted,” and he pressed Borís’ hand.
__label__twp “Do you know, I have not once been in to see the count.
__label__twp He has not sent for me....
__label__twp I am sorry for him as a man, but what can one do?”  “And so you think Napoleon will manage to get an army across?” asked Borís with a smile.
__label__twp Pierre saw that Borís wished to change the subject, and being of the same mind he began explaining the advantages and disadvantages of the Boulogne expedition.
__label__twp A footman came in to summon Borís—the princess was going.
__label__twp Pierre, in order to make Borís’ better acquaintance, promised to come to dinner, and warmly pressing his hand looked affectionately over his spectacles into Borís’ eyes.
__label__twp After he had gone Pierre continued pacing up and down the room for a long time, no longer piercing an imaginary foe with his imaginary sword, but smiling at the remembrance of that pleasant, intelligent, and resolute young man.
__label__twp As often happens in early youth, especially to one who leads a lonely life, he felt an unaccountable tenderness for this young man and made up his mind that they would be friends.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli saw the princess off.
__label__twp She held a handkerchief to her eyes and her face was tearful.
__label__twp “It is dreadful, dreadful!” she was saying, “but cost me what it may I shall do my duty.
__label__twp I will come and spend the night.
__label__twp He must not be left like this.
__label__twp Every moment is precious.
__label__twp I can’t think why his nieces put it off.
__label__twp Perhaps God will help me to find a way to prepare him!...
__label__twp Adieu, Prince! May God support you...”  “Adieu, ma bonne,” answered Prince Vasíli turning away from her.
__label__twp “Oh, he is in a dreadful state,” said the mother to her son when they were in the carriage.
__label__twp “He hardly recognizes anybody.”  “I don’t understand, Mamma—what is his attitude to Pierre?” asked the son.
__label__twp “The will will show that, my dear; our fate also depends on it.”  “But why do you expect that he will leave us anything?”  “Ah, my dear! He is so rich, and we are so poor!”  “Well, that is hardly a sufficient reason, Mamma...”  “Oh, Heaven! How ill he is!” exclaimed the mother.
__label__twp CHAPTER XVII  After Anna Mikháylovna had driven off with her son to visit Count Cyril Vladímirovich Bezúkhov, Countess Rostóva sat for a long time all alone applying her handkerchief to her eyes.
__label__twp At last she rang.
__label__twp “What is the matter with you, my dear?” she said crossly to the maid who kept her waiting some minutes.
__label__twp “Don’t you wish to serve me? Then I’ll find you another place.”  The countess was upset by her friend’s sorrow and humiliating poverty, and was therefore out of sorts, a state of mind which with her always found expression in calling her maid “my dear” and speaking to her with exaggerated politeness.
__label__twp “I am very sorry, ma’am,” answered the maid.
__label__twp “Ask the count to come to me.”  The count came waddling in to see his wife with a rather guilty look as usual.
__label__twp “Well, little countess? What a sauté of game au madère we are to have, my dear! I tasted it.
__label__twp The thousand rubles I paid for Tarás were not ill-spent.
__label__twp He is worth it!”  He sat down by his wife, his elbows on his knees and his hands ruffling his gray hair.
__label__twp “What are your commands, little countess?”  “You see, my dear...
__label__twp What’s that mess?” she said, pointing to his waistcoat.
__label__twp “It’s the sauté, most likely,” she added with a smile.
__label__twp “Well, you see, Count, I want some money.”  Her face became sad.
__label__twp “Oh, little countess!” ...
__label__twp and the count began bustling to get out his pocketbook.
__label__twp “I want a great deal, Count! I want five hundred rubles,” and taking out her cambric handkerchief she began wiping her husband’s waistcoat.
__label__twp “Yes, immediately, immediately! Hey, who’s there?” he called out in a tone only used by persons who are certain that those they call will rush to obey the summons.
__label__twp “Send Dmítri to me!”  Dmítri, a man of good family who had been brought up in the count’s house and now managed all his affairs, stepped softly into the room.
__label__twp “This is what I want, my dear fellow,” said the count to the deferential young man who had entered.
__label__twp “Bring me...” he reflected a moment, “yes, bring me seven hundred rubles, yes! But mind, don’t bring me such tattered and dirty notes as last time, but nice clean ones for the countess.”  “Yes, Dmítri, clean ones, please,” said the countess, sighing deeply.
__label__twp “When would you like them, your excellency?” asked Dmítri.
__label__twp “Allow me to inform you...
__label__twp But, don’t be uneasy,” he added, noticing that the count was beginning to breathe heavily and quickly which was always a sign of approaching anger.
__label__twp “I was forgetting...
__label__twp Do you wish it brought at once?”  “Yes, yes; just so! Bring it.
__label__twp Give it to the countess.”  “What a treasure that Dmítri is,” added the count with a smile when the young man had departed.
__label__twp “There is never any ‘impossible’ with him.
__label__twp That’s a thing I hate! Everything is possible.”  “Ah, money, Count, money! How much sorrow it causes in the world,” said the countess.
__label__twp “But I am in great need of this sum.”  “You, my little countess, are a notorious spendthrift,” said the count, and having kissed his wife’s hand he went back to his study.
__label__twp When Anna Mikháylovna returned from Count Bezúkhov’s the money, all in clean notes, was lying ready under a handkerchief on the countess’ little table, and Anna Mikháylovna noticed that something was agitating her.
__label__twp “Well, my dear?” asked the countess.
__label__twp “Oh, what a terrible state he is in! One would not know him, he is so ill! I was only there a few moments and hardly said a word...”  “Annette, for heaven’s sake don’t refuse me,” the countess began, with a blush that looked very strange on her thin, dignified, elderly face, and she took the money from under the handkerchief.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna instantly guessed her intention and stooped to be ready to embrace the countess at the appropriate moment.
__label__twp “This is for Borís from me, for his outfit.”  Anna Mikháylovna was already embracing her and weeping.
__label__twp The countess wept too.
__label__twp They wept because they were friends, and because they were kindhearted, and because they—friends from childhood—had to think about such a base thing as money, and because their youth was over....
__label__twp But those tears were pleasant to them both.
__label__twp CHAPTER XVIII  Countess Rostóva, with her daughters and a large number of guests, was already seated in the drawing room.
__label__twp The count took the gentlemen into his study and showed them his choice collection of Turkish pipes.
__label__twp From time to time he went out to ask: “Hasn’t she come yet?” They were expecting Márya Dmítrievna Akhrosímova, known in society as le terrible dragon, a lady distinguished not for wealth or rank, but for common sense and frank plainness of speech.
__label__twp Márya Dmítrievna was known to the Imperial family as well as to all Moscow and Petersburg, and both cities wondered at her, laughed privately at her rudenesses, and told good stories about her, while none the less all without exception respected and feared her.
__label__twp In the count’s room, which was full of tobacco smoke, they talked of the war that had been announced in a manifesto, and about the recruiting.
__label__twp None of them had yet seen the manifesto, but they all knew it had appeared.
__label__twp The count sat on the sofa between two guests who were smoking and talking.
__label__twp He neither smoked nor talked, but bending his head first to one side and then to the other watched the smokers with evident pleasure and listened to the conversation of his two neighbors, whom he egged on against each other.
__label__twp One of them was a sallow, clean-shaven civilian with a thin and wrinkled face, already growing old, though he was dressed like a most fashionable young man.
__label__twp He sat with his legs up on the sofa as if quite at home and, having stuck an amber mouthpiece far into his mouth, was inhaling the smoke spasmodically and screwing up his eyes.
__label__twp This was an old bachelor, Shinshín, a cousin of the countess’, a man with “a sharp tongue” as they said in Moscow society.
__label__twp He seemed to be condescending to his companion.
__label__twp The latter, a fresh, rosy officer of the Guards, irreproachably washed, brushed, and buttoned, held his pipe in the middle of his mouth and with red lips gently inhaled the smoke, letting it escape from his handsome mouth in rings.
__label__twp This was Lieutenant Berg, an officer in the Semënov regiment with whom Borís was to travel to join the army, and about whom Natásha had teased her elder sister Véra, speaking of Berg as her “intended.” The count sat between them and listened attentively.
__label__twp His favorite occupation when not playing boston, a card game he was very fond of, was that of listener, especially when he succeeded in setting two loquacious talkers at one another.
__label__twp “Well, then, old chap, mon très honorable Alphonse Kárlovich,” said Shinshín, laughing ironically and mixing the most ordinary Russian expressions with the choicest French phrases—which was a peculiarity of his speech.
__label__twp “Vous comptez vous faire des rentes sur l’état; * you want to make something out of your company?”       * You expect to make an income out of the government.
__label__twp “No, Peter Nikoláevich; I only want to show that in the cavalry the advantages are far less than in the infantry.
__label__twp Just consider my own position now, Peter Nikoláevich...”  Berg always spoke quietly, politely, and with great precision.
__label__twp His conversation always related entirely to himself; he would remain calm and silent when the talk related to any topic that had no direct bearing on himself.
__label__twp He could remain silent for hours without being at all put out of countenance himself or making others uncomfortable, but as soon as the conversation concerned himself he would begin to talk circumstantially and with evident satisfaction.
__label__twp “Consider my position, Peter Nikoláevich.
__label__twp Were I in the cavalry I should get not more than two hundred rubles every four months, even with the rank of lieutenant; but as it is I receive two hundred and thirty,” said he, looking at Shinshín and the count with a joyful, pleasant smile, as if it were obvious to him that his success must always be the chief desire of everyone else.
__label__twp “Besides that, Peter Nikoláevich, by exchanging into the Guards I shall be in a more prominent position,” continued Berg, “and vacancies occur much more frequently in the Foot Guards.
__label__twp Then just think what can be done with two hundred and thirty rubles! I even manage to put a little aside and to send something to my father,” he went on, emitting a smoke ring.
__label__twp “La balance y est...
__label__twp * A German knows how to skin a flint, as the proverb says,” remarked Shinshín, moving his pipe to the other side of his mouth and winking at the count.
__label__twp * So that squares matters.
__label__twp The count burst out laughing.
__label__twp The other guests seeing that Shinshín was talking came up to listen.
__label__twp Berg, oblivious of irony or indifference, continued to explain how by exchanging into the Guards he had already gained a step on his old comrades of the Cadet Corps; how in wartime the company commander might get killed and he, as senior in the company, might easily succeed to the post; how popular he was with everyone in the regiment, and how satisfied his father was with him.
__label__twp Berg evidently enjoyed narrating all this, and did not seem to suspect that others, too, might have their own interests.
__label__twp But all he said was so prettily sedate, and the naïveté of his youthful egotism was so obvious, that he disarmed his hearers.
__label__twp “Well, my boy, you’ll get along wherever you go—foot or horse—that I’ll warrant,” said Shinshín, patting him on the shoulder and taking his feet off the sofa.
__label__twp Berg smiled joyously.
__label__twp The count, followed by his guests, went into the drawing room.
__label__twp It was just the moment before a big dinner when the assembled guests, expecting the summons to zakúska, * avoid engaging in any long conversation but think it necessary to move about and talk, in order to show that they are not at all impatient for their food.
__label__twp The host and hostess look toward the door, and now and then glance at one another, and the visitors try to guess from these glances who, or what, they are waiting for—some important relation who has not yet arrived, or a dish that is not yet ready.
__label__twp * Hors d’oeuvres.
__label__twp Pierre had come just at dinnertime and was sitting awkwardly in the middle of the drawing room on the first chair he had come across, blocking the way for everyone.
__label__twp The countess tried to make him talk, but he went on naïvely looking around through his spectacles as if in search of somebody and answered all her questions in monosyllables.
__label__twp He was in the way and was the only one who did not notice the fact.
__label__twp Most of the guests, knowing of the affair with the bear, looked with curiosity at this big, stout, quiet man, wondering how such a clumsy, modest fellow could have played such a prank on a policeman.
__label__twp “You have only lately arrived?” the countess asked him.
__label__twp “Oui, madame,” replied he, looking around him.
__label__twp “You have not yet seen my husband?”  “Non, madame.” He smiled quite inappropriately.
__label__twp “You have been in Paris recently, I believe? I suppose it’s very interesting.”  “Very interesting.”  The countess exchanged glances with Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp The latter understood that she was being asked to entertain this young man, and sitting down beside him she began to speak about his father; but he answered her, as he had the countess, only in monosyllables.
__label__twp The other guests were all conversing with one another.
__label__twp “The Razumóvskis...
__label__twp It was charming...
__label__twp You are very kind...
__label__twp Countess Apráksina...” was heard on all sides.
__label__twp The countess rose and went into the ballroom.
__label__twp “Márya Dmítrievna?” came her voice from there.
__label__twp “Herself,” came the answer in a rough voice, and Márya Dmítrievna entered the room.
__label__twp All the unmarried ladies and even the married ones except the very oldest rose.
__label__twp Márya Dmítrievna paused at the door.
__label__twp Tall and stout, holding high her fifty-year-old head with its gray curls, she stood surveying the guests, and leisurely arranged her wide sleeves as if rolling them up.
__label__twp Márya Dmítrievna always spoke in Russian.
__label__twp “Health and happiness to her whose name day we are keeping and to her children,” she said, in her loud, full-toned voice which drowned all others.
__label__twp “Well, you old sinner,” she went on, turning to the count who was kissing her hand, “you’re feeling dull in Moscow, I daresay? Nowhere to hunt with your dogs? But what is to be done, old man? Just see how these nestlings are growing up,” and she pointed to the girls.
__label__twp “You must look for husbands for them whether you like it or not....”  “Well,” said she, “how’s my Cossack?” (Márya Dmítrievna always called Natásha a Cossack) and she stroked the child’s arm as she came up fearless and gay to kiss her hand.
__label__twp “I know she’s a scamp of a girl, but I like her.”  She took a pair of pear-shaped ruby earrings from her huge reticule and, having given them to the rosy Natásha, who beamed with the pleasure of her saint’s-day fete, turned away at once and addressed herself to Pierre.
__label__twp “Eh, eh, friend! Come here a bit,” said she, assuming a soft high tone of voice.
__label__twp “Come here, my friend...” and she ominously tucked up her sleeves still higher.
__label__twp Pierre approached, looking at her in a childlike way through his spectacles.
__label__twp “Come nearer, come nearer, friend! I used to be the only one to tell your father the truth when he was in favor, and in your case it’s my evident duty.” She paused.
__label__twp All were silent, expectant of what was to follow, for this was clearly only a prelude.
__label__twp “A fine lad! My word! A fine lad!...
__label__twp His father lies on his deathbed and he amuses himself setting a policeman astride a bear! For shame, sir, for shame! It would be better if you went to the war.”  She turned away and gave her hand to the count, who could hardly keep from laughing.
__label__twp “Well, I suppose it is time we were at table?” said Márya Dmítrievna.
__label__twp The count went in first with Márya Dmítrievna, the countess followed on the arm of a colonel of hussars, a man of importance to them because Nicholas was to go with him to the regiment; then came Anna Mikháylovna with Shinshín.
__label__twp Berg gave his arm to Véra.
__label__twp The smiling Julie Karágina went in with Nicholas.
__label__twp After them other couples followed, filling the whole dining hall, and last of all the children, tutors, and governesses followed singly.
__label__twp The footmen began moving about, chairs scraped, the band struck up in the gallery, and the guests settled down in their places.
__label__twp Then the strains of the count’s household band were replaced by the clatter of knives and forks, the voices of visitors, and the soft steps of the footmen.
__label__twp At one end of the table sat the countess with Márya Dmítrievna on her right and Anna Mikháylovna on her left, the other lady visitors were farther down.
__label__twp At the other end sat the count, with the hussar colonel on his left and Shinshín and the other male visitors on his right.
__label__twp Midway down the long table on one side sat the grown-up young people: Véra beside Berg, and Pierre beside Borís; and on the other side, the children, tutors, and governesses.
__label__twp From behind the crystal decanters and fruit vases, the count kept glancing at his wife and her tall cap with its light-blue ribbons, and busily filled his neighbors’ glasses, not neglecting his own.
__label__twp The countess in turn, without omitting her duties as hostess, threw significant glances from behind the pineapples at her husband whose face and bald head seemed by their redness to contrast more than usual with his gray hair.
